FOR OFFICIAL USE ONLY
B.   DEFINITIONS
Crude Birth Rate:   Number of live births per year per 1,000 of
population.
Crude Death Rate:   Number of deaths per year per 1,000 of population.
Rate of Natural
Increase:         Difference between crude birth and crude death rate;
usually expressed as a percentage.
Rate of Population
Growth:           Rate of natural increase adjusted for (net) migra-
tion, and expressed as a percentage of the total
population of a given year.
General Fertility
Rate:             Number of live births per year per 1,000 women, aged
15-49 years.
Age-Specific
Fertility Rates:  Number of live births to women in a given age group
per 1,000 women in the same age group, in a given
year. It is usually calculated for 5-year age
groups.
Total Fertility
Rate:             The average number of children that would be born
per woman if she were to live to the end of her
childbearing years, and bear children according
to a given set of age-specific fertility rates.
The Total Fertility Rate often serves as an esti-
mate of the average number of children per family.
Gross Reproduc-
tion Rate:        This is the same as the Total Fertility Rate, but
refers to the number of daughters a woman would
have under prevailing fertility patterns.
Net Reproduc-
tion Rate:        The number of daughters a woman would have under
prevailing fertility and mortality patterns, who
would survive to the mean age of childbearing.
Infant Mortality
Rate:             Annual number of deaths of infants under 1 year
per 1,000 live births during the same year.
This document has a restricted distribution and may be used by recipients only in the performance
of their official duties. Its contents may not otherwise be disclosed without World Bank authorization.



B.   DEFINITIONS  (Cont'd)
Maternal Mortality
Rate:              Number of maternal deaths per 1,000 births attri-
butable to pregnancy, childbirth, or puerperal
complications (i.e., within six weeks following
childbirth).
Life Expectancy:    Average number of years expected to be lived by
children born in the same year if mortality rates
for each age sex group remain the same in the
future.
Dependency Ratio:   Number of people of 14 years or under, plus people
65 years or over, divided by the population aged
15 to 64 years.

This report is based on the findings of the appraisal mission that visited
Thailand in November/December 1976 and post-appraisal missions in March and
October 1977. The appraisal mission was composed of Mr. P.A. Hall (Chief),
Dr. T.I. Kim (Health and Manpower), Mr. D.J. Radel (Information and Educa-
tion), Mr. G. Naleppa (Civil Works) and Mr. J.A. Ross (Consultant, Evalua-
tion and Research). Mr. G.B. Baldwin (Deputy Director) and Ms. J. Murphy
(Loan Officer) joined the mission in the field to explore possible donor
arrangements. This report was prepared by Mr. Hall with the assistance of
Mr. Radel and with the cooperation of Dr. Kim in particular sections.

THAILAND
APPRAISAL OF A POPULATION PROJECT
SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS
i.        This report appraises a population project estimated to cost
about US$68.6 million equivalent, with a foreign exchange component of
US$22.2 million or 32% of the total costs. The main objective of the
project is to assist the Royal Thai Government (RTG) in reducing the rate
of population growth from 2.5% per annum in FY 1977 to 2.1% in FY 1981.
A related objective is to assist the Government in reducing maternal and
infant mortality rates by about 20% by FY 1981. Such a reduction in in-
fant mortality would encourage parents to have fewer children. Both objec-
tives would be pursued through a substantial extension of the rural health
delivery system.
ii.       Firm action now will prevent the growth of population from becoming
a severe impediment to Thailand's economic and social progress. Thailand's
population, estimated at about 44 million in 1977, would reach about 108
million by the year 2000 if 1970 fertility were to remain constant. For-
tunately, fertility has begun to fall. But even if the target reduction of
fertility is achieved, the population will nearly double by the year 2000.
iii.      Thailand's population problem is characterized by:  (a) a high
dependency ratio, about 45% of the population being under 15 years of age;
(b) moderate but rising unemployment; (c) a high rate of migration from
the country to the towns, particularly Bangkok; and (d) mounting pressure
on arable land. These factors will make it difficult to raise the standard
of living unless the rate of growth of population, which has already fallen
from 3.0% in 1970 to 2.5% in 1977, continues to decline. The prospects for
a continued decline are good. Acceptance of family planning has increased
markedly and a wide variety of contraceptive methods are available within
the areas now reached by the health delivery system.
iv.       Since it was adopted in 1970, the Thai Government's National Family
Planning Program (NFPP) has successfully reached both its operational and
demographic targets in all respects. By the end of the First Five-Year Plan
period in FY 1976, over 30% of all eligible women of reproductive age were
practicing contraception, a rate which compares favorably to other developing
countries. Nevertheless, while success has been complete in terms of past
goals and objectives, there remains considerable unsatisfied demand for family
planning services. This demand cannot be met until steps are taken to extend
the health delivery system further into rural areas and to train more para-
medical and lay field personnel to provide family planning services. At
present the Thai family planning program is supply rather than demand con-
strained. If further progress is to be made, the network itself must be
expanded in terms of service points and personnel to staff them. Experience
to date in Thailand has shown that increasing the number of service points
leads to an immediate increase of acceptors.



- ii -
v.        The three-year project, 1978-80, is designed to:  (a) expand
the rural health infrastructure from about 5,400 service points in 1976
to about 7,000 in 1980; (b) increase the supply and the quality of para-
medicals (nurses, auxiliary midwives and practical nurse/midwives) and to
add, at the village level, new cadres of volunteer health and family plan-
ning worker; (c) stimulate demand for family planning services and encourage
broad community support for fertility reduction through an expanded infor-
mation, education and communicaLtion (IEC) program; and (d) improve evalua-
tion and research (E&R) capabilities.
vi.       The project includes over 100 distinct yet complementary activities
designed to broaden and deepen an already sound NFPP. The main assumption
underlying the project strategy is that significant numbers of new acceptors
can be recruited if family planning services are pushed "down and out", i.e.,
by pushing services "downwards" in the hierarchy of medical and paramedical
occupational groups and by pushing services "outward" from the static ser-
vice delivery points to points closer to the people concerned. Because 20
of the more densely populated of Thailand's 71 provinces lag behind the
others in terms of family planning acceptance, and health facility cover-
age, the project will finance additional rural health facilities, vehicles,
equipment, and staff to enable them to reach the national averages in these
respects by 1981. The components of the project therefore fall into two
categories: "national" components and "provincial" components. The latter
are geared to the needs of the 20 selected provinces, which are called accel-
erated family planning and health (AFPH) provinces.
vii.      The project components include at the national level:  (a) the
construction and equipping of: three practical nurse/midwifery schools
(including acquisition of land for one), one midwifery school with maternal
and child health (MCH) center, and one dormitory and staff housing for the
NFPP Training Center; and the expansion and equipping of: one nursing school,
two midwifery schools with MCHI centers, and two midwifery schools; (b) pro-
fessional services; (c) the purchase of about 90 vehicles and 1,550 motor-
cycles; (d) audiovisual equipment; (e) a health planner/economist and short-
term advisory assistance; (f) contraceptive supplies; (g) incremental salaries
and allowances, E&R studies, family planning and nutrition educational mate-
rials and activities, and the basic training of 270 assistant health workers
for MCH/family planning and in-service training of 600 nurse practitioners.
viii.     The project components at the provincial level include:  (a) the
construction and equipping of 225 midwifery centers, 150 health centers,
19 ten-bed district hospitals and 120 child nutrition centers (including
acquisition of land in some cases); (b) the upgrading and equipping of 30
midwifery centers to health centers and 6 ten-bed district hospitals to 30-bed
district hospitals; (c) professional services; (d) the purchase of about 125
vehicles and 2,550 motorcycles; (e) audiovisual equipment; (f) short-term
advisory assistance; and (g) incremental salaries and allowances, IEC, E&R,
and basic and in-service training activities.
ix.       As the program develops, new ideas for population activities that
are not now foreseen are likely to emerge. The project, therefore, includes
an allocation of US$1 million for "innovative activities."



- iii -
x.        The project will be administered by the Ministry of Public Health
(MOPH). The Deputy Undersecretary of State for Planning and Evaluation will
be designated as Project Director responsible for overall coordination and
implementation. Coordination will be provided by a Project Coordinating
Committee consisting of the heads of the eight MOPH divisions concerned
with the project.
xi.       The proposed project will be financed by loans or grants from IDA,
the United States, Canada, Australia and Norway totalling US$47.9 million
equivalent and by a RTG contribution of US$20.7 million equivalent. The
United States Agency for International Development (USAID) loan of US$5.5
million will be applied towards the short-term training and E&R activities of
the project, largely in the 20 AFPH provinces. The Canadian International
Development Agency (CIDA) loan and grant of US$6.3 million will be used to
finance the purchase of contraceptive supplies and the cost of the family
planning E&R studies, including some short-term advisory assistance. The
Association will help finance; the construction of the training institutions
and rural health facilities, procurement of furniture, equipment, vehicles,
materials, supplies and contraceptives, technical assistance, and innova-
tive activities. The IDA credit of US$33.1 million will finance US$14.8
million of foreign exchange costs or 67% of total foreign exchange costs.
The Norwegian grant of US$3.1 million will be included in the IDA Credit
of US$33.1 million and will be used to help finance the costs of the rural
health centers and the health planner/economist. The Australian Development
Assistance Bureau (ADAB) grant of US$3.0 million will be used to help finance
on a joint basis with the Association the other technical assistance, other
civil works, equipment, materials and vehicles. The RTG will finance the
balance of the project costs.
xii.      Each contract for civil works totalling more than US$750,000 equiva-
lent, each contract for equipment and supplies totalling more than US$100,000
equivalent, and contracts for vehicles will be awarded on the basis of inter-
national competitive bidding in accordance with Bank Group guidelines. In
evaluation of bids for goods, domestic manufacturers will be accorded a pref-
ercence of 15% of the c.i.f. price of competing imports or the custom duty,
if lower. Contracts for injectable contraceptives will be let on a negotiated
basis with manufacturers of the contraceptives. All contracts for civil works
totalling less than US$750,000 equivalent, contracts for equipment and sup-
plies totalling less than US$100,000 equivalent, and contracts for furniture
will be awarded in accordance with RTG procurement procedures, which have been
found satisfactory. However, to provide flexibility in the procurement of
smaller items, purchases of equipment, vehicles and supplies where the in-
dividual purchases total less than the equivalent of US$10,000 each may be
made on the basis of prudent shopping after soliciting at least three price
quotations up to an aggregate amount of US$100,000.



- iv -
xiii.     The project will assist the NFPP in reducing the crude birth rate
from the present 34 per 1,000 to 29 per 1,000 by FY 1981. If a net reproduc-
tion rate of 1.0 is reached in 1990, the total population of Thailand would
be 42 million less by the year 2000 than if fertility remained constant.
xiv.      The proposed project is suitable for an IDA credit of US$33.1 mil-
lion equivalent to the RTG on standard IDA terms. 1/
1/   The Kingdom of Norway intends to participate in the proposed credit by
making US$3.1 million available on a grant basis.



THAILAND
APPRAISAL OF A POPULATION PROJECT
I. INTRODUCTION
1.01      In November 1974, the Royal Thai Government (RTG) invited the IDA
to review Thailand's demographic situation and the National Family Plan-
ning Program (NFPP) with a view to assisting the Government in preparing
the Fourth Development Plan (FY 1977-81) and providing a basis for external
assistance for population activities during the Fourth Plan period. A Sector
Report issued in 1975 commented favorably on the NFPP but warned that the
program was constrained by a lack of service outlets, rather than a lack of
demand. The need to improve and extend the system for delivering family
planning services was identified as the number one priority. At the same
time, the report indicated that the long-run demand for family planning
services should be strengthened by improving and expanding family planning
information, education and communication (IEC) activities.
1.02      An IDA preparation mission visited Thailand in July 1976 to identify,
with senior Thai officials, possible components of an IDA-assisted project and
the activities required to prepare a project for financing. Based on these
discussions, the RTG identified the need: (a) to expand paramedical training
facilities for key nursing and auxiliary personnel primarily responsible for
providing family planning services; and (b) to extend the rural family plan-
ning/health services network in selected rural areas. A project proposal was
drawn up by the Ministry of Public Health (MOPH) and appraised by the Asso-
ciation in November/December 1976. During the appraisal and post-appraisal
missions the RTG requested the Association's help in mobilizing cofinancing
assistance to supplement an IDA Credit and in drawing up a proposed financ-
ing plan. Detailed Appraisal Mission Working Papers together with specific
financing proposals were produced and sent to the interested donors as a basis
for their deciding whether to participate in the financing of the project. 1/
This report describes and summarizes the components of a population project
recommended for financing by the Association and four cofinancing partners,
i.e., the Norwegian Government, the Australian Government (Australian Develop-
ment Assistance Bureau--ADAB), the Canadian Government (Canadian International
Development Agency--CIDA), and the United States Government (United States
Agency for International Development--USAID).
II. RECENT DEMOGRAPHIC TRENDS
2.01      The demographic history of Thailand over the past century, like that
of other developing countries, is one of accelerating population growth caused
primarily by a fall in mortality. In 1850, Thailand's population was probably
1/   The Appraisal Mission Working Papers will be updated periodically as a
basis for detailed planning for implementation.



- 2 -
about 6 million. By the time of the first census in 1911, it had grown to
no more than 8 million, much of the increase coming from Chinese immigration.
The crude death rate early in the century has been estimated at about 50 per
thousand. In the period between World Wars I and II, mortality fell markedly;
following the Second World War, a still more rapid fall took place, largely
as a result of public health measures. By 1970, the crude death rate had
fallen to about 10 per thousand, about the same as in Europe, and well below
that of most Southeast Asian countries. The 1970 crude birth rate was around
42 per thousand, with some estimates putting it over 45. The 1970 census
deterrmined that Thailand's population had reached around 36 million and was
growing rapidly. During the intercensal period 1960-70, the annual growth
rate was about 3%, one of the highest in the world and the third highest in
Asia. Thailand's population is estimated to have reached about 44 million
by early 1977.
2.02      As might be inferred from the high fertility, the relatively low
and declining mortality, and insignificant emigration, Thailand has a young
population. In 1970, slightly more than 45% of the population was below 15
years of age and only 3% was 65 or older.  If the working age population is
defined as those between 15 and 64, there were 93 dependents per 100 persons
of working age, compared with 85 per 100 in 1960.
2.03      The rapid growth of population has been accompanied by a movement
of population from the country to the towns.  The proportion of population
residing in urban areas has increased from 10% in 1947 to around 17% in 1976.
The Bangkok metropolitan area accounts for nearly one-tenth of the country's
population; the next largest city is about one-fortieth of its size. Over
80% of the population lives in rural areas. Although by Asian standards
Thailand is still relatively sparsely populated (about 80 persons per square
kilometer), the density per unit of cultivable land is much higher.
2.04      In spite of the high rate of population growth in recent years,
the demographic outlook is encouraging. This is because Thailand has suffi-
cient time to control its rapid population growth and because fertility has
already begun to fall, primarily because of the NFPP. The rate of growth has
dropped steadily from its peak of more than 3% in 1970 to about 2.5% in 1977.
By the end of 1976, over 30% of the women in the reproductive age group in
Thailand were practicing some form of contraception--one of the highest rates
in the developing world. This high rate of acceptance contributed to a reduc-
tion in the crude birth rate from 42 per thousand in 1970 to around 35 per
thousand in late 1976. Nevertheless, even if fertility falls drastically,
the present age structure will almost inevitably produce a population 80-100%
larger by the year 2000 than it was in 1970.
2.05      The need to reduce fertility further is best illustrated by compar-
ing population projections based on three different assumptions about fertil-
ity trends over the period 1970*-2000:
Projection 1: the total fertility rate remains constant;
Projection 2: the total fertility rate declines at a "moderate"
rate;



- 3 -
Projection 3: the total fertility rate declines sharply resulting
in a net reproduction rate of 1.0 by 1990.
PROJECTED POPULATION OF THAILAND (1970-2000)
(thousands)
Projections          1970     1980      1990      2000
1.   Constant fertility  36,181   50,955   73,595  107,971
2.   "Medium" decline    36,181   50,527   69,420   89,624
3.   "Thai target"        36,181   47,731   57,004   65,729
2.06      By the year 2000, the third projection (based on the Government's
fertility-reduction targets) results in a population some 40% smaller than
the constant fertility projection. Nevertheless, even under these favor-
able circumstances, the population would nearly double within this 30-year
period.
III. POPULATION POLICY AND PROGRAM ACTIVITIES
A. Present Policy
3.01      Thailand had a pronatalist population policy throughout the first
half of the present century. During World War II, the Government encour-
aged early marriages. A private family planning association was started in
the 1950s. Its main function was to provide family planning services. As
the Government became aware of the high rate of population growth, it grad-
ually altered its policy. In 1959, in part because of a comment by the
1959 World Bank economic mission on the implications of rapid population
growth for Thailand's economic development, the Prime Minister set up a
committee to review the problem and submit recommendations to the Cabinet.
Although the Government did not formally announce its support for family
planning until 1970, pilot family planning projects were begun as early as
1964, and the MOPH began to provide family planning services in 1968. A
National Population Policy Committee was established in 1970 and MOPH's
Family Health Project was renamed the National Family Planning Program
(NFPP). In 1974, a National Family Planning Coordinating Committee was
established. In addition to supervising the execution of the family plan-
ning program, the new Committee coordinates the population activities of



- 4 -
the various sectors and the external assistance for the program. This Com-
mittee is chaired by the Minister for Public Health and includes represen-
tatives from other ministries and agencies. A Committee on Population Policy
and Planning was created by the National Economic and Social Development Board
in 1974 to formulate national population policy. It assisted in preparing the
"Population, Manpower and Employment" chapter of the Fourth Development Plan,
FY 1977-81.
3.02      In 1970, the Cabinet made the MOPH responsible for carrying out the
newly approved population policy. The Under-Secretary of State for Health
was made the Director of the NF'PP, the administration of which was entrusted
to the Ministry's Family Health Division.
B. The National Family Planning Program
3.03      Thailand's NFPP has been successfully implemented to date.  The
First Five-Year Plan for the NFPP (FY 1972-76) established for the first
time a national demographic target (to reduce the estimated population growth
rate from over 3% to 2.5%) and facilitated the spread of family planning ser-
vices in a number of ways. Among these were: the utilization of paramedics
in supplying clinical contraceptives; the provision of motorcycles for mid-
wives; the introduction on a pilot basis of a new category of full-time, paid
field-workers; the expansion of in-service training for physicians, nurses and
auxiliary midwives; the accelerated development of special maternity hospitals
(called maternal and child health [MCH] centers) as institutions for family
planning and the provision of postpartum family planning services; the initia-
tion of evaluation and research (E&R) studies, including clinical trials
intended to extend the delivery network; and the establishment of a special
section in the Family Health Division for information, education and communica-
tion (IEC). By the end of the plan period, the program was well established
and had exceeded its acceptor targets. Specifically, it had: (a) recruited
2.5 million acceptors, an increase in the annual number of acceptors from
408,000 in 1971 to around 593,000 in 1976; (b) increased the number of MOPH
clinic-based family planning service delivery points from 270 in 1970 to 5,400
in 1976; (c) given orientation or skills training to about 20,000 paramedical
and medical personnel; (d) improved the data collection system; and (e)
undertaken, on a small scale, IEC activities in support of family planning.
This substantial increase in family planning activities is believed to be the
primary reason for the First Five-Year Family Planning Plan's achieving its
demographic target. 1/
1/   Results of the 1975 World Fertility Survey confirmed the belief of lead-
ing Thai demographers that the rate of population growth had fallen to
around 2.5% by 1977.



- 5 -
3.04      Thailand's NFPP is based on a high degree of integration of family
planning with basic health services. The MOPH network of general health
facilities is by far the most important source of family planning services.
During the past ten years, MOPH facilities have accounted for over 80% of
the family planning acceptors recruited through non-commercial sources.
Thailand has progressively liberalized the regulations defining the ser-
vices which particular categories of paramedical personnel are allowed to
perform. In 1970, for example, auxiliary midwives were permitted to pre-
scribe the oral contraceptive after administering a simple checklist of
contraindications; as a result, the number of service points for oral con-
traceptives increased from 270 to nearly 3,800; and the number of oral con-
traceptives acceptors increased from 132,000 in 1970 to 295,000 in 1971.
Since then, properly trained lay personnel have been authorized to distrib-
ute oral contraceptives on a resupply basis; the insertion of IUDs by auxil-
iary midwives is being tested on a pilot basis; nurses have been permitted
to give injectable contraceptives; and doctors have been trained to perform
sterilizations. The service program that now exists (including the contribu-
tion of the commercial sector and the small but growing number of private
networks) relies 60-65% on oral contraceptives, 20% on IUDs, and 15-20% on
sterilizations (mostly female). The level of use of injectable contracep-
tives is expected to increase significantly as soon as adequate supplies are
available (Annex 1).
3.05      Until recently, the direct costs of the NFPP were financed mostly
by external agencies--USAID, the United Nations Fund for Population Activ-
ities (UNFPA), the Population Council, the International Planned Parenthood
Federation, and others. Between 1969 and 1975 these agencies provided funds
for more than 90% of direct program costs. The RTG began making budgetary
appropriations for the NFPP in 1972, when US$0.5 million was provided. This
appropriation began to increase sharply in FY 1975; by FY 1977 it had risen
to US$2.5 million and over US$3 million is expected in FY 1978. The RTG share
of total NFPP funding is expected to reach about 80% by FY 1981; this sharp
change reflects an expected reduction of external assistance and continuing
program expansion. The indirect costs of the NFPP, such as the costs of
building, maintaining, and staffing the health delivery network through
which the Ministry's family planning services are provided, are carried in
other parts of the MOPH budget, almost all of which is funded by RTG without
external assistance. The project will provide external financial assistance
to supplement the RTG budget for both direct and indirect program costs.
C. The Rural Health Delivery Network
3.06      Thailand's health delivery system includes not only the MOPH
network, the largest and fastest growing provider of services, but also
a number of other public and private networks that serve both general and
specialized needs. The non-MOPH components include the hospitals and
and clinics operated by other Government agencies and a relatively small
number of privately run hospitals and clinics, including those operated by



-6-
foreign church missions and by private voluntary organizations. 1/ There
are in addition many unlicensed physicians who practice extensively in the
rural areas; numerous commercial pharmacies, although limited to towns; and,
in the family planning field, a few privately financed providers--e.g., the
McCormick Hospital Family Planning Program in Chiengmai Province and the
rapidly growing Community-Based Family Planning Services program. Although
the NFPP is making increasing use of some of these non-MOPH networks, the
MOPH system has recruited over three-quarters of all family planning accep-
tors, and its dominant position1 in the field of family planning service
providers, particularly in the rural areas, is expected to continue.
3.07      The MOPH has developed a standardized pattern for the delivery of
rural health services in which health care is provided through a hierarchy
of facilities. Although the Government intends to establish this pattern
nationally, some of Thailand's 71 provinces are much better served than
others. Each level of health care has been organized administratively to
provide a distinct set of services to a defined population group. The key
institution providing specialized medical care in each province is the pro-
vincial hospital, commonly located in tAe provincial capital. Each province
typically has about nine districts (amphoes); each district with 30,000 or
more population is intended to have a 10 or 30-bed district hospital. 2/
Districts are typically divided into about 10 units called tambols; each
tambol with 5,000 or more population is intended to have a health center
staffed by two paramedicals. At the lowest level, each village (muban) or
cluster of villages with a population of 2,000 is to have a midwifery center.
All of these facilities except the provincial hospital are considered part
of the rural health network; they are administered by the provincial health
office in the provincial capital. The district health office is responsible
for health services in the district, except for district hospitals, which
report directly to the provincial chief medical officer.
3.08      The table on the following page shows:  (a) the present number of
district hospitals, health centers, and midwifery centers in the country;
(b) the numbers required to meet MOPH standards; (c) the number which is
proposed to be in existence by the end of the Fourth Plan; (d) the number
of each type to be built during the Plan; and (e) the number to be built as
1/   In 1973, out of a total of about 45,000 hospital beds in Thailand, 26%
were run by non-MOPH government agencies and 7% by private agencies.
2/   Until recently, district hospitals were called "first-class health
centers" and what are now called health centers were known as "second-
class health centers". The use of the term "district hospital" for
the 10-bed facility differs from the use of the term in many other
countries, as the facilities are not hospitals in the normal sense
of that term. The two larger types of district hospitals now being
planned (with 30 and 60 beds), however, are similar to conventional
hospitals.

3.09      To provide family planning services and primary health care to
under-served villages, the MOPH plans to select and train village health
volunteers and village health communicators on a large scale. The intro-
duction of this new level of health worker has already been successfully
tried on a pilot basis in several areas. The village volunteers and com-
municators will not only contribute directly to health improvements and
the spread of family planning services but will also help to improve the
utilization of the Ministry's existing rural health facilities by refer-
ring villagers to them. By the end of the Fourth Plan period, it is pro-
jected that about one-half of the villages in Thailand will be covered by
these workers.
D. Main Needs for Strengthening the NFPP
3.10      The NFPP is presently characterized as supply, rather than
demand constrained. There is an existing unmet need for family planning
services, particularly among the rural population. The health delivery



-8-
system reaches only one half of the rural population, so that for the next
few years new acceptors can be recruited by increasing the availability of
family planning services, particularly in the rural areas. There is also
a need to use the IEC program to reinforce current practice in order to
increase continuation rates. At the same time the long-run demand for fam-
ily planning services should be increased by improving and extending IEC
activities. E&R activities also need to be improved and expanded.
3.11      The Family Health Division has drafted a Second Five-Year Plan
for the NFPP (FY 1977-81), which has been incorporated in the RTG's Fourth
National Development Plan. The NFPP Plan calls for a sizeable expansion of
the existing program in an effort to reduce the rate of population growth
from the present 2.5% per annum to 2.1% per annum by the end of the plan
period. 1/ To achieve this target the Plan calls for: (a) the extension
and improvement of family planniing services; (b) the basic and in-service
training of public health personnel who will be providing family planning
services; (c) the expansion and improvement of IEC activities; and (d) the
expansion of E&R activities. Ihe NFPP's basic strategy is to extend the
more effective family planning services to lower points in the hierarchy of
health delivery facilities and occupations, and to more geographical areas.
Doctors will perform sterilizations, and nurses will provide injectable con-
traceptives at district hospitals; mobile teams of doctors and nurses will
perform vasectomies at points where doctors are not regularly available;
auxiliary midwives are expected to be authorized to provide injectable
contraceptives and to insert IUDs at health centers and midwifery centers;
and village health volunteers will provide condoms and resupply pills.
3.12      An increase in the supply of family planning services depends on
an expanding health delivery system which is fully staffed, properly trained,
and adequately supervised. With advice from the World Health Organization
(WHO), the MOPE has prepared a Five-Year Health Development Program to extend
rural health and family planning services to 50% of the rural villages by
1981. The Program calls for the establishment of new well-located rural
health facilities; the rationalization of the use of existing facilities;
the improvement of supervision and staffing; the training of supervisory and
peripheral health workers; the provision of adequate supplies and medical
equipment; the fuller utilization of existing health personnel outside the
MOPH; and the selection and training of volunteers at the village level to
provide information, simple medical care, and contraceptives.
1/ This represents a decline in the crude birth rate from an estimated
34.5 per 1,000 at the end of 1976 to 29.0 per 1,000 by the end of
1981. The crude death rate is expected to decline by 0.3 per year
and thus be equal to 8.0 per 1,000 by 1981. Over the five-year period
an estimated 2.6 million births would need to be prevented to reduce
the crude birth rate to the desired level.



- 9 -
IV. THE PROJECT
A. Project Objectives and Strategy
4.01      The primary objective of the project is to enable the RTG to reach
its demographic target for the Second Five-Year Plan period (FY 1977-81) by
assisting in the recruitment of some three million additional acceptors, thus
adding 1.6 million new continuing users by the end of FY 1981. A related
objective will be to reduce both maternal and infant mortality rates by about
20%. 1/ Since there appears to be a long-run relationship between low infant
and child mortality and low fertility, it can be expected that, after some
time lag, these improvements will influence couples to have fewer children
(Annex 1). The project will also create the foundations for further reduc-
tions in fertility during the future.
4.02      The project will support the strengthening and extension of family
planning services throughout Thailand by: (a) increasing the supply of
nurses, auxiliary midwives, and practical nurses/midwives, all of whom are
key personnel in providing family planning services; (b) intensifying the
NFPP throughout Thailand with special attention to certain provinces; and
(c) increasing the demand for family planning services through an expanded
lEC program, particularly in the rural areas. The project will extend the
rural health network so as to push family planning services "down and out" in
the health delivery system, i.e., services will be increasingly performed by
lower levels of health workers in areas that are not now adequately served.
More specialized family planning services will be provided by mobile teams.
This strategy should significantly increase the number of new acceptors by
bringing family planning services closer to the rural poor who now lack
ready access to service centers.
4.03      The project will contribute to an increase in the number of clinic-
based family planning service delivery points from 5,400 in 1976 to 7,000
in 1980. In addition, it will enable the MOPH to extend its new community-
based primary health care program to around 90% of the villages in 20 selected
provinces with village health volunteers providing oral contraceptives on a
resupply basis and also distributing condoms. Further, as a result of the
project, the shortage of paramedical staff responsible for providing family
planning services will be substantially reduced, in-service training will be
extended to new groups, adequate supplies of contraceptives will be assured,
and more and better IEC materials for family planning will be produced.
The annual output of nurses, practical nurse/midwives, and auxiliary midwives
will increase by 100, 450 and 350, respectively. Manpower balances for key
health occupations, 1976-81, are shown in Annex 3. A specific program of
1/   This is an objective of the Fourth Five-Year Health Development Plan.
The RTG estimates that in 1970 the maternal mortality rate per 1,000
live births was 4 and the infant mortality rate was 80 per thousand
live births. By 1975 the infant mortality rate declined to 68 per
thousand.



- 10 -
family planning services has been drawn up emphasizing the more effective
methods of contraception (i.e., tubectomies, vasectomies, injectables, and
the IUD) (Annex 2). A short-term training program has been designed for new
lower level staff and volunteers to prepare them for their assignments in the
family planning and basic health care programs (Annex 4).
B. Project Components
4.04      The proposed three-year project consists of two sets of compo-
nents--national and provincial. 1/ The national components are primarily
designed to help the MOPH to train the additional paramedical staff called
for by the 1978-80 expansion of the family planning service network and to
ensure the availability throughout Thailand of contraceptive supplies and of
mobile sterilization and other family planning and related health services.
Other national components include the expansion of family planning IEC and
E&R activities, and strengthening the headquarters staff of the NFPP.
4.05      The provincial components are designed to accelerate the expansion
of family planning and rural health services in 20 selected provinces which
are characterized by low-to-moderate family planning acceptance, high popu-
lation densities, and poor health facility coverage and therefore require
special attention. These provinces will hereafter be referred to as the
accelerated family planning and health (AFPH) provinces (see Map IBRD-12792R).
They are geographically distributed in four regions of the country and in-
clude 40% of Thailand's total rural population. The project components
in the 20 AFPH provinces (rural health facilities, vehicles, audiovisual
equipment and incremental operating costs) directly support the activities
required to reach the RTG's national fertility and health objectives through
the accelerated extension of family planning and rural health services so
that by 1980 an improved health delivery system will be in place--one that is
well-designed, adequately staffed and supplied, and effectively managed. The
accelerated expansion of the program in these provinces should yield above-
average gains in family planning acceptance and MICH care. Project-supported
activities in the AFPH provinces differ from ongoing activities in the other
51 provinces in that they represent an accelerated pace of the implementation
of family planning and rural health activities and/or will be used to pioneer
improvements in the rural health services. Consequently, in certain respects
(e.g., population coverage by rural health facilities) the 20 AFPH provinces
will reach or slightly exceed national averages by the end of the three-year
project period, and in other respects (e.g., the appointment of provincial
health educators) the provinces will lead the way in introducing improvements
in the family planning and rural health services.
1/   Provincial components refer to those components of the project compris-
ing the activities to be undertaken under the project within and for the
benefit of 20 provinces receiving intensive inputs under the project.
National components refer to those components, other than the provincial
components, comprising the activities to be undertaken under the project
for the general benefit of the RTG's NFPP and related health activities.



- 11 -
4.06      The public's willingness to make use of rural health facilities
depends primarily on: (a) the convenient location of clinics; (b) adequate
staffing; (c) public confidence that the needed medical care (services and
medicines) will be readily available; and (d) good supervision of clinical
staff. The project includes special provision to ensure that these criteria
will be met in the 20 AFPH provinces. Regarding (a), the RTG agreed to:
(1) take all action necessary to acquire all land and rights to land required
for the construction and operation of the facilities included in the project
and furnish to the Association evidence that such land and rights in respect
of land are available for project purposes; and (ii) ensure that all facilities
to be constructed under the project are located on land selected in accordance
with agreed criteria (para 5.13 (b)). Regarding (b), during negotiations the
RTG agreed, except as it and the Association may otherwise agree, that the
existing and proposed facilities in the 20 AFPH provinces will be staffed not
later than May 31, 1981, according to the agreed staffing pattern (Annex 2,
Appendix 14). Regarding (c), the RTG is financing a study of the system for
procuring and distributing drugs and medical supplies to be carried out by
the National Institute of Development Administration. The results of the
study are expected to be available about October 1978 and will provide MOPH
officials with alternative proposals for improving the existing system.
During negotiations, the RTG agreed: (i) not later than December 31, 1978,
to submit the results of the study to the Association for review and comment;
and (ii) to introduce such modifications in the study as the Association
reasonably requests and, thereafter, take all reasonable measures necessary
to implement the study. While the costs of drugs, medical supplies, fuel and
vehicle maintenance have not been included in the project, the RTG has agreed
to take all measures necessary to ensure that the MOPH's facilities in the
AFPH provinces shall at all times have adequate supplies of fuel and spare
parts for vehicle operation and maintenance and of drugs and other medical
supplies to ensure the timely provision of these services. Regarding (d), the
supervision of clinic staff will be strengthened at the district, provincial
and national levels.

C. Description of Project Components
4.08      Project activities encompass five administrative levels (village,
tambol, district, provincial and national) and are grouped into five func-
tional categories (services, training, IEC, evaluation and research, and
management and administration). Annexes 2-7 provide detailed descriptions
of how the various project inputs, grouped according to particular func-
tional areas, will strengthen the existing system. 1/ Annex 8 provides a
project activity matrix which shows the relationship between project inputs
and each of the five functional activities.
1/   Six annexes are devoted to the five functional areas since two deal
with training: one with basic training institutions and the other
with short-term training programs.



- 15 -
4.09      The highest concentration of project inputs is at the rural
health facility level, supplemented by community-based outreach activities.
Rural health facilities are differentiated into four basic types, the char-
acteristics and functions of which are summarized in the table on the next
page. The nature of higher and lower institutions to which they are linked
and the staffing and manpower requirements serving the local population are
also depicted. A referral system is institutionalized in which more spe-
cialized medical care as well as technical supervision of staff and/or
volunteers working at lower levels in the system are provided by higher
level institutions, and lower level institutions refer clients to higher
institutions for those family planning and health services which are tech-
nically beyond their level (Annex 2).
Inter-Relationships Among Project Components in the 20 AFPH Provinces
4.10      Whereas Annexes 2-7 describe the project inputs according to their
function, the following paragraphs show how the functional components are
related to each other at the village, tambol, district and provincial levels
in the 20 AFPH provinces.
4.11      At the village level the project will support the training of
sufficient village health volunteers and health communicators to reach
directly nearly 90% of the village population in the 20 selected provinces.
To complement this training, the MOPH will be responsible for ensuring that
both groups (together with traditional midwives and tambol doctors) are pro-
vided with the necessary IEC materials (largely leaflets plus a special moti-
vation kit for use by village health volunteers). At the time of training,
each village health volunteer will receive a first-aid kit provided through
the project. Condoms and oral contraceptives (including those required for
resupply by the village health volunteers, traditional midwives, and tambol
doctors) also will be provided. These village workers' activities will be
supervised by the staff of the tambol health and midwifery centers.
4.12      At the tambol level the project will finance the construction
or upgrading of midwifery, health and child nutrition centers and provide
salary support for the standard paramedical staff (auxiliary midwives and
junior sanitarians) of the new health and midwifery centers. A practical
nurse/midwife or auxiliary will be added to the staff of each new or upgraded
health center; and an assistant health worker, to the staff of each midwifery
center, thus releasing the auxiliary midwives and junior sanitarians at these
centers to undertake more field work and enabling the centers to remain open
for longer hours. The project will provide training and motorcycles for
junior sanitarians and auxiliary midwives to enable them to recruit, train,
and supervise the village health volunteers and health communicators. Auxil-
iary midwives in tambol health facilities will receive in-service training in
basic medical care to enable them to provide improved curative services. The
project will also support the training of specialized attendants for the child
nutrition centers. The child nutrition centers serve to increase community

vide day-care services and some supplemental high protein foods for children;
and (b) serve as a center for mothers' classes in nutrition, family planning
and child care. Normally, fees are collected from parents to supplement
the modest stipend the child nutrition center attendant receives from the
Government.
4.13      At the district level project inputs are designed to strengthen
the role of the district hospital in providing technical backing for the
rural health and midwifery centers and the district health office which
provides technical and administrative support for preventive and promotive
health activities at the community level. The 10 and 30-bed hospitals will
assume responsibility for certain specialized services (e.g., sterilizations)
that were formerly available in provincial hospitals only. The project will
provide salary support for those auxiliary personnel who deliver family plan-
ning services (e.g., auxiliary midwives, junior sanitarians, and practical
nurse/midwives). The project will also provide these auxiliary midwives and
junior sanitarians with motorcycles. Each district hospital will be provided
with an assistant health worker for family planning duties. The project will
also provide district hospitals with oral and injectable contraceptives and
with various family planning IEC materials, including audiovisual aids to be
used for post-partum education and for public education in the waiting areas.
Nurse practitioners, who will be trained through the project, will be assigned
to districts with priority given to those with no physician present where they
will provide much needed curative care and technical supervision. To ensure
that these new resources are used effectively and to strengthen the role of
the district hospitals in providing technical support for lower levels, the
directors of all district hospitals in the 20 AFPH provinces, will partici-
pate in a short-term management training course during the project period.
4.14      A new post of deputy district health officer will be established
in each of the 199 districts (and subdistricts) in the 20 AFPH provinces
to provide supervision for primary health care activities. These deputy
district health officers will be trained, have their salaries provided, and
be given transportation (motorcycles) through the project. They will also
be responsible for epidemiological surveillance and take part in evaluation
and operational research. Two training programs will be provided to prepare
professional staff of the district health offices for their roles in the pri-
mary health care and family planning program: one for the district health
officers in techniques for training tambol health staff and the other for
the deputy district health officers in the organization and supervision of
the entire primary health care program. To familiarize themselves with
problems of provincial health planning and evaluation, the district health
officers will participate in a management course.
4.15      At the provincial level the project will provide the 23 provincial
hospitals 1/ with relatively small inputs designed mainly to strengthen their
1/   Three of the 20 AFPH provinces, because of their size, have two provin-
cial hospitals rather than one, hence 23.



- 18 -
family planning role. For this purpose each provincial hospital will add two
assistant health workers to its staff and will receive injectable and oral
contraceptives, audiovisual equipment and aids (films, slides, and prerecorded
audio cassettes), and simple IEC materials for distribution. Besides basic
training for the 46 assistant health workers, the project will also enable the
23 hospital directors to take a short-term course in management. This train-
ing will stress the role of the provincial hospital in providing technical
support for rural health facilities.
4.16      The project will considerably expand the capacity of the 20 provin-
cial health offices serving the AFPH provinces to plan, evaluate, and super-
vise the expansion and extension of rural health services "down and out". To
increase the capacity of provincial health offices to plan and direct health
services, short-term management training will be given to the provincial chief
medical officers and the chiefs of the following sections in their offices:
Health Technical Services; Planning and Evaluation; Health Education, Train-
ing, and Supportive Service; and Health Promotion.  In addition, the project
will provide salaries for the three key officers in each of the newly estab-
lished Planning and Evaluation Sections: the chief, an epidemiologist, and a
statistician.  The project will support E&R studies which will provide infor-
mation for use in health planning and management. These can be grouped into
three distinct types of studies: periodic and baseline surveys; operational
studies designed to assess the adequacy of training, referral processes, super-
visory arrangements, etc.; and external evaluation of the project's impact in
the 20 AFPH provinces to be carried out by Thai research institutes. Short-
term advisory assistance is provided to assist the Health Planning Division
and user divisions in designing the studies. Each provincial health office
will be provided with a vehicle to be used primarily by the provincial pub-
lic health nurse who has major responsibility for supervising the family plan-
ning activities carried out by paramedical personnel in the 20 AFPH provinces.
The project will provide each provincial health office with a van for mobile
sterilization teams, which can also provide medical care in remote areas.
Each team will include a physician. In addition, the project will provide
pre-service training and salary support for 20 provincial supervisors who will
supervise the implementation of the community-based primary health care and
family planning activities of the village health volunteers and communicators.
They will also be provided with transport. The project will provide salary
support and vehicles for 20 provincial health educators who, together with
other staff in provincial health offices engaged in training, will have access
to audiovisual support equipment, including the mobile equipment needed to
show films and slides to rural audiences. During negotiations agreement was
reached with the RTG that, except as the RTG and the Association may otherwise
agree, the Civil Service Commission will establish and fill the new posts
required at the provincial and district levels in a timely manner and that
the Budget Bureau will provide appropriate budgetary allocations for suitably
qualified persons.
Inter-relationships Among National-Level Components
4.17      The national level project components are designed to:  (a) in-
crease the output and improve the training of the paramedical workers who



- 19 -
provide family planning services; (b) ensure that the MOPH headquarters has
sufficient staff, technical assistance, special equipment, contraceptive
supplies, and IEC materials to direct and implement the NFPP; and (c) sup-
port the full extension of family planning services outside the 20 AFPH
provinces.
4.18      There are shortages in the three categories of paramedical per-
sonnel most important for family planning and MCH services: nurses, prac-
tical nurse/midwives, and auxiliary midwives. Doctors are also in short
supply, especially for rural service, and steps are being taken to devolve
some of their functions on a new category, the nurse practitioner (Annex 3).
The project includes the construction of and equipment for new training
institutions for paramedics required during the Fourth Plan period as well
as the expansion of four midwifery schools, two of which have associated
MCH centers. The MCH centers, of which the country now has four, are free-
standing maternity hospitals that have proven very effective in recruiting
family planning acceptors and providing a wide range of clinical training
in family planning and MCH. During negotiations the RTG agreed to:
(a) not later than August 31, 1978 prepare and submit to the Association
for its comments a detailed staffing plan for teaching positions and a re-
vised 18-month curriculum designed to ensure that (i) all training institu-
tions under the project are, at all times, adequately staffed and effec-
tively utilized, and (ii) sufficient time and attention are devoted to
family planning and medical care in the revised curriculum; and (b) intro-
duce such modifications in the detailed staffing plan and revised curri-
culum as the Association shall reasonably request and, thereafter, take
all measures necessary promptly to adopt and implement the same. To help
assure that paramedics already in the field are adequately trained in fam-
ily planning, the project also includes the construction of dormitory space
at the NFPP Training Center for 100 trainees, as well as staff housing. To
transport nursing trainees, tutors and short-term family planning trainees,
16 micro-buses and two larger buses are required. The Government will fi-
nance under the project the purchase of land needed for one nurse/midwifery
school.
4.19      The project includes financial support for:  (a) salaries and
allowances at MOPH headquarters for 84 positions to strengthen the operational
divisions which will carry out the project (Family Health, Rural Health, Con-
struction and Design, Health Planning and Health Education) and for six staff
to be assigned to the Project Administration and Financing Unit; (b) technical
assistance from a health planner/economist and short-term advisory assistance
in the design of family planning/population studies to be carried out by the
NFPP and Thai population institutions; (c) professional services to assist the
Design and Construction Division in the construction program; (d) audiovisual
and production equipment, including basic printing and filmmaking equipment
for the Health Education Division; (e) vehicles to enable MOPH staff to carry
out their project responsibilities; (f) contraceptive supplies to supplement



- 20 -
those provided by USAID and UNFPA; 1/ (g) the stipends and other basic train-
ing costs of 269 assistant health workers for family planning and in-service
training of 600 nurse practitioners (Annex 4); (h) recurrent cost support for
the expanded IEC production program (Annex 5); (i) the strengthening of the
family planning service statistics system and the costs of about 10 E&R
studies, five to be conducted by the Family Health Division and five by local
population institutions (Annex 6); and (j) recurrent cost support for the
Population Awareness Program carried out by Chulalongkorn University (Annexes
4 and 5). Regarding (a), except as the RTG and the Association may otherwise
agree, the RTG agreed during negotiations that all of the 84 positions re-
quired at the national level will be established on a permanent basis by the
Civil Service Commission and that the Budget Bureau will provide appropriate
budgetary allocations for suitably qualified persons to be appointed to the
above-mentioned Divisions. Regarding (b) and (c), during negotiations the
RTG agreed to: (i) employ a health planner/economist whose qualifications,
experience and terms and condil:ions of employment shall be satisfactory to the
Association; and (ii) in carrying out the project, employ suitably qualified
consultants and experts whose qualifications and experience are satisfactory
to the Association. The RTG also agreed that the health planner/economist
and other consultants and experts will be provided for the duration of their
employment with suitable facilities, adequate counterpart staff and other
appropriate support as necessary for the efficient discharge of their activi-
ties under the project. Regarding (d), a much increased IEC production
program, involving a variety of printed and audio materials on family planning
and nutrition and expanded use of mass media (radio and cinema) to increase
community understanding of family planning will be carried out (Annex 5).
During negotiations it was agreed that by October 1, 1978 the RTG will take
all measures necessary to ensure that:  (i) the MOPH's Family Health Division
is authorized to purchase, produce and duplicate color films, including
entertainment-type films, designed to increase community interest in and
understanding of family planning; and (ii) adequate radio time on sufficient
stations throughout the country is allocated or purchased at appropriate times
each day for broadcasts on family planning by the Family Health Division, all
on the basis of criteria acceptable to the RTG and the Association.
4.20      Regarding (e) and (f), the project will provide the vehicles and
contraceptive supplies to ensure the ready availability of family planning
services at all existing and projected service delivery points in the 51
provinces outside the 20 AFPH provinces. Although UNFPA provided over 4,000
motorcycles in the past, 20% of the auxiliary midwives in the field still
1/   Faced with declining grant support and increased demand for contracep-
tives, the RTG has requested financial assistance for procuring injec-
tables over the three-year project period and support for oral contra-
ceptives in 1979 and 1980. The injectable contraceptives have been well
researched over a ten-year period in Thailand and found to be highly
acceptable, clinically sound, and compatible with the existing contra-
ceptive service delivery system.



- 21 -
require motorcycles, as will all new recruits during the three years of the
project. The MOPH is also establishing mobile teams in all 71 provinces,
each team to spend about two days a week performing male sterilizations in
rural areas where doctors are not available. Thirty-one of these vans will
be purchased through the project. In addition, two-wheel drive vehicles will
be provided for use in the supervision of family planning work, one for each
of the 51 non-AFPH provinces.  Regarding (j), during negotiations the RTG
agreed that adequate and suitably qualified staff will be made available to
Chulalongkorn University to carry out the Population Awareness Program.
Allocation for Innovative Activities
4.21      An amount of US$1 million is earmarked for funding innovative pro-
posals that may be made during the course of project implementation. Such a
provision is intended to add flexibility to the project and to encourage the
authorities to seek innovations. During negotiations the Government agreed
to obtain the Association's approval of proposals prior to using this source
of funds.
V. PROJECT COSTS, FINANCING, AND ADMINISTRATIVE ARRANGEMENTS
A. Project Costs
5.01      The total estimated project cost is US$68.6 million equivalent,
of which US$22.2 million or 32% is foreign exchange. The foreign exchange
component has been calculated assuming 24% for construction, 30% for fur-
niture, 60% for equipment, 70% for vehicles, 75% for technical assistance,
and 100% for special equipment and contraceptive supplies. Project cost
estimates are given in Annex 9 and summarized in the table on page 23.
5.02      Cost estimates for construction, furniture, and equipment are
based on the experience of the MOPH with similar buildings and take into
account recent cost trends. Allowances have been included for provincial
differences in construction costs. Site development costs, including land
purchase costs, have been included where required. Cost estimates for vehi-
cles and special equipment are based on recent price quotations. Incremental
operating costs, including salaries and allowances for key family planning
personnel, short-term training activities, IEC activities, and E&R activities
are included in project costs. Salary and allowance estimates include allow-
ances for housing, per diem and travel. Training costs include per diem,
travel, training materials, and, in some cases, honoraria for instructors.
The costs of surveys, operational research and seminars are calculated on
the basis of standard local travel and per diem allowances. The contin-
gency allowances of US$13.4 million include: (a) physical contingencies
for unforeseen factors estimated at 10% of base civil works costs; and (b)
price contingencies averaging 18% of base costs and physical contingencies.

B. Financing of the Project
5.04      The proposed three year project, which forms an integral part of
the Five-Year NFPP and the Health Development Program, will be financed by
(a) an IDA Credit of $33.1 million equivalent including a US$3.1 million
equivalent grant from the Norwegian Government; (b) loans or grants from
ADAB, CIDA and USAID totalling US$14.8 million equivalent; and (c) an RTG
contribution of US$20.7 million equivalent. The Norway grant of US$3.1
million equivalent and the ADAB grant of US$3.0 million equivalent will
be financed on a joint basis with the Association, with Norway and ADAB
financing identifiable components in conjunction with the Association.
Specifically, Norway will finance around 50% of the costs of the rural
health centers to be constructed and the full cost of the health planner/
economist. The ADAB funds will be disbursed against initial expenditures
for: (a) the construction of training institutions and rural health facili-
ties (district hospitals and midwifery centers); (b) vehicles, equipment, and
furniture and materials; and (c) professional services. In both cases, IDA
has been requested to disburse their funds. Norway is participating in the
credit under an agreement to be signed with the Association immediately after
credit signature. ADAB is in the process of finalizing its agreement with
the RTG. During negotiations, it was agreed that as a condition of credit
effectiveness all conditions precedent to the effectiveness of the ADAB
agreement will have been satisfied.
5.05      USAID and CIDA have expressed definite interest in participating
in the project. They have identified with the RTG the components they would
like to finance and are in the process of completing their project documen-
tation and securing the approval of their management. Specifically, CIDA
is expected to finance: (a) 11 person-months of advisory assistance for
the design of studies to be undertaken by the NFPP and the population insti-
tutes; (b) 12.5 million cycles of oral contraceptives and two million doses
of injectable contraceptives; and (c) E&R studies carried out by the NFPP
and the population institutes. USAID is expected to finance the primary
health care activities in the 20 AFPH provinces, namely: (a) short-term
training; (b) E&R activities; and (c) short-term technical assistance for
the design of studies to be undertaken by the Health Planning Division and
independent local research institutes. It will also finance through its
assistance to the NFPP the costs of around 20 vans for the sterilization
teams. CIDA expects to obtain its management's approval in late January
and to sign its agreement with the RTG by early April. During negotiations,
agreement was reached as a condition of credit effectiveness that CIDA will
have signed its agreement with the RTG. USAID has obtained the approval of
its management to develop detailed project documents on the basis of the
components proposed above. USAID is expected to finalize its agreement
