The proposed contributions of all donors are based on the cost estimates
provided in this report. The RT'G will finance the balance of the project
costs (30%) not financed by the donors, namely 28% of civil works components
and 100% of the salaries and allowances and of the family planning and nutri-
tion education materials included in the project.
5.06      By FY 1981 the project is expected to add about US$8 million a year
on average to the MOPH's operating costs.  In FY 1977, the Ministry's total
operating costs stood at around US$125 million; by FY 1981 they are expected
to be between $180 and $200 million in constant prices. These figures indi-
cate that the project, which will account for most of the service-network
expansion in the 20 AFPH provinces and for the establishment of all new para-
medical training institutions, will be responsible for around 11-15% of the
expected total increase in recurrent costs. Total MOPH costs have recently
accounted for just under 4% of the RTG's total recurrent expenditures; by FY
1981 the figure is expected to rise to around 5 or 6%, reflecting the higher
priority being given to health care and family planning during the Fourth Plan
period. Thus, the increases directly related to the project are reasonable
both in terms of resources likely to be available to the MOPH and in terms
of the proportion of the population they will benefit.
C. IDA-Financed Components
5.07      The proposed IDA Credit, which will be the Association's first
for a population project in Thailand, will assist in financing primarily



- 27 -
the capital cost components of the project, with important contributions
to incremental contraceptive supplies, technical assistance, and unforeseen
innovative activities. These components were selected on the basis of the
expressed preferences of the contributing donors and the RTG. Specifically,
the IDA financed components will include: (a) the construction, furnishing
and equipping of three practical nurse/midwifery schools, one midwifery
school with MCH center, one dormitory and staff housing for the NFPP Training
Center, 225 midwifery centers, 150 health centers, 19 ten-bed district hospi-
tals and 120 child nutrition centers; the upgrading of 30 midwifery centers
to health centers and of six ten-bed district hospitals to 30-bed district
hospitals; and the expansion of one nursing school, two midwifery schools
with MCH centers, and two midwifery schools; (b) the provision of about 90
vehicles and 1,550 motorcycles at the national level and 125 vehicles and
2,500 motorcycles at the provincial level; tc) the provision of audiovisual
production and utilization equipment required at the national and provincial
levels; (d) the provision of one million doses of injectable contraceptives
to finance the incremental supply requirements; (e) the provision of technical
assistance (a health planner/economist for 36 person-months) and for profes-
sional services for design and construction supervision to supplement the
services provided by the Design and Construction Division; and (f) provision
for the financing of unforeseen innovative activities.
5.08      The total cost of the items above is estimated at around US$48.1
million. The IDA credit of US$33.1 million would finance about 48% of total
project costs and would cover about US$14.8 million in foreign exchange, or
about 67% of the total foreign exchange costs, and US$18.3 million in local
costs, or about 39% of total local costs.
D. Administrative Arrangements for Project Implementation
5.09      The project will be administered and coordinated by the I4OPH
through the Office of the Under-secretary of State for Public Health, who
is the senior civil servant in the Ministry. The Deputy Under-secretary of
State for Planning and Evaluation will be designated as Project Director,
responsible for overall coordination and implementation. The Project
Director's office will have attached to it a Project Administration and
Financing Unit, which will provide the Project Director with staff support
in the following areas related to the coordination of project administration:
reporting project progress; processing reimbursement applications; approving
contract documents and tender specifications for civil works, vehicles and
special equipment; coordinating the preparation and submittal to the Asso-
ciation of all designs for facilities to be constructed under the project;
and monitoring of project planning and implementation activities. The Proj-
ect Administration and Financing Unit will include: one financial adminis-
trator, one health planner/economist, one coordinator of field activities,
two accountants, and two secretary/typists. During negotiations agreement



- 28 -
was reached that the RTG will:  (a) establishas a condition of credit effec-
tiveness, and thereafter maintain and staff with suitably qualified personnel,
the Project Administration and Financing Unit under the Project Director; (b)
vest the Unit with responsibility for coordinating project administration
including the matters specified above. Agreement was also reached that the
RTG will: (a) audit the accounts and financial statements of the Project
Administration and Financing Unit, in accordance with appropriate auditing
principles consistently applied by independent auditors acceptable to the
Association; and (b) will furnish to the Association not later than four
months after the end of each fiscal year, certified copies of such audited
statements, and the report by the auditors.
5.10      The' technical aspects of project implementation will be handled
by the following Divisions of MOPH: Family Health (responsible for family
planning), Rural Health, Construction and Design, Nursing Colleges, Health
Education, Health Planning, Health Training, and Nutrition. To ensure effec-
tive coordination and that appropriate project actions are taken, a Project
Coordinating Committee consisting of the heads of these eight Divisions and
chaired by the Project Director or his Deputy will be established. Agree-
ment was reached during negotiations that the RTG will: (a) establish,-as a
condition of credit effectiveness, and thereafter maintain, a Project Coor-
dinating Committee under Chairmanship of the Project Director or his Deputy
and composed of the heads of these eight Divisions; and (b) vest the Project
Coordinating Committee with the responsibility for ensuring effective coordi-
nation of the activities of the Divisions in the execution of the Project.
Annex 7 provides a description of the administrative arrangements for imple-
menting the project.
5.11      Since the project provides for an expansion of MOPH activities,
particularly in the 20 AFPH provinces, incremental salary support is in-
cluded for those divisions in the MOPH which will have to take on addi-
tional responsibilities.
5.12      Family planning and health services in the 20 AFPH provinces will
be administered as part of the Ministry's strengthened provincial health
care system. The project will utilize the existing system in accordance
with MOPH plans to strengthen the provincial administration to include plan-
ning, implementation, budget allocation, and personnel control. This decen-
tralization will enable provincial administrators to have greater flexibil-
ity in solving local health problems. At the same time the proposed project
will fully utilize existing committees, including Provincial Health Advisory
Councils, District Health Committees, Tambol Councils, and Muban Committees,
to coordinate health activities and to ensure the involvement of community
leaders in setting health policy. Project implementation in each of the 20
AFPH provinces will be overseen by the Health Planning and Evaluation Com-
mittee, which will be responsible for planning, evaluation, follow-up, and
coordination of project implementation. These working committees, which
have been established as part of the Ministry's efforts to decentralize
decision-making, are comprised of representatives of the various health
departments along with invited resource persons from other ministries and



- 29 -
non-governmental organizations. Day-to-day project implementation will be
the responsibility of the provincial chief medical officers and their staffs
in the provincial health offices. Staff support for planning, managing, and
evaluating project activities at the provincial level will be provided pri-
marily by the new Planning and Evaluation Sections of the provincial health
offices. Each of these Sections will be staffed by a chief, an epidemiologist,
and a statistician. The proposed project includes incremental salary support
for these cadres. These arrangements for project administration are described
in detail in Annex 7.
E. Physical Facility Implementation
5.13      Administrative arrangements for implementation of the construction
components of the project are as follows:
a.   Designs for project buildings will be based on standard
designs that have been developed by the Design and Con-
struction Division of the MOPH. Responsibility for the
remaining design work and for supervising site selection
and construction work will be assigned to the special sec-
tion within the Design and Construction Division that will
work full-time on the project. To avoid over-extending
the capacity of this section, the peak load of required
professional services will be covered by technical staff
temporarily recruited from outside the MOPH. During nego-
tiations the RTG agreed to submit for IDA approval all
designs for facilities to be constructed under the project.
b.   Criteria for Site Selection have been agreed between the
Government and the IDA and will be applied in judging the
suitability of land already owned by the Government or
donated by the community. To ensure optimal siting of all
facilities, during negotiations the RTG agreed: (i) that
the physical facilities to be constructed under the project
will be properly sited according to the agreed criteria
(Annex 2, Appendix 9); and (ii) that sites will be pur-
chased where suitable donated or Government-owned land does
not exist.
c.   Construction Work under the project is within the capacity
of the local building industry. The small and scattered
construction work in the rural areas will attract only
small, but reasonably capable local contracting firms.
The Design and Construction Division, which handles all
aspects of the MOPH's construction program, does not have
sufficient capacity to handle the substantial increase in
construction volume scheduled for the project. Provision
is therefore made for professional support services (as well
as some additional staff) to enable the Division to handle



- 30 -
adequately the construction program of the project, par-
ticularly in supervising the construction of rural health
facilities.
d.   Furniture and Equipment will be standardized for the different
types of facilities. Standard lists have been prepared and
are being reviewed.
e.   Physical Implementation is scheduled to be completed in
three years.
F. Procurement
5.14      Procurement of items financed by USAID and CIDA will follow the
normal procurement procedures of those agencies. Procurement of all goods
and services financed through the ADAB grant and the IDA credit (including
its Norwegian grant component) will follow the Association's normal procedures
except for (a) the construction and equipping of the child nutrition centers,
which will be built by the villagers themselves, using locally available mate-
rials purchased under procedures acceptable to the Association; and (b) in-
jectable contraceptives (totall:ing US$1 million), which will be purchased on
a negotiated basis since at present only two products are on the market and
only one of these is currently approved for use by the RTG.
5.15      With the exception of the child nutrition centers, all civil works
to be tendered in a given year will be grouped separately for the national
and provincial levels in appropriate bid packages on the basis of geographical
location and type of work to be performed. Contracts for packages of civil
works totalling the equivalent of US$750,000 or more will be awarded after
international competitive bidding in accordance with Bank/IDA guidelines.
At the national level there are expected to be five such contracts totalling
approximately US$6.8 million. Most national-level institutions are to be
constructed in widely separated locations so very little grouping will be
possible. It is expected that Thai contractors will be the successful bid-
ders in most cases. At the provincial level, separate bid packages for all
construction in each of the 20 AFPH provinces will be prepared annually in
such a fashion as to attract qualified contractors. It is anticipated that
none of these packages will be large enough, however, to require international
competitive bidding. Contracts for packages of civil works totalling less
than US$750,000 will be awarded on the basis of local competitive bidding in
accordance with Government procurement procedures, which are acceptable to
the Association. Foreign contractors are allowed to participate in local
tendering although it is unlikely that they will do so.
5.16      Similarly, contracts for vehicles, for equipment and supplies,
and for furniture will be grouped in appropriate bid packages. Except as
specified below, those for vehicles and for equipment and supplies will be
awarded after international competitive bidding in accordance with Bank/IDA
guidelines. In the evaluation of bids, domestic manufacturers will be



- 31 -
accorded a preference margin of 15% of the c.i.f. price of competing imports
or, if lower, the applicable customs duties. Contracts for equipment and
supplies that cannot be reasonably grouped in packages equivalent to at least
US$100,000 and contracts for furniture will be awarded after local competitive
bidding in accordance with Government procurement procedures acceptable to
the Association. Notwithstanding the above, however, in order to provide
flexibility in the procurement of smaller items, contracts for equipment,
vehicles, and supplies where the individual contracts total less than the
equivalent of US$10,000 each may be procured through prudent shopping after
solicitation of at least three price quotations so long as their aggregate
does not exceed the equivalent of US$100,000. The Project Administration
and Financing Unit will be responsible for the procurement of vehicles, equip-
ment and supplies, and furniture under the project.
G. Disbursements
5.17      Disbursements from the proposed IDA Credit would be on the following
basis:
(a) 72% of expenditures for health centers;
(b) 72% of expenditures for training institutions, district
hospitals and midwifery centers;
(c) 100% of expenditures for directly imported equipment,
furniture, vehicles and materials; 65% of expendi-
tures for locally procured off-the-shelf items; and
100% of expenditures (ex-factory) for items procured
locally;
(d) 100% of foreign expenditures for contraceptives;
(e) 100% of expe&nditures for the services of a health
planner/ecdnomist;
(f) 100% of expenditures for other technical assistance; and
(g) 100% of expenditures for innovative activities approved
by IDA.
Norway's participation in the credit in an amount of about US$3.1 million
will, at its request, be used to help finance items (a) and (e) above.
Norway would reimburse the Association for disbursements it has made against
these items at the rate of 76% and 100% of such disbursements respectively.
The mechanism for doing so will be spelled out in the participation agree-
ment to be signed with the Association which calls for the establishment
of a special account at the Bank of Norway in the Association's name to be
periodically replenished with Norwegian kroner. At the request of the
Australian Government, the Association will disburse the ADAB grant against



- 32 -
initial expenditures for items (b), (c) and (f) above, at the same disburse-
ment percentage as the IDA credit. When grant funds have been exhausted,
disbursement from the credit against these items would commence. The ADAB
would establish a special account at the Reserve Bank of Australia in an
amount of $A 2.6 million (currently equivalent to US$3.0 million) from which
the grant funds would be disbursed. Disbursements from the ADAB grant ac-
count are expected to be completed by June 30, 1979 and those from the IDA
credit account, by December 31, 1981. The estimated quarterly disbursement
schedule is given in Annex 11.
H. Project Readiness
5.18      The MOPH, as the principal implementing agency, is completing the
details of project preparation so that implementation can begin as scheduled
on February 1, 1978. Preparation is fairly advanced. Civil works drawings
have been completed, locations selected, and site acquisition nearly com-
pleted for the rural health facilities to be constructed in 1978. The loca-
tions and sites for all training institutions to be constructed have already
been selected. Standardized designs for major parts of the training insti-
tutions have been completed and have been modified where necessary to fit
the space requirements of the training institutions. The MOPH is also com-
pleting the drawing up of specifications and tender advertisements for the
rural health facilities and vehicles financed under the project. To com-
plete the preparation work and to assist in the selection of sites, the MOPH
has hired two architects, three engineers and six technicians to staff the
special section of the Design and Construction Division. Regarding non-
civil works components, the MOPH has in most instances completed the curricula
for short-term training courses,, initiated preparation or identification of
training materials, and begun training of trainers. IEC and E&R preparation
activities are also well advanced. Further, the MOPH has met with the Civil
Service Commission and the Budget Bureau. It has requested Civil Service Com-
mission approval of the posts required in the first two years of the project
and has held discussions with the Budget Bureau to ensure that sufficient
budgetary resources are available in FY1978 to implement the project. The
MOPH has also completed detailed implementation schedules for the various
aspects of the project. These are included in Annexes 2-7. The MOPH intends
to up-date these each year. During negotiations, the RTG agreed to submit to
the Association, for review and comment, a detailed schedule of works expected
to be carried out during the next year, not later than 90 days prior to the
start of FY 1979 and of each fiscal year thereafter.
VI. PROJECT JUSTIFICATION
6.01      The current target of the NFPP is to reduce the crude birth rate
from the present 34 per thousand to 29 per thousand by the end of FY 1981.
This will require the recruitment of about three million new acceptors dur-
ing the Fourth Five-Year Plan, an average of 600,000 new acceptors per annum,



- 33 -
compared with an average of 490,000 during the preceding five years. By
1981, the number of active users (i.e., couples with the wife under 45 years
who practice contraception) will have to rise from the present figure of 1.3
million couples to more than 2.2 million. If the target fertility reduction
is achieved by FY 1981 and a net reproduction rate of 1.0 is reached in 1990,
the total population of Thailand will by the year 2000 be 42 million less
than if fertility remained constant at the 1970 level (Annex 12).
6.02      The principal justification of the project lies in its contribu-
tion to the NFPP's fertility-reduction objectives. It is not possible
to quantify precisely this contribution independent of the NFPP's overall
performance. The selection and design of project components is based on
judgments as to the resources and activities needed to reach the stated goals
of the project. Since the extension and strengthening of the rural health-
delivery system is the principal means of realizing the project's primary
fertility-reduction objective, it is inevitable that the health system itself
will be a major beneficiary of the project. As a result of the project, the
coverage of medical care by physicians will increase from 29% in 1977 to 31%
in 1980, and the coverage of preventive and promotive health care by para-
medics (sanitarians and auxiliary midwives) will be increased from 56% in 1977
to 66% in 1980. In the 20 AFPH provinces medical coverage will increase from
25% to 28% and health care from 47% to 65%. In these provinces, upon project
completion, community-based primary health care and family planning services
will be available to 90% of the village population, or to over 14 million
people. The project will thus produce an important set of ancillary health
benefits, chief among which will be improvements in MCH including improved
child nutrition.
6.03      Finally, the Association's support for the NFPP, accompanied by a
well-coordinated program of other external support, will lay the foundation
for future reductions in fertility.

THAILAND
National Family Planning Program Targets
Family Planning Program Performance
1.        Since 1970, the Thai National Family Planning Program has
developed into one of the most effective in the world (Appendix 1).
The results of the 1975 World Fertility Survey indicate that over 30%
of women of reproductive age in Thailand were practicing some form of
contraception, continuing a very sharp upward trend since the late 1960s.
2.        Thailand began its program with the advantage of a fairly exten-
sive health network, including "auxiliary nurse midwives" deployed to some
3,800 points in rural areas and only a moderate shortage of doctors. These
circumstances permitted the family planning and public health programs to
be integrated and conducted by existing health personnel.
3.        For the most part, the numbers of acceptors have significantly
and consistently exceeded the targets, although there have been striking
changes in the methods chosen (Appendix 2). In 1970 and 1971 the program
achieved a breakthrough in the distribution of oral contraceptives by
authorizing screening and resupply through auxiliary nurse midwives. In
recent years the number of sterilizations has grown rapidly; in 1975, there
were about 90,000 (primarily tubal ligations). Comparatively speaking, in-
jectables play a more important role in the Thai program than in any other
country. Continuation rates have been uniformly high, and the spread of
acceptance across different age and parity groups has produced a younger
distribution of acceptors than in many other countries (partly because of
a strong trend in this direction in the national postpartum program). For
three consecutive years (1973-1975) about 60% of all acceptors were below
30, and about 60% had fewer than four children.
4.        In recent years, the program has made use of mass media and school
education. It has offered subsidies to promote sterilization. Compared with
other national programs, it is well dispersed throughout rural areas, reach-
ing at the periphery, small (one or two room) health clinics. Although the
general performance has been successful, there is considerable variation
among provinces. During 1974, the proportion of married women under 45 who
accepted family planning services ranged from 20.5% in Khon Kaen province
to 2.0% in Si-Sa-kat (Appendix 3). Oral contraceptives were the most popu-
lar method, the majority of new acceptors in all but three provinces choos-
ing that method. On the average, about 7% of all married women under 45
accepted the oral contraceptive; about 1.4% and 1.5% accepted the IUD and
sterilization, respectively.



ANNEX 1
Page 2
Demographic Targets
5.        For the first Five-Year Family Planning Program period (FY 1972-1976),
the demographic target was to reduce the rate of growth of population from 3%
to about 2.5%. It is now reasonably certain that this target was reached. 1/
The target for the period FY 1977 to FY 1981 is 2.1%. The crude death rate is
expected to decline by 0.3 per 1,000 per year, thus falling to 8.0 per 1,000
by 1981. If this 2.1% rate of growth is to be reached, the crude birth rate
must therefore fall from an estimated 34.5 per 1,000 at the end of 1976 to
29.0 per 1,000 by the end of 1981. Empirically, a crude birth rate of 30 per
1,000 population is associated with a contraceptive prevalence rate among
women of reproductive age of about 30%. The current rate (over 30%), is
likely to increase as services are extended more widely, so that the goal of
a 29 per 1,000 crude birth rate is well within reach. Moreover, the expanded
national family planning program will lay the foundation for further reduc-
tions in the rate of growth of population after 1981.
Health Targets
6.        It is the objective of the Fourth Five-Year Development Plan
(FY 1977-1981) to reduce both the maternal and infant mortality rates by
20%. To achieve these targets, public health services must be improved
and expanded. The plan proposes to provide:
a.   prenatal care to 90% of the pregnant women in Bangkok and
80% of the pregnant women in other urban and rural areas;
b.   delivery services to 930% of the pregnant women in Bangkok,
60% of the pregnant women in other urban areas, and 44% of
the pregnant women in rural areas; and
c.   postnatal care to 60% of urban women and 68% of rural women
after delivery.
7.        In addition, the plan proposes to improve the nutrition of preg-
nant and lactating women and of infants and pre-school children (0-5 years
of age) by providing nutrition education for new mothers, emphasizing breast
feeding and pre-school health care, and providing high protein supplementary
foods for infants (0-6 months), children (6 months to 2 years), and pre-school
children (3-5 years).
Acceptor Targets
8.        National Level:  For the First-Year Family Planning Program Plan
(FY 1972-1976), the Government set targets for recruitment of acceptors, by
1/ Based on recent data from the World Fertility Survey.

Page 3
method (Appendix 2). The achievement in terms of IUD acceptors has been very
close to the targets over the five years. Acceptors of oral contraceptives,
the most popular method, have exceeded the targets in all but one year. The
demand for sterilization, however, has shown a consistent and significant
increase over the five-year period. In 1971, the target was exceeded by
nearly 20%; in 1975, more than twice as many persons chose sterilization as
had been targeted.
9.        The second Five-Year Plan (FY 1977-1981) also sets acceptor targets
for achieving the demographic objective. Since sterilization and injectables
have proven to be such popular methods, the targets for these methods have
been increased substantially. Over the five-year period the program expects
to recruit more than three million additional acceptors (Table 1). Rough
estimates indicate that 1.6 million of these acceptors will still be prac-
ticing contraception by mid-1981, and about 3.2 million couple-years of
contraceptive protection will be achieved within the five years.

ANNEX 2

THAILAND
DELIVERY OF INTEGRATED HEALTH AND FAMIILY PLANNING SERVICES
A. Present Situation: Key Role of the Ministry of
Public Hlealth in Family Planning
General
1.        Thailand's National Family Planning Program (NFPP), which is
integrated with the basic health program, is administered by the Ministry
of Public Health (MOPH). 1/ In 1975, 97% of the 5,300 registered family
planning service units in the country were part of the MOPH health delivery
system. Virtually every type of public health center--from big hospitals
in towns to midwifery centers in small villages--is used for delivering fam-
ily planning services. During the past ten years, MOPH facilities have
accounted for over 80% of the family planning acceptors recruited through
non-commercial channels. More details of the past performance of the pro-
gram are given in Appendix 1.
Increasing the Availability of Family Planning Services at Existing Facilities
2.        Thailand has pioneered in increasing the availability of many family
planning services by entrusting their delivery to lower levels of health pro-
fessionals (see Appendix 2). Family planning performance records for Thailand
show that the larger the number of health facilities and staff available, the
greater the number of acceptors. In 1970, for example, a ruling that permit-
ted 3,500 auxiliary midwives to prescribe oral contraceptives on the basis
of a simple checklist of contra-indications increased the number of service
points for oral contraceptives from 270 to nearly 3,800, and as a result the
number of oral contraceptive acceptors jumped from 132,000 in 1970 to 295,000
in 1971.
3.        Provincial hospitals provide a full range of contraceptive
services from sterilizations to condoms. District hospitals 2/ provide
1/   In the Bangkok metropolitan area, health services are the responsibility
of the Bangkok Municipality. Some health services are provided by other
agencies (see para. 14).
2/   The term "district hospital" is now used for what was called before 1976
a "medical and health center" or a "first class health center." Even-
tually every district hospital will have either ten or 30 beds, but
during the transitional period there are some that do not, and some that
are not yet staffed by physicians.



ANNEX 2
Page 2
IUDs and oral contraceptives and, as the physicians staffing them are
trained to do so, they are beginning to provide sterilizations as well.
Health centers 1/ and midwifery centers primarily provide oral contracep-
tives, but auxiliary midwives, who are based at both, eventually will be
trained to perform IUD insertions as well. The proportions of various
family planning services provided at each type of facility are shown in
Appendix 3.  It is expected that the general use of the injectable contra-
ceptive will be authorized by mid-1978, which means that this type of con-
traceptive will be available in the future at health and midwifery centers
and, of course, at hospitals.
Introduction of New Methods
4.        Sterilization and IUDs:  Current training programs are making
female sterilizations and IUDs more widely available. In 1975, for exam-
ple, after 100 doctors from district hospitals had been trained to perform
mini-laparatomies, they completed 8,000. Together with male sterilizations,
a total of 90,000 sterilizations were performed in 1975, thus establishing
an annual performance rate for Thailand--l.6% of married couples of repro-
ductive age--that is among the highest in the world. During 1977 another
140 doctors from district hospitals are being trained to perform steril-
izations. Although initially only physicians were allowed to insert IUDs,
tests indicate that continuation rates are as good and client reactions are
better when insertions are performed by nurses. Beginning in March 1975,
nurses were permitted to insert IUDs, and a nationwide training program was
started. It is expected that, if a current program is successful, auxiliary
midwives will be permitted to insert IUDs by 1978 or early 1979, and a mas-
sive training program will be undertaken immediately thereafter.
5.        The Injectable Contraceptive:  Thailand has played an important
role in the clinical investigation and use of an injectable progestogen. 2/
Much of the initial work was done at McCormick Hospital in Chiengmai, where
over half a million three-month injections were given between 1965 and the
end of 1976. The MOPH approved the method in 1974, and in 1975 trials were
arranged in 14 provinces. So far, only doctors and nurses have been per-
mitted to inject this compound, so that its use has been restricted to
about 300 provincial and district hospitals. Pilot tests using auxiliary
1/ The term "health center" is now applied to what formerly was called a
" second class health center."
2/  The compound used, depot medroxyprogesterone acetate or DMPA, is also
known by the trade name Depo-Provera. At least one other injectable
compound is about to be marketed. This report will simply use the
term "injectable contraceptive" throughout.



ANNEX 2
Page 3
midwives have been successful, and it is expected that by mid-1978 they
will be allowed to administer the injectable contraceptive at health and
midwifery centers. The trials conducted in Thailand confirmed that neither
return of fertility nor health status was adversely affected by the use of
the injectable contraceptive. In addition, the injectable has proven to be
popular among Thai women and fits well into the family planning delivery
system.
6.        The Thai Government, in reaching its present position, has satis-
fied itself that the injectable contraceptive is safe and appropriate to use
in Thailand. Foreign governments and international agencies have helped the
NFPP to introduce the injectable contraceptive, but now that the number of
service points providing this method is to be greatly increased, an assured
supply is required (see para. 44). The number of injectable contraceptive
acceptors increased from 24,000 in 1975 to 67,000 in 1976. This is a jump
from 5% to 11% of all acceptors. Acceptance continued to increase rapidly
during the first months of 1977, 1/ but then the MOPH had to restrict the
injectable to those women who were already using it due to the rapidly
diminishing supply.
Need to Increase Number of Family Planning Service Points
7.        Taking into account the recent and current development described
above, the NFPP has gone about as far as it can in exploiting the possibil-
ities of the existing delivery network. If further progress is to be made,
the network itself must be expanded in terms of service points and staff
(including lay distributors or volunteers) to run them. Experience has
shown that increasing the number of service points leads to an immediate
increase in the number of acceptors. Since there is no evidence that the
saturation point has been reached or is even near, the project is designed
to assist the MOPH to: (a) increase the number of service points providing
integrated family planning and basic health services, particularly rural
midwifery and health centers; (b) increase demand for the services available
at these points; and (c) use village health volunteers to delivery oral
contraceptives and condoms. Special attention will be given to 20 provinces
which at present are far less well served than the rest of the country.
B. Rural Health Facilities
Linkage between Family Planning and Health
8.        The NFPP's emphasis on the integration of family planning and health
services is maintained in the rural areas where 85% of the people live. This
1/   During the first six months of 1977, over 34,000 injectable contraceptive
acceptors were recruited, most of these in the first quarter of calendar
1977.



ANNEX 2
Page 4
integration has important advantages, since family planning and basic health
services, especially maternal and child health (MCH) services, can reinforce
and support each other. Among other things, improved health services reduce
child mortality, and thus reduce the need for large families. Family plan-
ning is readily introduced in the course of routine antenatal and postnatal
MCH care when the same health workers are responsible for both services.
9.        Already MOPH's rural health facilities 1/ recruit about 60-70%
of all non-commercial family planning acceptors in Thailand (and 75-80%
of all MOPH acceptors). While non-MOPH delivery networks may grow in
relative importance, MOPH's rural health network is likely to remain the
single most important source of family planning services in Thailand.
Plans for Increasing the Number of Health Facilities
10.       With technical assistance from WHO, the MOPH has prepared a number
of ambitious health projects for the period of the Fourth Five-Year National
Economic and Social Development Plan, FY 1977-81. The Provincial Health
Care Project includes the expansion of rural health facilities, the inte-
gration of provincial hospitals into the national health system, and the
widespread use of mobile units. The type of facility allocated to an admin-
istrative unit depends mainly on its population (Appendix 4). In general,
each rural district 2/ with at least 30,000 people should have either a
ten-bed or a 30-bed hospital; each tambol with more than 5,000 people should
have a health center; and each tambol or group of villages (mubans) with
between 2,000 and 5,000 people should have a midwifery center. Some dis-
tricts are not eligible for district hospitals, and served by health cen-
ters and midwifery centers only. Likewise, some tambols are not eligible
for health centers. Many of the districts or tambols that meet the cri-
teria do not yet have the facilities to which they are entitled. Table 1
indicates the shortfall in rural health facilities as of late 1976.
1/ For the purpose of this report, "rural health facilities" are defined
as all facilities that come under the aegis of the Rural Health Division,
namely, district hospitals, health centers, and midwifery centers.
2/ For administrative purposes, Thailand is divided into 71 provinces,
each of which in turn is divided into an average of about nine districts.
Districts, in turn, are divided into units known as tambols; there is
an average of about ten tambols per district. Finally, tambols are
divided into mubans or villages, with an average of ten mubans to
a tambol.

11.       MOPH's plans for the first year of the Fourth Five-Year Develop-
ment Plan (FY 1977) emphasize the establishment of additional 30-bed dis-
trict hospitals in order to provide the full range of medical services
that can be offered by district hospitals and to serve as a referral center
for expanded primary medical care generated by lower level rural health
facilities.

13.       It is estimated that population covered by rural health facilities
will increase considerably by the end of the Fourth Five-Year Plan. The
coverage of medical care by physicians will increase from 21% to 32%, and
the coverage of preventive and promotive health care by paramedical person-
nel (junior sanitarians and auxiliary midwives) will increase from 53% to
67% (see Appendix 5). These increases in coverage will considerably in-
crease the population covered by family planning services.
Other Sources of Family Planning and Basic Health Services
14.       About 20% of the family planning acceptors in Thailand are recruited
through non-commercial sources outside the MOPH, the most important of which
are McCormick Hospital in Chiang Mai and the Community-Based Family Planning
Services. The former, as mentioned above, has demonstrated the popularity
and safety of the injectable contraceptive among rural Thai women. The lat-
ter has organized a widespread system of village-level distributors who sell
oral contraceptives and condoms, keeping part of the proceeds as an incentive.
Called the Village Program, this is the major distribution program operated
by Community-Based Family Planning Services. Distributors, who typically
are shopkeepers, are identified in each village or muban (population of
about 500 to 1,000) and given several days' training. They are supervised
and resupplied by district supervisors, who work closely with the district
health officer and other MOPH officials.  By October 1977 this program cov-
ered 130 districts (90 supported by the International Planned Parenthood
Federation and 40 by USAID). Another 40 districts (also funded by USAID)
were about to be opened up with an expanded version of the program that
includes health and hygiene activities on the part of the distributors.



ANNEX 2
Page 7
Gradually health and hygiene activities will also be added to the respon-
sibilities of all distributors. The relationship between these activities
and the MOPH's new Primary Health Care Program is described below in para.
27. Community-Based Family Planning Services also has a Public Institution
Program, which involves distribution of family planning information and sup-
plies through large Government and industrial organizations, and a Private
Sector Program, which involves special outlets for family planning supplies
in urban areas and fund raising activities (e.g., the sale of special family
planning T-shirts). The Planned Parenthood Association of Thailand concen-
trates mainly on information, education and communication (IEC) activities
and has only a small service program. Other ministries have hospitals, as
do the major universities. The latter have played an important role in
pilot family planning projects. The Ministry of Interior has a number of
basic health programs, and many of its paramedical staff have been trained
in family planning by the MOPH.
C. Strengthening the NFPP: An Integrated Approach
Additional Personnel to Staff New Facilities
15.       Because Thailand's NFPP links family planning services with gen-
eral health services, the strengths and weaknesses of the system as a whole
are in many ways the strengths and weaknesses of the family planning pro-
gram. As indicated above, the health network must be expanded in order to
bring clinic-based family planning and basic health services closer to the
people in rural areas but, without an increase in personnel (particularly
nurses, practical nurse/midwives, and auxiliary midwives), it will not be
possible to staff the facilities that are to be built. A detailed analysis of
staffing needs and a description of plans for meeting them by expanding the
capacity of basic training institutions are to be found in Annex 3.
Utilization of Facilities and Quality of Service
16.       There has been some concern in Thailand about the underutilization
of rural health facilities. A review of the factors affecting utilization by
the 14OPH has identified two main causes of underutilization, The first, the
poor siting of facilities, can be largely dealt with through improved siting
criteria, and through the provision in the project of funds to purchase land
where donated land is poorly situat:ed. The second cause is inadequate quality
of service in terms of the availability and training of staff, the services
they can offer (there is much demand for curative services), and the avail-
ability of drugs and other supplies. The project will provide a number of
inputs to help to overcome these problems: additional staff, short-term
training to upgrade staff skills in management and curative services, improved
supervision and management, and adequate supplies of contraceptives. On other
points, such as the availability of drugs and medical supplies, adequate
supplies of fuel and spare parts, etc., agreement was reached with the RTG
during negotiations that it would ensure that these supplies and materials
will be readily available as required.



ANNEX 2
Page 8
Direct Improvement of Family Planning Services
17.       The project will assist the Family Health Division to expand
its training capacity (Annex 4); improve its information, education, and
communication (IEC) activities (Annex 5); and undertake additional eval-
uation and research (E&R) projects (Annex 6).
18.       Nationwide Impact:  By means of inputs described above, the proj-
ect will contribute to an increase in the number of clinic-based family plan-
ning delivery points from the present 5,400 to 7,000 by 1980. In addition,
the project will enable the Ministry's new community-based primary health
care program to provide basic family planning services (resupply of oral
contraceptives and distribution of condoms) in about half of Thailand's
55,000 villages. These contributions are expected to lead to an increase
in the annual number of family planning acceptors from 535,000 in 1975 to
about 755,000 by 1980.
19.       Impact in 20 Disadvantaged Provinces:  Certain project inputs
(particularly new rural health facilities, salary support for upgraded
staffing, and various forms of short-term training) will be concentrated
in 20 of Thailand's 71 provinces which, in comparison with other provinces,
have fewer health service units in relation to population, lower rates of
family planning acceptance, larger rural populations, and comparatively
better utilization rates. Appendix 6 describes the characteristics of these
20 disadvantaged provinces, which are shown on map no. 12792R at the back of
this report. The project will enable these provinces to catch up with the
national averages by 1980. For this reason they have been designated the
accelerated family planning and health (AFPH) provinces. Various inputs to the
20 provinces are discussed in the annexes referred to in para. 17 above, and a
summary of the total range of project inputs into the AFPH provinces is given
in Annex 8.

E. Staffing of Rural Health Facilities in the 20 AFPH Provinces
Incremental Paramedical Staff
22.       During the preparation of the project, the MOPH revised its stan-
dard staffing patterns to make them compatible with the projected level of
recruitment of key health staff in the 20 AFPH provinces during the plan
period (Appendix 14). Salary support for additional staff needed as the
result of the construction of new or upgraded facilities will be included
in the project (Appendix 15). By the end of the project period, the fol-
lowing additional staff will be supported through the project (this does
not include the new grades of staff described in paras. 24 and 25 below):
a.   75 nurses (including nurse practitioners);
b.   242 practical nurse/midwives;
c.   394 auxiliary midwives; and
d.   199 junior sanitarians.
1/   One two-wheel drive vehicle is provided to each of the 25 district
hospitals for supervision and transport for mobile clinic services
and referral services for patients.



ANNEX 2
Page 10
Changes in Staffing Patterns
23.       Additional Staff Members in Midwifery and Health Centers:  All
but 255 of the 5,431 rural health facilities in the country are staffed
by paramedical personnel only: one auxiliary midwife for each of the
midwifery centers and one auxiliary midwife and one junior sanitarian
for each health center. When these paramedics do field work, no one is
available at the centers to serve outpatients. The MOPH has, therefore,
obtained approval from the Civil Service Commission to add one practical
nurse/midwife as a second staff member at midwifery centers and as a
third staff member at health centers. The assignment of a practical
nurse/midwife to each facility will improve the simple medical care avail-
able and will permit auxiliary midwives and junior sanitarians to spend
more time in the field.
24.       Assistant Health Workers for Midwifery Centers:  The MOPH has
decided to implement this upgraded staffing pattern in the 20 AFPII prov-
inces ahead of the others, but because of the shortage of practical nurse/
midwives, an assistant health worker will be appointed, as a stop-gap
measure, as the second staff member at midwifery centers. The project
will support the training and salaries of 735 of these assistant health
workers, who will be recruited locally and will receive six weeks of
training at the regional MCH centers (Annex 4). As they prove themselves,
the assistant health workers will eventually be upgraded to practical
nurse/midwives or auxiliary midwives by taking additional training.
25.       Assistant Health Worker for MCH/Family Planning:  The project
will support the training nationwide of 450 assistant health workers for
MCH/family planning, who will be deployed in provincial hospitals, district
hospitals, and provincial health offices (Annex 4). One hundred and eighty
one of these assistant health workers will be posted to the 20 AFPH prov-
inces, and the project would provide salary support for them. The vast
majority (about 90%) will be assigned to district hospitals (one each) and
provincial hospitals (two each). The other 10% will be posted to provin-
cial health offices with family planning clinics and to the MCH centers.
Their main duties will be to set up MCH and family planning clinics and
assist in clinic sessions; to supply condoms and oral contraceptives; and
to carry out face-to-face communication activities (including the use of
simple audiovisual equipment being provided through the project) on
hospital wards, in clinics, and during home visits.
26.       During negotiations it was agreed that by no later than May 31,
1981 the RTG will staff all rural health facilities in the 20 AFPH prov-
inces--new and existing--in accordance with the staffing patterns described
above and as given in Appendix 14.



ANNEX 2
Page 11
F.  Extending Family Planning Services:  Primary Health Care
Village Health Volunteer/Health Communicator Program
27.       The MOPH is placing considerable emphasis on efforts to extend
basic health and family planning services to the village level in rural
areas. Called the "primary health care program", this approach has as its
core element the mobilization of volunteer health workers in the villages.
Over the past few years a variety of health projects in Thailand have made
use of different kinds of volunteers. WHO, for instance, has identified
about 14 such projects, including the use of volunteer distributors in the
Community-Based Family Planning Services program described above (para. 14).
Data gathered from the Lampang Project (formerly known as the DEIDS Project)
indicate that in a relatively short period of time the volunteers become known
and accepted as a source of health advice or services. After the first year
nearly 70% of the people in the project area were familiar with the volunteers.
Of these, about 55% had received advice from the communicators and nearly
all of these, in turn, had already acted on it or intended to do so.
About 30% of those who were familiar with the volunteers had turned to the
health post volunteers for help during illness. Consequently during the
Country Health Programming Exercise it was decided to launch a nationwide
scheme for recruiting two types of village-based volunteer health workers:
(a) village health volunteers, who have educational and minor curative
functions; and (b) village health communicators, who have educational
functions only. The ultimate aim is to have one village health volunteer
and an average of ten health communicators in each village without a health
or midwifery center. With an average of nine to ten villages (mubans) per
tambol, each tambol would eventually have about eight village health volun-
teers and 80 health communicators. The national scheme calls for about half
of the volunteers to be recruited and trained by the end of the five-year plan
period in 1981. In the 20 AFPH provinces, however, recruitment will be
accelerated and by the end of the project most of the villages in the scheme
will be covered. In 57 of the 199 districts in the AFPH provinces, Community-
Based Family Planning Services (para. 14) has already recruited and trained
family planning distributors at the village level. Currently they are under-
going additional training in order to add basic health and hygiene activities
to their responsibilities. In these 57 districts the project will not support
the development of village health volunteers and village health communicators.
In the remaining 142 districts there will be about 7,900 village health
volunteers and about 79,000 village health communicators, who will cover about
90% of the rural mubans in which no rural health facility is located by the
end of the project. Appendix 16 shows the distribution of districts between
the MIOPH primary health care program and the Community-Based Family Planning
Services program.
28.       The village health volunteers will supply condoms, and oral
contraceptives to women who have been screened for contraindications by
qualified paramedicals at a rural health facility. Likewise, they will



ANNEX 2
Page 12
provide minor health care in order to extend the services of the health
center to the village level. They will diagnose simple ailments and
sell household drugs. For problems with which the volunteer is unable to
cope, the patient will be referred to the nearest appropriate health facil-
ity. Finally, the volunteer will serve as "chairman" of the approximately
ten health communicators in the village.
29.       Three factors that appear to be vital in assuring an adequate
motivation and continuing participation on the part of volunteers are:
(a) management of the program at the local level; (b) recognition of the
volunteers' contributions by their communities and by the health system
itself; and (c) technical advice and support from the tambol health staff.
It is important, for instance, that health staff should not take the view
that the volunteers are there to carry out unskilled tasks that they prefer
not to do themselves. Consequently, both preparatory training (Annex 4)
and supervision of health and midwifery center staff are essential to a
successful volunteer program.
30.       The Initial Phase of the Program:  The implementation of the
village-level volunteer program will be phased through the first several
years of the current five-year plan. Implementation in the 20 AFPH prov-
inces will follow this original plan. As a result, the first phase of the
program has already been initiated in four AFPH provinces in 1977, i.e.,
before the start of the project. The other 14 provinces will begin the
program in 1978. The program in each province starts with (a) training
provincial and district level staff (Annex 4); and (b) testing the program
in one tambol in each district participating in the program. The experience
of these pilot tambols will be evaluated informally by the provincial and
district staff with the help of the E&R Section of the Health Education
Division and other offices at Ministry headquarters.
31.       The Second Phase:  Training Health Staff:  The latter part of the
first year and some of the second year will be devoted to training tambol
level paramedicals (auxiliary midwives and junior sanitarians) for their
role as trainers and technical supervisors of the village health volun-
teers and village health communicators. During the course of three years,
nearly 2,200 auxiliaries will undergo this training in the 142 districts
of 20 AFPH provinces that are participating in the program. This train-
ing is described in detail in Annex 4. All auxiliary midwives and junior
sanitarians (i.e., two staff members in each health center and one in each
midwifery center) will be responsible in this year and each subsequent
year for recruiting and training village health volunteers and health
communicators. Priority will be given to villages furthest away from
health facilities.
32.       Organizing the Program at the Village Level:  As a first step,
health staff will help village leaders to set up a committee (or use an
existing committee) whose primary function will be to assist in the selec-
tion and management of the village health volunteers and the health



ANN'iX 2
Page 13
communicators.  Whe-n t-e health communicators have agreed to serve, they
will elect their ow-n chaJirman, who will also serve as village health volun-
teer.  Local health st-ff will then arrange informal training for the vil-
lage health volunt.eer and the health communicators (Annex 4).  The health
communicators will work together with the village health volunteer as a team,
and as such, will hold occasional meetings, sometimes with the health center
staff and/or the village committee. l/
33.       Supervisory Responsibilities of Local Health Staff:  Besides advis-
ing the village nealth volunteer and the health communicators, the local
health staff will need to ensure t:hat they have adequate supplies of drugs
and IEC materials.  The volunteers and health communicators will all receive
special manuals, which have been included als part of the project training
costs for the 20 AFPH Drovinces. The villlage health volunteer will also
receive an initial allotment of drugs and a first-aid kit, the cost of the
latter being included in the project training budget.
34.       Evaluation. of the Ville Health Volunteer/Health Communicator
Program:. Ahthough the program design is based on considerable localized
experience in different parts of the country going back over several years,
the NMiOPH has just begun to initiate the program on a nationwide basis in 1977.
Each province will be testing the basic approach in one trial tambol per
district during the first months of the program in that province (para. 30).
This is primarily to make local adjiustments in the basic design and to gather
other useful feedback for provincial and district staff responsible for im-
plementation of the program. Beyond this, however, the newness of this
large-scale program requires that in the 20 AFPH provinces a more comprehen-
sive review and evaluation be undertaken after about a year's experience in
implementing the program.  More details on this evaluation study are given in
Annex 6.
Role of Traditional Midwives in Family Planning
35.       Traditional midwives, known as _ohtamyae, are being trained in MCH
and family planning under the aegis of MOPH's Family Health Division with
funding from USAID. Training and follow-up supervision will be provided by
auxiliary midwives in midwifery centers and health centers.  The traditional
midwives still perform a majority of the deliveries in rural Thailand, al-
though the proportion is steadily declining. In the 20 AFPH provinces about
2,000 will be trained, which is an average of one for each auxiliary midwife
working outside hospitals.
1/   It should be noted that variations on this model will occur in response
to local circumstances.



ANNEX 2
Page 14
Role of "Tambol Doctors" in Family Planning
36.       Traditional healers, often called "tambol doctors" 1/ are also
found widely in rural areas, and the Family Health Division has launched
a program to train them in family planning. In the 20 AFPH provinces,
about 1,200 will be trained, an average of less than one per health cen-
ter. Tambol doctors will also be allowed to supply oral contraceptives
and condoms. They will be supervised by the local health center staff.
Vehicles for Supervision of Primary Health Care Activities
37.       During the three years of the project, auxiliary midwives and
junior sanitarians in the 20 AFPH provinces will be assuming responsibil-
ity for an ever-growing number of village-level health and family planning
activities and workers (health volunteers, health communicators, traditional
midwives, and tambol doctors) in addition to their regular responsibilities
and will, therefore, require greater mobility. In the past UNICEF and, more
recently, UNFPA have provided motorcycles for auxiliary midwives, but some
do not as yet have them. Therefore the project includes 1,554 motorcycles
for auxiliary midwives outside the 20 AFPH provinces and 675 for those in the
20 provinces. In the 20 AFPH provinces, 1,661 motorcycles will be supplied
to junior sanitarians, who have hitherto not been provided with transport.
Supervision of the Primary Health Care Program
by District and Provincial-Level Staff
38.       The recruitment, training, and supervision of these village health
volunteers, health communicators, traditional midwives, and tambol doctors
will impose a heavy additional burden on the tambol health staff. Although
it is planned to train auxiliary midwives and junior sanitarians to cope
with this new burden, there is a clear need for constant supervision and
support from district and provincial staff. In the 20 AFPH provinces one
supervisor will be appointed to each district health office (as deputy dis-
trict health officer) and to each provincial health office. Their func-
tions are described in paras. 46 and 47 below.
Child Nutrition Centers and Community Initiative
39.       The child nutrition centers at rural health facilities provide
day-care services, supplementary feeding for children, and a focal point
for community participation and education. The three ministries offering
1/   In common usage in Thailand, "tambol doctor" refers to a number of
different types of healers, both traditional and modern. As used
here, it refers to various types of traditional healers and not
just to traditional healers who have been trained by and receive a
fixed monthly stipend from the Ministry of the Interior. In fact,
the majority of the latter received family planning training from
the Ministry of Health several years ago.



ANNEX 2
Page 15
day-care services (Public Health, Education, and Interior) have agreed to
a standardized  program.  In cooperation with the Ministry of Agriculture,
protein-enriched cookies are provided as a food supplement. In addition,
the auxiliary midwife responsible for the management of the center fre-
quently arranges for the mothers to attend cooking demonstrations and educa-
tional sessions in nutrition and family planning at the end of the day.
Day-care is provided by one or two young women attendants selected by the
mid-wife and the village committee. The MOPH pays each attendant a basic
stipend of US$18 per month, which is supplemented by monthly fees paid by
those parents who can afford to do so (about US$0.50 per month per family).
An average of 30 children is enrolled in each child nutrition center, and
as of early 1977 there were 465 centers in the country, 166 of them in the
20 AFPH provinces.
40.       To increase local participation in village health and family plan-
ning activities, the UIOPH supplies the building materials and equipment for
child nutrition centers, while the community provides the labor for construc-
tion. This helps to ensure that the centers are built only where they are
wanted and needed.  Health center attendance appears to increase after a
child nutrition center has been added, probably because the child nutrition
center not only meets a clear need but also serves to link the community
closer to the health center. Local interest and participation will in the
future be enlisted by village health volunteers and health communicators with
the support of the tambol health staff. Funds to cover building materials
and equipment for a maximum of 120 centers in the 20 AFPH provinces are
included in the project. Construction wi:Ll be based on a standard design.
G. Mobile Family Planning Services
41.       Physicians are deployed down to district hospitals; many districts,
however, (about 40% of those eligible) have no district hospitals at all.  The
MOPH is training district hospital physicians to perform sterilizations and
is also organizing a mobile family plannirng service team in each province to
enable trained physicians from provincial hospitals to meet the needs of
people outside the reach of district hospitals.  The project would provide 31
mobile vans, 20 for the AFPH provinces and 11 for other provinces.  USAID and
the MOPH will provide these vehicles as part of a separate USAID population
project agreement with the RTG. Under thaet agreement, USAID and the MOPH have
agreed to finance the vehicles on a matching basis.  Jointly USAID and the RTG
have already provided some 40 mobile vans for provinces outside of the 20 AFPH
provinces. Therefore, all 71 provinces will have a mobile team. The provin-
cial health office in each province will be responsible for the operation of
the mobile van. Each team is expected to work from seven to ten days a month
and in that time to perform about 100 vasectomies. The rest of the time the
van will be used for mobile medical care, which will also be the responsibil-
ity of the provincial health office. The Assistant health workers (MCH/family

planning) assigned to provincial health offices (and who are being trained
and, in the AFPH provinces, paid through the project) will join the mobile
teams on family planning days.

and three million doses of an injectable contraceptive will need to be pro-

1980                     6.5            5.5               12.0
1981                     8.0            4.5               12.5
TOTAL                   31.6           24.0               55.6
No immediate shortage of oral contraceptives is expected. However, since the
RTG will be more than doubling its budgetary support (in local currency) for
family planning activities throughout Thailand as a result of the project, the
Government has requested that its share of oral contraceptives needed in 1978
and 1979, as shown above, be furnished through external resources (as this
item requires 100% foreign exchange). Specifically the MOPH wishes to provide
its share through project financing during 1979 and 1980 (12.5 million cycles).
The above quantities of oral contraceptives are adequate at projected acceptance
and continuation rates.



ANNEX 2
Page 17
44.       Injectables:  Estimates of the injectables required during the
1978-80 period are necessarily uncertain, since demand for this method is
growing very rapidly. It is likely that auxiliary midwives will be permitted
to give injections by 1978, which will significantly increase the number of
service delivery points at which this method is available and therefore the
number of users.  Thus far the program has been supported by contributions of
injectable contraceptives from the United Kingdom and later from UNFPA, which
is now providing around 300,000 does per year. The use of injectables has
expanded to the point that a substantial inventory and an orderly schedule of
procurement are necessary. If annual new acceptors during the project period
reach around 125,000 per year as envisaged, and if mean use time of each
acceptor on average is two years, then approximately one million doses of
injectables will be required each year by the NFPP (Annex 1). 1/
i. Strengthening the Supervision of Family Planning and
Basic Rural Health Services
Need for Increased Supervision
45.       In addition to personnel, supp:Lies, facilities, and transport, one
other important ingredient--supervision---is necessary to ensure that adequate
services are available to the public.  The expansion in the number of facili-
ties and, more importantly, the addition of new activities--especially the
sizeable primary health care program--have increased the demands on MOPH's
technical supervisory staff. This situation--for a typical district--is shown
graphically in Appendix 17.
District Supervisors of Primary Health Care
46.       The primary health care program calls for new skills and activities
on the part of rural health workers.  In addition, the extent to which the
volunteers participate will in the long run largely be determined by the atti-
tude of the health workers toward them. A third requisite for the success
of the program is adequate supplies of contraceptives and drugs. All these
matters require constant monitoring and supervision by someone close to the
scene. At present there is only one professional staff member in each dis-
trict health office (a senior sanitarian with the title of district health
officer), and under the present administrative system the district hospital,
where one exists, is only nominally responsible for the health and midwifery
centers in the district. To fill this critical gap in the 20 AFPH provinces,
the MOPH will shift an experienced junior sanitarian, nurse, or auxiliary
midwife to each district health office to become the deputy district health
officer and to be responsible for the supervision of the primary health
care program and for health surveillance in that district. All 199 districts
and subdistricts will be covered in the first year. The project will meet
the salaries and allowances for these 199 district supervisors, the costs of
1/   Each user requires four doses per year.



ANNEX 2
Page 18
their four-week training program (Annex 4), and the cost of a motorcycle
for each one. In addition, clerical support is being provided for district
health offices in the form of a clerk/typist and typewriter for each.
Provincial Supervisors for Primary Health Care
47.       To oversee the district supervisors (deputy district health
officers), and to ensure adequate support at the provincial level for the
primary health care program, a provincial supervisor will be appointed to
each of the provincial health offices in the 20 AFPH provinces. These pro-
vincial supervisors will be recruited from among senior sanitarians, public
health nurses, and other qualified personnel. They will take the same four-
week training program as their district counterparts (Annex 4), and the
project will similarly cover their salaries, allowances, and transportation
(two-wheel drive vehicles). Besides supervising the deputy district health
officers and ensuring that contraceptives, drugs, IEC materials, and other
supplies reach the districts, the provincial supervisors will represent the
primary health care program in provincial health planning and similar exer-
cises, and in relations with other government and non-government agencies.
They will work closely with other officials in the provincial health office,
especially the provincial public health nurse supervisor.
Transport for Provincial-Level Family Planning Supervisors
48.       The midwives and nurses who now deliver family planning services
(except sterilizations) are supervised by the public health nurse supervisor
in the health promotion section of the provincial health office, who also
plays a key role in in-service family planning training. For instance, if
a new contraceptive method is introduced, she will first participate in a
special "training for trainers" program; then she will organize training
programs for nurses and auxiliary midwives within her own province; and
finally, she will follow-up to ensure that field staff are following accept-
able procedures. To discharge these and other responsibilities, the provin-
cial public health nurse supervisor requires her own transport.   The project
includes a two-wheel drive vehicle for each of the country's 71 provincial
public health nurse supervisors, i.e., for those in the 20 AFPH provinces as
well as the other 51 provinces.
Strengthening the Supervisory Roles of Headquarters Units
49.       Training and Supervision Section, Family Health Division:  Respon-
sibility at the national level for the supervision of family planning services
rests with the Training and Supervision Section of the Family Health Division
(Annex 4). Several units of the section, including the Supervision Unit, are
divided along regional lines. There are nine regions. Posts in the Super-
vision Unit corresponding to five of these regions are vacant at present, and
a sixth is filled by a staff member on loan from another section of MOPH. The
project includes the salaries and allowances for six nurses to fill these
posts, to be phased in at a rate of two per year during the three years of the



ANNEX 2
Page 19
project. These new posts will help to provide the technical supervision that
is becoming increasingly important as large numbers of new health workers are
trained.
50.       Rural Health Division:  The rapid expansion of rural health facili-
ties in the 20 AFPH provinces will also increase the burden on the Rural
Health Division of the MOPH. To assist this division to provide technical
supervision, the project includes four new posts in the Rural Health Division:
a doctor, a public health nurse, a senior sanitarian, and a secretary/typist.
The doctor would be head of the Rural Health Services Section and would be
responsible for administering the Division's part of the project and for the
technical evaluation of the health services provided by rural health facili-
ties. In both of these tasks, he would need to work closely with the Health
Planning Division. The public health nurse will be responsible for planning
the roles of nurses and midwives in the 20 AFPH provinces with special refer-
ence to family planning, MICH, and school health. The senior sanitarian would
have parallel functions in respect to sanitarians in the 20 AFPH provinces.
The project will also provide transport (two two-wheel drive vehicles) to
support field supervision in the 20 AFPH provinces by these and other Rural
Health Division staff.
J. Summary: Organizational and Administrative Arrangements for the
Delivery of Integrated Health and Family Planning Services
51.       The organizational structure and administrative arrangements for
delivering primary health care and medical care services, including family
planning, are depicted in Appendix 17. The focal point for the delivery
of these services is the district hospital, which provides medical care for
the district and technical supervision and logistical support for the rural
health centers and midwifery centers. The district hospital is headed by a
medical officer, who, as the sen:ior health professional in the district, is
responsible for the quality of health care provided throughout the district.
The medical officer is assisted in this capacity by a nurse practitioner, who
oversees the medical and health care provided at health centers and midwifery
centers. 1/ The nurse practitioner has two principal roles. She provides
medical treatment and performs minor operations at the district hospital, and
she supervises and provides in-service training to paramedical personnel based
at the midwifery and health centers. She visits each midwifery center and
health center at least once a month. The project provides for the in-service
curative training and management training of the nurse practitioner to enable
her to perform her expanded functions.
52.       Administration of public health services is the responsibility of
the district health officer, who has primary responsibility for the adminis-
tration of preventive and promotive services provided in the district. The
1/   In those districts with no hospital and hence no physician, the nurse
practitioner is the senior health professional and assumes some of
the medical officer's responsibilities.



ANNEX 2
Page 20
project will considerably strengthen the capability of the district health
office to deal with the new primary health care program by providing a deputy
district health officer to be responsible for the village health volunteers
and communicators. While the nurse practitioner will look after medical and
health care activities at the tambol level, the deputy district officer will
have similar responsibilities at the village level. The deputy district
health officer will oversee the training, supervision, and management of
the village health volunteers and communicators.
53.       This additional administrative and technical support is required
at each distinct level of health care in the district to meet the increased
demands for health services generated by the primary health care program and
by the improved quality of medical care provided at the district hospitals,
midwifery centers, and health centers.
Implementation Schedule
54.       An implementation schedule for the "service" components appears
in Appendix 18. Appendix 19 gives the construction costs for all of the
facilities described in this Annex.

THAILAN:D
LOCATION AND SITE SELECTION CRITERIA FOR RURAL
HEALTH FACILITIES IN THE 20 AFPH PROVINCES
1. The following criteria are to be app:Lied by the Ministry of Public Health
in locating the rural health facilities.
a. Midwifery Centers
The facility:
i. should be central:Ly located in a cluster of mubans
of not less than 2,000 population;
ii. should be located at least 4 km away from any other
rural health facility; and,
iii. if there is no health center elsewhere in the tambol,
the criteria for deciding location and site should be
the same as those of a health center so that the
midwifery center may be upgraded to a health center
in the near future.
b. Health Centers
The facility:
i.  should be centrally located in a tambol of not
less than 5,000 population where, at present, there
is no district hospital/medical and health center
or health center; and,
ii. should be located 8-15 km away from other health
centers of neighboring tambols (depending upon
communications).
c. District Hospitals, 10 beds
The facility:
i. should be located in a district of not less than
30,000 population where, at present, there is no
district hospital/medical and health center; and,
ii. should be located in the tambol where the district
office is situated.



ANNEX 2
Appendix 9
Page 2
d. District Hospitals, 30 beds
The facility:
i. should be located in a district of not less
than 50,000 population;
ii. should not be located less than 40 km away
from the provincial hospital or other district
hospitals; and,
iii. should be upgraded from an existing 10-bed
district hospital, which is well utilized.
2. The criteria for selecting sites of the rural health facilities are the
following:
a. Area requirements:
-Midwifery Center                               not less than 1 rai
-Health Center                                  not less than 1 1/2 rai
-District Hospital, 10 beds                     not less than 10 rai 1/
-District Hospital, 30 beds                     not less than 15 rai
b.  Strategic location:
The sites should, as far as possible, be centrally located
and accessible. Such locations are usually found near
markets, religious shrines, other government offices, etc.
The site should be such that the facility is directly
connected with a main road or street served by public
transport.
Water and electricity must be available at sites where
district hospitals are going to be established. In the
case of midwifery centers and health centers, the
availability of water and electricity is desirable but
not absolutely necessary if other criteria for location
and site selection are met.
1/ In the event that a 10-bed district hospital is to be upgraded to a 30-bed
hospital in the near future, the site should be not less than 15 rai.
