THAILAND
MANPOWER AVAILABILITY AND THE NEED FOR NEW TRAINING FACILITIES
A. Introduction
1.        To assist in preparing its sector plan for the Fourth Five-Year
Development Plan, the Ministry of Public Health (MOPH), with assistance from
IIHO, carried out a "Country Health Programming Exercise" in 1975. This Exer-
cise includes not only a plan for the establishment of new schools and for
the expansion of existing ones for the basic training of paramedical person-
nel; it also includes plans for curriculum revision for three key categories
of paramedical personnel (practical nurse/midwives, auxiliary midwives, and
junior sanitarians) to make each category somewhat less specialized and more
oriented to basic health. The resulting program for the expansion of train-
ing facilities for occupations working in family planning is well-justified.
A considerable armount of flexibi:Lity for varying training schedules and for
training different but related occupations exists to accommodate actual train-
ing requirements that are different from those forecast.
B. The Existing Situation
2.        Although MOPH has several departments and programs, the Provin-
cial Health Care program is much the largest, since it includes all provin-
cial hospitals and all rural hea]Lth facilities. Thus the program employs
high proportions of the main grades of health workers (Table 1). In addi-
tion, the Provincial Health Care Program provides over 95% of all government
family planning service points. Consequently the availability of personnEl
for the Provincial Health Care Program determines the availability of per-
sonnel for the MIOPH's family planning services.

3.        As of September 1976, shortages existed in four of the five main

misleading, since the "Practical Nurse/Midwife" and "Auxiliary MLidwife"
categories are very close and are likely to be merged, which would leave
an overall shortage of 2,555 or 19%.

C. Projection of Health Manpower Requirements
for the Fourth Five-Year Plan Period
4.        Health manpower requirements have been estimated on the basis of:
(a) the number of health facilities by type, (b) the staffing pattern for
each type of facility, and (c) the annual attrition rate for each category.
During project preparation, the mission assisted the MOPH in reviewing
alternative expansion targets and staffing norms. The MOPH subsequently
fixed the 1981 targets for the provincial health care facilities shown in
Table 3.

5.        Annual attrition rates have been estimated by the MOPH as 2% for
physicians and junior sanitarians; 5% for auxiliary midwives; 5% for nurse
practitioners and practical nurse/midwives; and 10% for nurses. Projected
availability of additional staff to meet manpower needs due to expansions
and dropouts are shown in AppendiLces 1 and 2. However, current projected
output of the training institutions will not fully meet MOPH's requirement,
as shown in Appendix 3. As a result, continuing but less pressing short-
ages will occur as shown in Appendix 4. Since the likelihood that the HIOPH
will succeed in increasing its service facilities as rapidly as planned is
small, the projected 1981 manpower shortages will probably be a less serious
problem than they now appear to be (Appendix 5). In fact, in recognition of
the manpower constraint, the MOPE has already adjusted downwards the number
of service facilities it expects to construct by 1981 (Annex 2). The effi-
cient distribution of new staff as they are produced will be essential for
achieving optimum program effectiveness, especially for the Provincial Health
Care Program.
D. Availability of Key Health Personnel
During the Current Five-Year Plan Period
6.        Physicians:  It is expected that there will be about 2,400 new
medical graduates during the plan. period, of whom the MOPH hopes to recruit
over 1,800.   The mission believes, however, that the MOPH will be able to
recruit only about 1,200 of the hoped for 1,800 (Appendix 3). The Provin-
cial Health Care Program will require about 1,000 of these 1,200 additional
physicians.



ANNEX 3
Page 4
7.        Nurses:  The country's 23 nursing schools produce about 1,600
nurses annually, 500 from the MOPH's seven nursing colleges and 1,100 from
schools run by other organizations. The MOPH plans to establish three new
nursing colleges during the plan period, and to expand the seven nursing
colleges so that each produces 100 nurses annually. In addition, the MOPH
plans to provide fellowships for about 600 nursing students in non-MOPH
nursing schools which will assure that they will be recruited by the
Ministry. Altogether, about 4,800 additional nurses should be available
to the MOPH during the plan period, compared with a total requirement of
about 6,300. There will thus be a shortage of about 1,500 nurses. Over
80% of this shortage will arise from the upgrading of nurses to nurse
practitioners (see para. 11 below). Since most of these nurse practi-
tioners will carry out higher order nursing duties, the shortages will
not be as severe as the numbers indicate and the long-term impact on
health status of the rural population they serve can be expected to be
a positive one. Again, the allocation among the various health programs
of the MOPH will be an important factor in determining whether the needs
of the Provincial Health Care Program will be met. The Provincial Health
Care Program will need at least 3,800 additional nurses during the period,
about 80% of the total number available. An effort should be made to reduce
the requirements for nurses in other programs of the Ministry.
8.        Practical Nurse/Midwives:  There are 23 training institutions
producing about 1,300 practical nurse/midwives or practical nurses a year.
Fifteen of these training institutions are run by the MOPH--seven regular
practical nurse/midwife schools, three specialized practical nurse schools,
and five that form part of the Ministry's seven nursing colleges. The three
specialized practical nursing schools produce about 190 graduates annually
for special disease hospitals. The remaining 12 schools produce about 680
practical nurse/midwives to meet the needs of the Provincial Health Care Pro-
gram. Because of the shortage of practical nurse/midwives, many provincial
hospitals have trained nurse's aides to serve as temporary substitutes for
nurses, and the MOPH has organized a "crash" training program to upgrade
nurse's aides to practical nurse/midwives. Between 1976 to 1978, 4,000
additional practical nurse/midwives will be trained through this crash
training program. Two nursing colleges are starting to produce practical
nurse/midwives in 1977, with an annual output of 30 each in the first two
years and 50 each from the third year. In addition to this, one new school
already established will start producing 50 graduates a year beginning in
1979. The MOPH also plans to establish three new schools during the Fourth
Five-Year Plan Period, each with an annual intake of 70 students. The annual
output should increase by about 300 by 1981. If these plans go well, MOPH
should be able to recruit nearly 9,000 additional practical nurse/midwives
(including 950 practical nurses for special disease hospitals) during the
Plan period (Appendix 3). These plans will ensure that nearly 90% of the
total requirements for the period are met.
9.        Auxiliary Midwives:  Thie country produces about 500 auxiliary
midwives a year from six schools. One school, run by a private hospital,
offers a two-year program and produces 50-60 a year. The remaining five

P ,months.  Students spend six months ir. school, six months in rural areas,
fou-r months wi,th district or provincial hospitals and two months at provin-

produce about 3,400 new junior sanitarians during the period (Appendix 3).
This willl give an apparent surplus of 300 junior sanitarians which will
all-ow the M-inistry to promote some of the experienced existing junior san:i-

ll.         urse Practitioner:  In order to overcome the shortage of physi--
cians in the MOPH's health delivery system, the MOPH has organized a ten-
mmonth training program to upgrade experienced nurses to "nurse practition--
ers."  This new group will provide substantial medical care in the Provin--
cial Health Care Program at provincial and district hospitals.  The Schoo:L
of Public Health, Mahidol University, provides 25 weeks of institutional
training in public health and medical care, and the MOPEI, 14 weeks of fie'd
training. The original plan was to produce 1,400 (280 a year) nurse prac--
titioners during the Five-Year Plan period, but the start of the program was
delayed, and the first group of 136 trainees was not admitted until miid-1977.
A revised plan was subsequently formulated and beginning in May 1978 and
annually thereafter, two groups of about 150 trainees each will be admitted
to the program.  One group will i-ollow the ten-month course as originally
poa-r-ned, but the second group, which will consist of nurses who have already
^ad pub]ic health nurse training,, will have about four m.lonths training in
medical care only,
E. Proiect Support of Basic Training
12.       To help to expand the supply of paramedical personnel for family
planning services, the project will construct all of the planned new para-
medical training institutions and will expand six existing institutions,
as f o 1 lows :
a. the construction and equipping of:
three practical nurse/midwifery schools,
- one midwifery school with MICH center;



ANNEX 3
Page 6
b. the expansion and equipping of:
- one nursing school,
- two existing midwifery schols with MCH centers,
- two existing midwifery schools, and
c. the provision of vehicles for these schools.
The location of these facilities is given in Appendix 6 and on the map (no.
12792R) at the end of the report.
13.       Practical Nurse/Hidwifery Schools:  The project will establish three
new schools for practical nurse/midwives in Nakhon Si Thammarat, Phayao (Chiang
Rai Province), and Phra Phutthabat (Saraburi Province). Each school will offer
an 18-month training program, and requires 225 student places to accommodate
75 students every six months. These new schools will produce 450 graduates per
year. There will be no difficulty in providing sufficient delivery cases for
midwifery training at Nlakhon Si Thammarat if the existing number of hospital
beds (300) is doubled during the Fourth Plan period. Special training arrange-
ments can be made with a number of district hospitals nearby, even if the
implementation of the hospital expansion plan is delayed.
14.       Nursing School:  The project will help to expand a nursing school
at Lampang Provincial Hospital, with an annual output of 100 nurses. The
training period, which includes six months of midwifery training, is four
years. After the expansion, the school will accommodate 400 students. The
300-bed Lampang Provincial Hospital will provide clinical training. A mid-
wifery school is already attached to the hospital. It is expected that the
Ministry will expand the number of hospital beds by 300 during the plan
period. This expansion will enable the hospital to handle more than 5,000
deliveries a year, enough to give each student at least 20 cases. The role of
staff nurses in family planning is vital not only for the direct contribution
in providing clinical contraceptive services (injectables and IUDs), but also
in the training and supervision of auxiliary nursing personnel such as prac-
tical nurse/midwives and auxiliary midwives, the key staff in the rural health
care and family planning service-delivery system. Each MOPH-sponsored nursing
graduate will be obliged to serve in MIOPH facilities for four years.
15.       Midwifery Schools and MCH Centers:  With one exception (Lampang),
midwifery schools are closely linked with regional maternal and child health
(MICH) centers, which basically are maternity hospitals that provide the nec-
essary clinical training. In addition to the basic training of auxiliary
midwives, the MICH centers provide in-service training for health staff in
MCH and family planning. There are at present four of these centers, at
Khon Kaen, Chiang Mai, Ratchaburi and Yala. Each complex consists of a
midwifery school, a maternity hospital, a health promotion center, and an
administrative section. The project will establish a new midwifery school



ANNEX 3
Page 7
with 225 student places and a new MCH center with 120 beds in Nakhon Sawan
province, and will also expand four existing midwifery schools by adding 75
student places to each (in Chiang Miai, Khon Kaen, Lampang, and Ratchaburi).
Sixty beds will be added to the existing MCH centers at two of these schools
(Khon Kaen and Ratchaburi). These facilities will increase the annual output
of auxiliary midwives by 350 (150 from the new school and 200 from the four
expansions). The training program is for 18 months. In Thailand auxiliary
midwives are trained as public health midwives and carry out MCH and family
planning services at rural health facilities without immediate supervision
from a physician or a nurse. Besides MCH care, they are taught how to screen
for contra-indications for pills, and will soon be trained to insert IUDs and
to provide injectable contraceptives.
16.       Vehicles:  Two 50-seat buses and one micro-bus will be provided for
the nursing school, and two micro-buses will be provided for each of the new
practical nurse/midwifery schools and the new midwifery school. In addition,
one micro-bus will be provided for each of the five existing nursing colLeges.
These buses will be used mainly to take students to field practice areas.,
F. Improvements in Staffing and Scheduling
in Basic Training Facilities
17.       The availability of trained tutors is essential to the expansion
of all three types of basic training institutions. The situation is summa-
rized in Appendix 7. As of June 1976, there were 367 tutors in the three
sets of institutions, giving an average tutor/student ratio of 1:13. If
the IIOPH succeeds in carrying out its expanded tutor training program as
scheduled, more than 400 additional tutors will be supplied during the Plan
period. Even though the student population will increase, the tutor/stu-
dent ratio should rise to 1:10 by 1981. This is reasonable, although far
from MOPH's long-term goal of a 1:4 ratio (Appendix 7).
18.       During project preparation, the mission noted that if the prac-
tical nurse/midwifery schools could change the student intake from once a
year to twice a year, the MOPH would be able to increase annual output by
one-third. However, unless existing teaching staff time and clinical train-
ing materials are substantially increased the change might affect the qual-
ity of training. At present, most of the training institutions suffer from
a lack of accommodations and of teaching staff. In some cases, not enouglh
hospital deliveries are available. The mission therefore did not press this
point further with respect to existing schools, but the MOPH has agreed to a
semi-annual intake for new schools financed through the project.
19.       At present, the basic curricula for auxiliary midwives and prac.-
tical nurse/midwives are similar except that the auxiliary midwife is given
more training in public health and maternal and child health, while the
practical nurse/midwife is given more ward practice (Appendix 8).  In both



ANNEX 3
Page 8
programs family planning is taught as part of maternal and child health.
It is recommended that in the revision of the curricula, the time given to
clinical training in family planning be increased.
20.       The number and types of key health personnel that will be avail-
able for recruitment by the MOPH during the Fifth Five-Year Plan period
are shown in Appendix 9. Sufficient physicians and nurses to reach the
staff level required by 1981 will not be available until 1985. This means
that the further expansion of hospital services should be slowed until near
the end of the Fifth Five-Year Plan period (1985) when these shortages should
end. There will be some, although less serious, deficit of practical nurse/
midwives, unless the MOPH introduces semi-annual intakes into all training
schools for practical nurse/midwives as early as possible and maintains the
18-month training program throughout the Fifth Five-Year Plan period. Suf-
ficient auxiliary midwives will be available during the plan period to per-
mit the establishment of new midwifery centers and/or the improved staffing
of health centers by assigning a second auxiliary midwife to each of the ex-
isting health centers as a third member of the staff. There will be enough
junior sanitarians to permit the upgrading of many midwifery centers to
health centers and the promotion of experienced junior sanitarians to senior
sanitarians.
G. Conclusions
21.       The shortage of key health staff will not be substantially eased
until the Fifth Five-Year Plan Period. The following are recommended ways
of easing MOPH manpower shortages:
a.   The MOPH, the Budget Bureau, and the authorities responsible
for the non-MOPH nursing schools should try to ensure that
the 2IOPH will have the funds needed to sponsor and thus to
recruit 30 to 50% of the annual graduates from the non-MOPH
nursing schools;
b.   To overcome the shortage of nurses and practical nurse/
midwives, some of the provincial hospitals should continue
to employ "nurse's aides" where they are more needed;
c.   Consolidation of the practical nurse/midwife and auxiliary
midwife categories into a single multipurpose auxiliary
nurse/midwife category would simplify manpower planning,
training, assignment to posts, and on-the-job utilization
of these key paramedicals;
d.   About one-third of the 4,000 practical nurse/midwives pro-
duced by the 1976-78 crash training program should be allo-
cated to the 20 AFPH provinces for assignment as third staff
members at health centers;



ANNEX 3
Page 9
e.   The causes of the present high attrition rate (10%) of nurses
should be investigated in order to see what can be done to
reduce it;
f.   During the Fifth Five-Year Plan period, improvement of staffing
at existing facilities should have priority over the expansion
of facilities;
g.   The Ministry's expansion plans should be periodically reviewed
to assure balanced staffing for all programs.
H. Implementation Schedule
22.       The implementation schedule for the basic training activities
directly supported by the project--the construction and equipping of train-
ing institutions and allied clinical facilities (MCH centers)--is given in
Appendix 10. Summarized cost estimates for each type of training facility
appear in Appendix 11.

THAILAND
SHORT-TERM TRAINING IN SUPPORT OF FAMILY PLANNING AND PRIMARY HEALTH CARE
1.        The concept of short-term training, as used in this report,
refers to training courses and seminars ranging in length from a week or
less to several months. It includes short basic or pre-service training
for junior auxiliaries, such as assistant health workers and child nutri-
tion center attendants, but does not include the basic training of regular
paramedicals, which requires several years to complete (Annex 3).  It does,
however, encompass in-service training for upgrading the skills of these
paramedicals.  Included in the project is support for about twelve short-
term training programs, as well as some staff and facilities to support
selected elements of this short-term training.
A. Present Program of Short-Term Family Planning Training
Introduction
2.        To meet the substantial demand for training from both within and
outside the lMinistry of Public Health (MOPH), the National Family Planning
Program (NFPP) has: (a) organized a continuous program of in-service train-
ing to prepare various MOPII grades of staff for their new and changing roles
in delivering family planning services; (b) provided pre-service training
for certain auxiliaries for specialized family planning projects; and (c)
provided training for selected staff in other ministries and private organi-
zations. These training activities have been organized and/or conducted by
the Training and Supervision Section of the Family Health Division, which
is also responsible for supervision as a natural extension of the training
process. Altogether, from 1971 through 1976, the Section has trained nearly
22,000 health personnel from the MOPH and other ministries for family plan-
ning work (this figure includes some multiple counting).
3.        Initially, emphasis was placed on training the MOPH's own staff.  In
1968 in-service courses were initiated for doctors and nurses, and over 4,000
eventually were trained in contraceptive methods and other aspects of family
planning. In 1972 a new round of nationwide in-service training was begun to
provide more job-oriented training for field staff and to reach lower levels
of paramedical staff, especially auxiliary midwives and sanitarians. This
training program, which has been funded by the United Nations Fund for Popu-
lation Activities (UNFPA) and supported by technical assistance from the
United Nations Development Support Communication Service, i/ has stressed
client motivation and counseling and, for nurses and midwives, the clinical
skills required for offering oral contraceptives.
1/   Recently renamed the Asia and Pacific Programme for Development Train-
ing and Communication Planning.



ANNEX 4
Page 2
This job-oriented training program has a number of innovative
features and has become a model for many subsequent training programs.
It was initiated by giving nurses in the Training and Supervision Section
a solid grounding in training methodology and in the use of educational
media. They were then assigned to specific regions to establish regional
training teams consisting of senior nurses from the provincial health
offices. These regional training teams were trained in family planning;
information, education, and communication (IEC); training methods; and the
use of educational media. Provincial staff then adapted thie curricula to
meet the circumstances of their own provinces and initiated the training of
paramedicals responsible for family planning services and IEC in hospitals,
health centers, and midwifery centers. Courses for nurses and midwives
require 35 hours to complete; those for sanitarians, 21 hours. Stress has
been placed on training nurses and midwives, since their roles in family
planning are more comprehensive (both service and education) than that of
sanitarians (primarily education). Consequently, most of the country's
6,500 auxiliary midwives have already been trained, although as of early
1977 many of the junior sanitarians still need to be trained. Audiovisual
aids and other training devices have been used at all stages of this train-
ing. Audiovisual vans of the Family Ilealth Division's Public Relations and
Information Section support the training of auxiliaries and then follow
them into the field to assist with community meetings and film shows.
5.        As sDecific methods of contraception are proven acceptable and effec-
tive through pilot projects, training programs to introduce them throughout
the health care system are set up by the Training and Supervision Section.
Beginning in 1979, for instance, 240 auxiliary midwives are to undergo a ten-
week training course in IUD insertion each year. It is expected that the
injectable will be approved in mid-1978 for administration by auxiliary mid-
wives 'When it is, the Section will arrange a similar training program for
midwives on administration of the injectable. The maternal and child health
(MCH) centers and provincial and university hospitals play an important part
in clinical training.
Five-Year Short-Term Family Planning Training Plan, FY 1977-81
6.        A five-year plan has been prepared by the Training and Supervision
Section for the Fourth Development Plan period, FY 1977-81. MIost of the train-
ing covered by the plan is being funded by grants from UNFPA and the United
States Agency for International Development (USAID). Besides training for
MOPH's own auxiliaries, the plan includes the following: (a) in-service
family planning training for 530 members of Mobile Hill-tribe Welfare and
Development Teams (public welfare officers, health workers, and assistant
teachers); (b) in-service family planning training for nearly 1,100 staff
and personnel associated with the Public Welfare Department's Self-help
Land Settlements (superintendents of settlements, nurse assistants, and
special village health volunteers); (c) in-service family planning train-
ing for approximately 1,000 health workers (male auxiliary nurses) with
the Border Patrol Police; (d) multi-tiered family planning training pro-
grams for community workers (4,400 tambol doctors and 7,500 traditional



ANNEX 4
Page 3
midwives); (e) training in family life education for about 100 agriculture
officers; (f) training in family planning education in factories for about
120 factory managers; and (g) in-service training in population and family
planning for 60 junior military officers so that they, in turn, can instruct
recruits. The Section has set aside part of the UNFPA Grant to meet requests
for family planning training from other ministries and non-governmental or-
ganizations.
The Family Health Division's Training and Supervision Section
7.        The Training and Supervision Section has four units:  Technical
Training, Clinical Training, Supervision, and Administration (see Appendix
1 for Organization Chart). The Technical Training Unit designs curricula
and prepares training materials, the latter often in cooperation with the
Public Relations and Information Section of the Family Health Division.
The Technical Training Unit also works with provincial staff to set up
specific training schemes, such as those for auxiliaries described above.
The Clinical Training Unit, whose work is closely linked with seven clinical
training facilities around the country, helps to plan the clinical component
of training programs. The Supervision Unit works with provincial staff to
assure that service delivery standards are maintained and that staff members
in the field get help with family planning. A small administrative unit man-
ages the affairs of the Section.
B. Strengthening the Training and Supervision Section
8.        The Training and Supervision Section will, when fully staffed, have
40 staff members. Of these, 35 will be professionals (almost all nurses) and
five will comprise the support staff. As of early 1977, 12 of the 35 profes-
sional positions were unfilled. An additional six professional positions and
one support position were being filled by staff on loan from other divisions
of the Ministry. These 19 vacancies and seconded posts are fairly evenly
spread throughout the four units of the Section. Together they represent
nearly 50% of the total planned staffing for the Section.
9.        To bring the staffing of the Section up to the planned level will en-
tail the funding of 19 permanent posts, ten of which still need to be approved
by the Civil Service Commission. The project will provide salary support for
these posts, which will be phased in during the three years of the project.
Project support for new staff will enable the Section to rerLedy existing defi-
ciencies and to carry out its increasingly varied training responsibilities
within and outside of the HIOPH.



ANNEX 4
Page 4
C. Strengthening of the NFPP Training Center
10       Some of the training organized by the Training and Supervision
Section needs to be centralized because of its specialized nature. A
former United States Coast Guard station has been made available to the
HIOPM for this purpose, and designated the NFPP Training Center. Located
in Hanchat District of Lampang Province, the NFPP Training Center is readily
accessible by air, rail and road, from all parts of the country. Although the
Center has adequate training space, more accommodations for staff and trainees
are needed if large groups are to be handled. The Division's regular budget
for FY 1977 includes funds to remodel existing facilities to make training
space available for up to 120 trainees in groups of various sizes. The
Project will support the construction of: (a) dormitories for 100 trainees
(another 20 can be accommodated in existing facilities); and (b) housing for
10 permanent trainers. In addition, the project will provide two micro-buses
for the NFPP Training Center to meet arriving trainees and to take trainees
on field visits.
11.       Key training activities that have been scheduled as part of the cur-
rent five-year short-term training plan for family planning would take place
at the NFPP Training Center. This training program appears in Appendix 2.
Three key courses are involved: (a) basic family planning training for the
auxiliary nurses that serve under the Border Patrol Police; (b) courses in
family planning supervision and in new contraceptive methods that are to be
introduced into the health services for public health nurses and other senior
provincial staff; and (c) basic family planning and population courses for
junior military officers from all branches of the military to enable them to
give appropriate instruction to new recruits.  In addition, some of the UNFPA
training grant has been set aside for special training programs requested by
other ministries and non-governmental organizations. Much, if not all, of
this training would take place al the NFPP Training Center. Up to about 2,250
trainee-weeks of capacity is available each year for this type of training and
for the use of other divisions of the MOPH. When the NFPP Training Center is
not required for short-term training in family planning, it will be available
for short-term in-service training by other divisions of the M4OPH. First pri-
ority in releasing space, when it is not required for family planning training,
will go to basic health care (e.g., MCH and nutrition) and to rural health in
general. Some family planning content will typically be included in this basic
health training.
12.       The ten permanent family planning training staff to be assigned to
the NFPP Training Center will consist of two senior nurses, five middle-level
staff, and three junior staff.  This core training staff will be complemented
as necessary by temporary trainers. The 25 administrative and service staff
would include an administrator, an accountant, a clerk/typist, a maintenance
engineer, an electrician/audiovisual technician, cooks and kitchen helpers,
laundry workers, drivers, gardeners and guards.



ANN4EX 4
Page 5
D.  Short-Term Training Activities Supported Through the Project
13.       In order to upgrade the skills of existing staff or to train new
junior staff or volunteers in family planning and basic health care, a variety
of short-term training activities are included in the project. Eleven such
training programs are planned over the three-year project period: three at
the national level and eight in the 20 APPH provinces. The number of trainees
in each of these 11 programs is shiown in Appendix 3. An implementation sched-
ule for these training programs, along with the additional personnel described
above, appears in Appendix 4.
Short-Term Training at the National Level
14.       Niational Policy Makers and Opinion Leaders:  The Institute of Popu-
lation Studies at Chulalongkorn University will conduct a series of one and
two-week seminars for senior government officers, key people in the private
sector, and other national and provincial policy makers and opinion leaders.
The seminars will be held twice a year, each with about 30 participants.
Project funds will be transferred from the I4OPH to the Institute of Popula-
tion Studies.
15.       Assistant Health Workers (Maternal and Child Health/Family Planning):
This grade of health worker is being trained to assist in the operation of
MCH and family planning clinics, carry out IEC activities in clinics and
maternity wards, maintain family planning records, and, in some cases, visit
drop-outs, thus freeing more highly trained staff to do other work. They
will be assigned to provincial and district hospitals, provincial health
offices where family planning services are offered, and MCH centers. Four
hundred and fifty will be trained during the project period. Priority in
training will be given to the 181 who will be assigned to the 20 Accelerated
Family Planning and Hlealth (AFPH) provinces and whose salaries will also be
paid through the project. (The master budget - Annex 9 - distinguishes
between the training for assistant health workers in the 20 AFPH provinces
and those in the other 51 provinces, which is treated as a national-level
component.)  The assistant health workers will receive two weeks' classroom
training and two weeks' practical training. The former will be at the MCH
centers, which altogether will accommodate 15 groups of ten each per year,
and the latter will be on a one-to-one basis, typically under the tutelage of
a nurse in the hospital to which the assistant health worker will be assigned.
16.       Nurse Practitioners:  To overcome serious shortages in physicians
(Annex 3), the 140PH has embarked on a new program to upgrade the skills of
nurses to provide medical care in hospitals currently experiencing physician
shortages and in those districts in which the absence of a hospital precludes
physician care, these new personnel will supervise the overall public health
and medical care activities of auxiliary staff in rural health facilities,
such as health centers and midwiferv centers. This new category of health
worker, known as the "nurse practitioner," is being prepared through a
training program that was initiated in mid-1977. Nurses who already have
been trained in public health nursing will receive a concentrated 14-week



ANNEX 4
Page 6
course in medical care whereas those who do not have a background in public
health will participate in a ten-month program that includes both public
health and medical care. To help ensure that sufficient numbers of this
vital category of health personnel are available to rural health services
throughout the country, the project will support the training of 800 nurse
practitioners: 600 at the national level and 200 for the 20 AFPH provinces.
Priority will be given to the preparation of nurse practitioners for the 20
provinces and for the training institutions that will train auxiliary mid-
wives in medical care (para. 18). The project will support the allowances
for the 14-week field training in medical care, transport, training mate-
rials, and medical kits for each trainee. (The overall project budget--
Annex 9--distinguishes between training costs for the 600 nurse practi-
tioners at the national level and the 200 to be trained for the 20 AFPH
provinces.)
Short-Term Training in the 20 AFPH Provinces
17.       Assistant Health Workers for Midwifery Centers:  A total of 750
assistant health workers for midwifery centers will be trained within the
20 AFPH provinces in order to provide a second person to staff each of the
735 midwifery centers that will be functioning by the end of the project
period. 1/ Currently these facilities are staffed with one person, an auxil-
iary midwife, who finds it difficult to provide simultaneously adequate
clinical services at the midwifery center and extension services in the
community. To ease the burden on midwives until their centers are upgraded
to health centers (with one or two additional auxiliaries), assistant health
workers will be temporarily assigned to the midwifery centers. They will
be trained to perform routine tasks at the center and in the field in order
to free the midwife for other activities. Training will last six weeks, of
which two will be devoted to classroom training, and four to practical train-
ing. Ten groups of 25 each (i.e., 250) will be trained each year for three
years, using two of the MCH centers for classroom training; clinical training
will take place in smaller groups or individually in various hospitals and
rural health facilities.
18.       Curative Training for Paramedicals in Rural Health Facilities:  A
serious barrier to greater utilization of rural health facilities has been
the inability of the paramedicals staffing them to offer the kinds of curative
services demanded by the public. This problem is partly the result of legal
restrictions on the types of services that may be offered by paramedicals.
Some of these restrictions have recently been lifted. Although they may
already have been trained in some of the relevant skills, the paramedicals
staffing rural health facilities need refresher training to consolidate and
legitimize their existing skills and to develop additional curative skills.
Additional curative training is also being added to the basic training of
these cadres, and in the long run it is expected that the MOPH will staff
health centers with nurse practitioners. The first major step in the pro-
cess of increasing the medical care skills of auxiliary personnel will be
the development of a four-month course in curative care to be given to all
1/ Number trained allows for attrition.



ANNEX 4
Page 7
auxiliary midwives assigned to health centers and midwifery centers in the
20 AFPH provinces.  Priority is to be given to auxiliary midwives since
they already play a more important role than junior sanitarians (the other
major auxiliary staffing rural health facilities) in providing medical care.
In addition, auxiliary midwives have a crucial role in delivery of family
planning services, and improving their skill in medical care will increase
public acceptance of their advice and help in this and other areas of basic
health. The training program in curative care will consist of one month's
theoretical content and three months' practical training. Over the three-
year project period, 2,200 auxiliary midwives will undergo training in 88
groups of 25 trainees each. The project will fund training costs for individ-
ual trainees (stipends, travel, and training materials) as well as training
aids and equipment for each training center and basic medical kits for each
trainee's home institution. Fourteen training institutions will participate
in the program: three MCH centers and 11 nursing and practical nurse/mid-
wifery schools. Practical training will take place in provincial and dis-
trict hospitals. Nurse practitioners trained under the project will play
a key role in training the auxiliary midwives. The detailed curriculum is
being prepared by the MOPH in consultation with the staff of the Lampang
Project (in northern Thailand) in order to make use of the latter's experi-
ence in training and deploying auxiliaries with special training in medical
care. As soon as auxiliary midwives are permitted to insert IUDs (Annex 2),
IUD insertion training will be initiated under the UNFPA-funded family plan-
ning training grant with priority being given to midwives who have already
completed the four-month curative care training program.
19.       Provincial and District Supervisors of Primary Health Care
Activities: The MOPH, especially in the 20 AFPH provinces, is introducing
a primary health care program based on the use of village health volunteers
and health communicators (Annex 2). Most of the responsibility for the
execution of this new program will be placed on the staff of tambol health
facilities--junior sanitarians and auxiliary midwives--who will be super-
vised in this respect by a sanitarian, nurse, or midwife (deputy district
health officer) based at the 199 district health offices. A senior sani-
tarian, public health nurse, or other staff member with similar qualifica-
tions would be appointed provincial supervisor of primary health care at
each provincial health office. A four-week training program is planned
for the first year of the project to prepare the 219 supervisors for these
tasks. Altogether 225 provincial and district level supervisors will be
trained to allow for attrition. They will be trained in eight groups at the
four Regional Health Training Centers.
20.       Village Health Volunteer/Health Communicator Training Scheme:
The scheme for training the village health volunteers and health communi-
cators consists of several stages. The first is to train staff at the
provincial and district levels in the essentials of the program with con-
siderable emphasis on training techniques. Five staff members from each
provincial health office and one from each district health office, includ-
ing the supervisors described in the previous paragraph, will participate



ANNEX 4
Page 8
in this "train the trainer" course of one week's duration. One auxiliary
from each district will also participate at this stage in order to get a
head start in launching the program in his or her tambol. This training
will take place throughout the country in three annual waves beginning in
1977. Four of the 20 AFPH provinces are included in the first annual wave,
so that this phase of the village health volunteer/health communicator train-
ing scheme will not be funded in those provinces.  All the remaining 16 prov-
inces will take part in 1978. About 280 staff will be trained in these 16
provinces.
21.       The second phase will be the training of junior sanitarians and
auxiliary midwives to organize the village health volunteer/health communi-
cator program in their tambols. This training, given in one-week courses,
will take place in the provincial capitals during the same year as and dur-
ing the year subsequent to the "train the trainer" phase described above.
About 2,200 auxiliaries will undergo this training. They will come from
districts (142) in which the program of Community-Based Family Planning
Services is not taking place (Annex 2).
22.       Each trained junior sanitarian and auxiliary midwife in the 142
districts will be responsible for organizing the village health volunteer/
health communicator program in nearby villages (mubans). This will entail
establishing a special village committee (or working with an existing com-
mittee); helping in the selection of about ten health communicators, one of
whom is, in turn elected village health volunteer; and then carrying out
the training of these volunteers. The village health volunteer will be
responsible for providing a limit-ed range of basic medical care (simple
diagnosis and dispensing of basic drugs), first aid, family planning, and
basic health education (Annex 2). Although the curriculum to be covered
would take about 15 six-hour days, arrangements will be worked out on an
ad hoc basis between the village health volunteer and his/her "trainer" to
suit the convenience of both.  Modest training materials will be provided
to each facility for this purpose. Each trainee will be given a manual and
a first aid kit during the training period.  It is estimated that as a result
of the project about 7,900 village health volunteers will be recruited and
trained in the 142 districts.
23.       An average of about ten health communicators will be selected in
each village to perform basic health education and referral tasks in close
collaboration with the village health volunteer, who serves as "chairman"
of the health communicators. The health communicators will receive an
abbreviated version of the training given to village health volunteers,
with much of the emphasis being placed on IEC techniques and on basic con-
tent in family planning, MICH, and other topics in basic health. In addi-
tion, then, to training village health volunteers, each auxiliary will train
health communicators. The total time required to cover the health communi-
cator curriculum is five days (30 hours). As in the case of the village
health volunteer training, however, both the training venue and hours will
be arranged to suit the convenience of the participants and their "trainer".



ANNEX 4
Page 9
During the course of the project, the junior sanitarians and auxiliary mid-
wives assigned to health centers and midwifery centers in the 142 districts
where this program will operate will be responsible for training about 80,000
health communicators.
24.       Management Training for Provincial Health Officials:  To increase
the management competence of senior and middle-level health administrators
in the 20 AFPH provinces, funds are included in the project to support man-
agement courses for various categories of MIOPH officials at provincial and
district levels. One set of courses will bring the two top administrators
for each province together--the provincial chief medical officer and the
deputy provincial chief medical officer, who serves as head of the Health
Technical Services Office--the first and then again in the second year of
the project. The purpose of these courses is to ensure that these 40 key
administrators develop the skills needed to manage the project and other
aspects of the increasingly more complex provincial health program.
25.       Management Training for Middle-Level Health Administrators:  To
improve health planning and public health administration at the middle
levels in the 20 AFPH provinces, special training will be organized for
the 20 chiefs of the Health Education, Training, and Supportive Service
Sections and the 20 chiefs of the Health Promotion Sections in the provin-
cial health offices (once during the project), for the 20 chiefs of the
Planning and Evaluation Sections in the provincial health offices (twice
during the project), and for the 199 district health officers (once during
the project).
26.       Management Training for Hospital Administrators:  Mlanagement train-
ing will also be offered to the approximately 140 directors of provincial and
district hospitals in the 20 AFPH provinces. This one-week program will be
held the first year of the project. It will stress the changing role of hos-
pitals in support of lower level facilities and of primary health care activ-
ities, as well as improved management of the hospital itself. The approxi-
mately 115 chiefs of Promotion Sections in the district hospitals will also
receive one week's training in management through the project.
27.       Child Nutrition Center Attendants:  Provision has been made in
the project budget to support the construction of up to 120 child nutrition
centers in the 20 AFPH provinces (Annex 2). When a community decides to
construct a child nutrition center, one or two local women (depending upon
the number of children expected) are selected to be attendants and are sent
to the provincial capital for two weeks' training. This training must be
conducted on an ad hoc basis, since there will only be about three trainees
per province per year. Provision is made in the project budget for training
up to 180 child nutrition center attendants.

THAILAND
INFORIIATION, EDUCATION AND COMMUNICATION (IEC)
IN SUPPORT OF FAMIILY PLANNING AND PRIMARY HEALTH CARE
A. Introduction
lo        Information, education and communication (IEC) activities in support
of family planning and basic health are carried out by the Ministry of Public
Health (MOPE) and, to a limited extent, by other agencies, both governmental
and private. The IEC component of the project seeks to strengthen some of the
weak areas in present IEC activities. Among other things, the project will
support the production of additional materials on family planning, population
and nutrition for use in interpersonal communication and the mass media,
provide production and utilization equipment, and strengthen IEC staff at
headquarters and in the field. This component is closely linked with the
service component of the project, since the personnel who provide family
planning and basic health services also carry out IEC activities in the
field.
B. Present IEC Situation
IEC Activities in the M4OPH
2.        The MOPE's current IEC policy emphasizes face-to-face communication
in family planning clinics, hospiltal maternity wards, rural health centers,
and through the home visiting and other extension activities of paramedical
staff (especially auxiliary midwives). Various materials have been prepared
in support of these activities, including posters, leaflets, booklets and
films. Some small scale complementary mass media efforts have been under-
taken.
3.        IEC work in support of ifamily planning has so far been largely aimed
at informing married couples in rural areas about contraceptive methods.
XThile this approach is warranted as long as there is an unsatisfied demand for
contraceptive services, in time the MOPH will need to combine it with an
approach aimed at changing social norms and practices affecting childbearing,
family size, the role of women, and family life.  To assure that this and
other adjustments in the IEC strategy are promptly made, the work plan calls
for an annual review of IEC performance and needs.
4.        In 1974 the MOPH, with technical assistance from the UN Development
Support Communication Service and with financial support from UNFPA, prepared
a communication plan for family planning. Many elements in this plan have
not yet been implemented, so that it will continue to guide IEC activities for
several years to come although there will be nmodifications from two sources:



ANNEX 5
Page 2
the annual review of IEC performance and needs and the new inter-agency
consultative process that the MOPH is about to initiate, which will enable
other ministries and non-governmental organizations to indicate the kinds
of IEC support materials or activities they would like the MOPH to provide
on their behalf or jointly.
5.        Most of the Ministry's 6,500 auxiliary midwives have undergone
special in-service training in family planning, including IEC. Junior sani-
tarians are gradually being trained. After a midwife has been trained, the
audiovisual van used in the training program visits her area for a day of
meetings with village leaders and with women. In the evening everyone is
invited to a showing of family planning motivational and information films.
In late 1976 the Ministry began distributing a special "motivational" kit to
family planning clinics, health centers, and midwifery centers. Among other
things, the kit contains a flip chart, a pelvic model, wallcharts, sample
contraceptives, and samples of the family planning leaflets and posters
available for distribution. To help field staff and other interested persons
keep informed about the program and developments in family planning, they
receive a quarterly magazine from headquarters. IEC activities in nutrition
and MCII have been limited almost exclusively to face-to-face communication by
MOPH field staff, supported by booklets and leaflets.
6.        To date, only limited use has been made of the mass media in
support of family planning. A half-hour weekly radio program, consisting of
popular music interspersed with information about family planning is being
carried by about 30 stations. Since mid-1976 radio spots have been aired at
least twice daily on about a dozen stations, a new spot being prepared every
two months. A few slides have been prepared for TV. Six films were ready for
distribution by the end of 1976, including one for policy-makers, an informa-
tional film on family planning methods, and three separate motivational films
for different regions. (The sixth film was for training purposes.)
7.        At least five units of the MOPH are concerned with various aspects
of IEC in support of family planning, maternal and child health (MCH), nutri-
tion and basic health. For family planning IEC, primary responsibility rests
with the Public Relations and Information Section of the MOPH's Family Health
Division. Training in family planning IEC for field staff is carried out by
the Training and Supervision Section of this Division. A third section of
the Division, the MCH Planning and Program Implementation Section, is respon-
sible for IEC in support of MCH. Nutrition IEC is under the Nutrition Educa-
tion Section of the Nutrition Division. Although all these sections have been
using commercial resources for the production of some of their IEC support
materials, some of their production needs have been met by the Health Educa-
tion Division. For an organization chart showing the relationship between
these divisions and other parts of the MOPH, see Annex 7. All these units
confine themselves mainly to the framing of IEC strategy, the production of
materials, and the support of in-service training. IEC is largely carried out
by the various grades of paramedicals in connection with their general duties.
There is no staff specializing in IEC at the provincial or district level.



ANNEX 5
Page 3
8.        The project is providing direct assistance to three of the units
mentioned above: the Public Relations and Information Section of the Family
Ilealth Division, the Nutrition Education Section, and the Health Education
Division. (Support for the Training and Supervision Section is also included;
see Annexes 2 and 4,)
9.        Public Relations and Information Section:  This section has three
operational units:  Mobile Infornation, which is responsible for ten audio-
visual vans; Production, which produces and distributes training and IEC
support materials; and Mass Media, which designs and distributes materials
for the mass media. As of November 1977, the section had 21 professional
and technical staff members and 35 support staff. An Organization Chart
showing the staffing of each unit and other details appears in Appendix 1.
10.       Nutrition Education Section: This section, which has six pro-
fessional staff members, is one of seven that constitute the Nutrition
Division.  In addition to the preparation of IEC materials and the setting
up of exhibitions, the section is responsible for in-service training in
nutrition.
II.       Health Education Division and its Role in IEC Coordination:  The
Health Education Division is generally responsible for public health educa-
tion. Certain MOPH Divisions, such as Family Health and Nutrition, have
their own health education units. Liaison between these units and the Health
Education Division has been very uneven in the past, and since this Division's
production equipment has been substandard, those MOPH divisions that could
afford to do so have largely or exclusively relied on commercial printers and
production units. A new national health education program, approved in 1976,
includes the establishment of a sub-committee for public health education,
which will serve to bring together various divisions in the MOPH, including
those receiving IEC support through the project. For an organization chart
showing the different sections of the Health Education Division and their
staffing, see Appendix 2.
Other Public and Private Agencies Interested in IEC
12.       Other ministries interested in population and family planning
usually include information on family planning in their adult education and
extension activities, especially those directed towards women. Some use is
made of MOPH materials. In July 1976 the Ministry of Education established
a Population Education Unit to undertake both in-school and out-of-school
population education. In the latiter area, for example, the unit has prepared
a series of wall newspapers 1/ on population themes for posting in village
1/   A wall newspaper is similar to a poster, but it has more written content
and is intended to be replaced when a new "issue" comes out, often at
fixed intervals, such as monthly.



ANNEX 5
Page 4
reading centers. The Labor Training Center in the Ministry of Interior has
been holding several courses a year on population for industrial workers.
The Department of Community Development, which is also in the Ministry of
Interior, gives various courses in health and family planning at the village
level. The Ministry of Agriculture's extension program, especially for
women's clubs, includes education in family planning, nutrition, and other
aspects of basic health.
13.       In the private sector, Community-Based Family Planning Services,
besides distributing MOPH materials, has been producing its own wall news-
papers for posting by its distributors and renting audiovisual vans to show
entertainment films; between reels a staff member introduces the local dis-
tributors and describes their services. The Planned Parenthood Association
of Thailand has been working closely with various branches of the Ministry
of the Interior, especially in workers' population education and programs
for youth. The Association also occasionally uses radio and TV.
14.       The Institute of Population Studies at Chulalongkorn University,
founded in 1966, is an autonomous research and training institute of the
University. Besides conducting some of the evaluation and research projects
supported by the project (Annex 6), the Institute will carry out some IEC
activity. The Institute seeks to promote public and official interest in
population, including "beyond family planning" measures. Project funds will
enable the Institute to systematize and strengthen its work in this area.
C. Project Strategy: Identification and Systematic
Strengthening of Weak Areas in IEC
15.       Objectives of the Family Planning IEC Program:  The objective of the
family planning IEC program is to help the national family planning program
reach its target of recruiting three million acceptors during the current
Five-Year Plan period and maintaining a clientele of more than two million
continuing users at the end of the period. It is estimated that by 1981
Thailand will have about nine million couples of reproductive age who will
need to be instructed about the benefits available to them, their children,
and their communities in conjunction with limiting births. Special emphasis
will be placed during this period on improving continuation rates, shifting
acceptors to more reliable and more permanent methods, reaching further into
rural areas not yet reached by the program, and working more closely with
other public and private agencies.
16.       Numerical Targets for IEC:  W4hile there is, as yet, no methodology
for extrapolating from the number of family planning acceptors required to
the number of couples (or women) who need to be reached through a program's
IEC arm, in the Thai case several things are obvious: (a) the number of
couples that can be reached with the help of present funding is totally in-
adequate (e.g., the current UNFPA Grant will permit the production each year



ANNEX 5
Page 5
of about 200,000 copies of one new leaflet and about 100,000 copies of one
new poster); and (b) the number of couples that can be reached with the
additional resources provided through the project is limited by MOPH's
absorptive capacity.
17.       Key IEG Problems and Steps to be Taken to Solve Them:  The IEC
components of the project are designed to build on the strong points of the
IEC program and to overcome as many of the weaknesses as possible. Table 1
indicates the problems that have been identified and the activities to be
taken to overcome them. (The paragraph numbers given in the table indicate
the paragraphs of this Annex in which more details may be found; in a few
cases reference is also made to other Annexes.)
D. Proposed Strengthening of IEC at the National Level
General
18.       Inputs from the project at the national level primarily consist
of support for: (a) the production activities of the Family Health and
Nutrition Divisions and the Institute of Population Studies; (b) production
equipment for the Health Education Division and the Family Health Division;
(c) vehicles for the Public Relations and the Information Section of the
Family Health Division; (d) audiovisual utilization equipment for prov-
inces outside the 20 AFPH provinces; and (e) incremental staff for the Family
Health Division and the Health Education Division and for the Institute of
Population Studies.
Expanded Production and Distribution of IEC Materials (MOPH)
19.       Production of Additional Copies of Existing Materials:  A serious
constraint on the IEC program is an insufficient quantity of materials for
use in the field. Hence an important element of the IEC component of the
project is the greatly expanded production and distribution of family plan-
ning IEC materials that have already been prepared and pretested. Additional
copies of existing nutrition IEG materials will also be produced through the
project.
20.       Production of New Materials:  In addition, the project will provide
the following new materials: (a) additional printed materials and teaching
aids on population/family planning to be developed in cooperation with other
ministries and non-governmental organizations for use in their own extension
programs; (b) 22,500 simple "motivation" kits for the use of village health
volunteers; (c) a daily half-hour dramatic program for radio; (d) 200 slide
sets; (e) 10,000 audio cassettes; (f) 20 sets of entertainment films to attract
crowds to IEG events; and (g) 800,000 copies of an illustrated catalogue list-
ing the available films, audiovisual aids, and printed materials to facilitate
