demonstration kits for mothers' clubs attached to the child nutrition centers
(Annex 2). Cost estimates for the production activities described in this
and the preceding paragraph are given in Appendix 3.
Equipment and Vehicles (MIOPH)
21.       Production Equipment:  To support the expanded production of
materials described above, various types of production equipment are required
at the national level, mainly for the Health Education Division's production
unit. Existing equipment, especially in the print shop and for filmmaking,
is obsolete. Besides a new offset press and associated equipment (e.g., an
automated camera), two sets of basic filmmaking equipment will be obtained.
During the three years of the project, up to 60% of the capacity of this new
production equipment will be reserved for family planning, MCH, and nutrition.
Besides films for IEC programs, the Health Education Division will also pro-
duce training films for the basic and in-service training programs supported



ANNEX 5
Page 9
through the project (Annexes 3 and 4). The IEC activities of the Family
Health Division include mass mailings (primarily the quarterly family planning
journal), but in the past distribution lhas been handicapped by an inadequate
address system. To overcome this problem, the Family Health Division will
procure an electric addressing machine.  The Division will also obtain an
audio cassette duplicator, which will be shared with other IEC units. Cost
estimates of required production equipment and associated supplies appear in
Annex 9.
22.       Vehicles:  The Public Relations and Information Section of the
Family Health pivision requires three two-wheel drive vehicles. One of these
would give access to mass media outlets in the Bangkok area, and the other
two would be used to enable field staff to pretest IEC materials.
23.       Utilization Equipment:  A 1976 survey by the Family Health Division
revealed that very few functioning projectors and other audiovisual utiliza-
tion equipment are available in the field. To overcome this problem, the
MOPH intends to provide each provincial health office and all provincial and
district hospitals with basic audiovisual equipment. To qualify for this
equipment, a provincial health office or a hospital must first permit at least
one staff member to take a one-month course on audiovisual equipment. Because
of the greater concentration of personnel with special IEC responsibilities
in the 20 AFPH provinces, additional utilization equipment will be supplied
to provincial health offices and hospitals in those provinces (see para. 34
below).
Incremental Staff: HOPH
24.       In order to increase their production of IEC materials, the Public
Relations and Information Section of the Family Health Division and the
Audiovisual Section of the Health Education Division require additional staff.
No additional staff are required by the Nutrition Education Section. The
phasing and costing for all IEC personnel who will receive salary support
through the project are given in Annex 9.
25.       Public Relations and Information Section, Family Health Division:
The Mobile Information Unit needs qualified staff to take charge of five
of the ten mobile units. To keep the mobile units going, staff recruited
for mass media work have had to do double duty. Project funds will support
this incremental staff, together with four other communicators--two each
in the Production and the Mass Media Units, as indicated in Appendix 1.
26.       Audiovisual Section, Health Education Division:  In order to make
effective use of the production equipment provided through the project,
the Health Education Division requires additional staff for its Audiovisual
Section. Altogether seven additional professional and technical posts are
to be funded through the project: an audiovisual officer, who will work with
other divisions in planning and designing audiovisual support materials for
training and IEC uses; an electronics technician, who will be responsible



ANNEX 5
Page 10
for the coordination of film production and production of audio cassettes;
two cameramen and two assistant cameramen; and a paper cutter. All of these
posts, except those of the audiovisual officer and of the electronic tech-
nician still need to be established through the Civil Service Commission or
as permanent, non-Civil Service Commission posts as indicated in Appendix 2.
Supportive Activities (MOPH)
27.       Technical Assistance:  No technical assistance in IEC is included
in the project. Short-term technical assistance in family planning IEC is
available through several UN agencies in conjunction with the UNFPA grant for
IEC. Likewise, the Health Education Division expects to receive support from
WHO for short-term local IEC consultants and the Nutrition Education Section
expects to receive some assistance from UNICEF.
28.       Research and Evaluation:  The current Grant from UNFPA also pro-
vides some funding for IEC-related research and evaluation. Since there
is no immediate demand for additional research in this area, no IEC-related
research and evaluation projects have been expressly included in the project,
although it is possible that some of the uncommitted research funds will.
need to be used for IEG-related research by the third year of the project.
In addition, one small-scale study is to be carried out by the Health Edu-
cation Division to evaluate the utilization and impact of audiovisual aids
and equipment provided through the project. For the details of this study,
see Annex 6.
29.       Population Awareness Program, Chulalongkorn University:  The
Institute of Population Studies, besides its research and teaching, is
committed to the promotion of public and official awareness of population
matters. To systematize its work in this area, which is basically IEC, the
Institute plans to launch the Population Awareness Program, which would
include, twice a year, one and two-week seminars on population policy issues
of current interest for senior government officials and other policy makers
and opinion leaders (Annex 4); the publication of an expanded version of
the quarterly Population Newsletter; the publication of research reports and
papers in Thai; and reference and copy services from the Institute's library.
Support will be provided to the Institute for a part-time publications and
information officer, clerical assistance, seminar costs for about 240 par-
ticipants, publication and dissemination of the Population Newsletter and
of various papers and research reports, library supplies, copy services,
and miscellaneous office supplies.
E. Proposed Strengthening of IEC in the 20 AFPH Provinces
General
30.       In the 20 AFPH provinces, the IEC component of the project will pro-
mote several developments that the MOPH is planning eventually to implement



ANNEX 5
Page 11
nationwide. Most important will be the appointment of a trained health edu-
cator to each provincial health office. The MOPH will also appoint Drovin-
cial and district supervisors whose duties will include IEC. In support of
family planning services at hospitals, provincial health offices, and else-
where, the IIOPH will appoint assistant health workers (MCH/family planning)
who will have some IEC responsibilities. To facilitate IEC activities, ad-
ditional audiovisual utilization equipment as well as funds for local produc-
tion will be provided. Through support of short-term training, incremental
salary support, and other inputs, the project will also greatly strengthen the
community level of IEC activities of MOPH field staff, village health volun-
teers, village health communicators, traditional midwives, and the extension
staff of other ministries and agencies. Appendix 4, which shows the size of
various audiences for family planning IEC in the 20 AFPH provinces, indicates
the magnitude of the IEC task in the field.
The Provincial and District Levels
31.       Provincial Health Educators:  Several weaknesses in the present
IEC situation as well as in the health education program can be attributed
to the absence of a trained, full-time IEC specialist at the provincial
level. It is, therefore, proposed that a provincial health educator be
assigned to each of the 20 AFPH provinces. Fifteen provinces are to be
covered in 1978 and the other five in 1979. Salary support and needed
transportation (a two-wheel drive vehicle for each) will be provided through
the project. The provincial hea'Lth educators will: (a) supervise the IEC
activities of MOPH hospital and field staff; (b) oversee the distribution
and utilization of IEC equipment and materials; (c) oversee the operation
of audiovisual vans; (d) prepare locally oriented IEC materials and events;
(e) provide IEC inputs in various training programs; and (f) cooperate in
IEC activities with other ministries. The provincial health educators will
be assigned to the Health Education, Training and Supportive Service Sec-
tions of the provincial health oi-fices.
32.       Provincial and District Supervisors of Primary Health Care: At the
provincial and district levels, the critical element in relation to the
primary health care program is that of supervision. The MOPH will appoint 20
provincial and 199 district supervisors for community-based health and family
planning in the 20 AFPH provinces, support for their salaries being included
in the project. Their job descriptions are given in Annex 2. They will have
important IEC responsibilities. The provincial supervisor will work closely
with the provincial health educat:or to ensure that community level activities
have appropriate IEC content. The district supervisors will ensure that vil-
lage health volunteers (para. 37), and health communicators (para. 38) receive
adequate training and supervision in IEC and that IEC materials reach them as
required.
33.       Assistant Health Workers (MCH/Family Planning):  To relieve nurses
and auxiliary midwives at hospitals from routine clinical and record-keeping
duties, the Family Health Division proposes to train and deploy 450 more



ANNEX 5
Page 12
assistant health workers (MCH/family planning) during the three years of the
project (Annex 2). In addition to providing clinical services, they will
assume responsibility for clinic education under the supervision of the nurse
or auxiliary midwife in charge of the clinic, and in larger district hospitals
and in provincial hospitals, they will play a key role in a comprehensive
postpartum educational program in family planning, MCH and nutrition. Those
posted to district hospitals, provincial health offices, and MCH centers are
expected to make home visits to discontinuing users. The IEC training for
this group will include the operation of the audiovisual equipment provided to
hospitals through the project.
34.       Additional Audiovisual Utilization Equipment:  As indicated above,
audiovisual utilization equipment will be provided for provincial health
offices and hospitals. In the 20 AFPH provinces, the 20 provincial health
offices, the 23 provincial hospitals, and the 40 larger (i.e., 30-bed) dis-
trict hospitals will also each receive an automatic slide projector with
rear projection screen for daytime viewing and a cassette tape recorder/
player with built-in synchronizer to operate the slide projector. The 81
smaller district hospitals (i.e., those with ten beds) will be given a simple
slide projector in addition to the basic audio system. Cost estimates of
equipment to be procured for use in the 20 AFPH provinces are given in
Annex 9.
35.       Local IEC Activities:  To encourage local IEC activities the proj-
ect budget includes small subventions to the 20 AFPH provinces, averaging
US$500 each annually. The Public Relations and Information Section of the
Family Health Division will be responsible for notifying provincial authori-
ties of the availability of these special funds, for judging the merits of
proposals and for providing technical advice on implementation. It is ex-
pected that provincial health educators will play a key role in using these
funds.
The Community Level
36.       IEC Responsibilities of MOPH Field Staff:  As shown in the chart
in Appendix 5, auxiliary midwives and junior sanitarians in health centers
and midwifery centers not only provide education within the clinic to family
planning acceptors or potential acceptors, but also carry out home visits and
work with various groups in the community. In the future, they will have
important responsibilities for the organization, training, and supervision of
the new primary health and family planning program (Annex 2). Many junior
sanitarians still require training for their role in family planning, which
is largely in the area of IEC. The 20 AFPH provinces will be given priority
in this respect. Besides their responsibilities for supervising village
health volunteers and health communicators, which they share with junior
sanitarians, auxiliary midwives have several special IEC functions: (a)
they supervise traditional midwives and tambol doctors (para. 39) and (b)
they organize mother's classes (and clubs) in conjunction with the child
nutrition centers. The project inputs that will directly strengthen the



ANNEX 5
Page 13
IEC activities of MOPH field staff are two-fold: (a) there will be more
IEC materials available and (b) there will be supervision and support from
the provincial health educators and provincial and district supervisors of
primary health care.
37,       Village Health Volunteers:  The responsibilities of village health
volunteers are described in Annex 2 and the extensive project-supported
training program for the 7,900 m.embers of this new grade in the 20 AFPH pro-
vinces is described in Annex 4. Village health volunteers will inform their
neighbors about family planning, MCH, nutrition, and communicable diseases,
and encourage them to use family planning and other health services. The
project will support the provision of a special family planning motivation
kit for the use of the village health volunteers. They will work closely with
local health center staff to organize local IEC events.
38.       Village Health Communicators:  Like the village health volunteers,
the health communicators in the 20 AFPH provinces will be trained with support
from the project (Annex 4). Unlike the village health volunteers, the health
communicators will not provide family planning supplies and basic drugs, and
their role will be almost entirely in the IEC area. Since there are to be
about ten health communicators for each village health volunteer, about 79,000
will be recruited and trained in the 20 AFPH provinces during the three years
of the project. Communicators are to be identified through sociometric pro-
cedures and interviews in the village by health center or midwifery center
staff. Where a village health committee or other suitable body exists, it,
along with the village health volunteer, would oversee the work of the health
communicators in their village and the local health staff would serve as
f'advisors." Otherwise supervision will be the responsibility of health center
and midwifery center staff serving that village. As with village health
volunteers, health communicators will informally inform their neighbors and
relatives about family planning, MCH, nutrition, important communicable
diseases, sanitation and other basic health topics. They will encourage
people to make use of the health services available in their area. To assist
health communicators with their IEC tasks, project funds will be used to
provide them with copies of simple materials on family planning and nutrition
for distribution. Health communicators will also promote participation in
local IEC events, such as film showings and mass immunization programs.
39.       Traditional Midwives and Tambol Doctors:  As described in Annex 4,
the MOPH is training traditional midwives (mohtamYae) and tambol doctors for
active roles in family planning. Besides supplying condoms and resupplying
oral contraceptives, they are to participate in !EC by: (a) advising their
"patients" on MCH and family planning; (b) correcting rumors and misunder-
standings about contraceptive methods and services; and (c) referring poten-
tial acceptors of clinical methods (oral contraceptives, IUDs, injections,
or sterilization) to the nearest source of services. Additional copies of
leaflets and other IEC materials will be prepared under the project to assist
the mohtamyae and tambol doctors.



ANNEX 5
Page 14
40.       Field Staff of Other Agencies:  The project will provide funds to
enable the MOPH to produce IEC materials for the extension education cadres
of other ministries and of non-governmental organizations. 2Iaterials would
be designed jointly. In addition, at the provincial level, funds available
for local IEC activities could be used to produce materials for the use of
staff of other agencies. To facilitate extension workers, teachers, and others
making use of the existing materials (audiovisual aids, films, etc.), funds
from the project will also be used to prepare, print, and distribute widely
a catalogue describing the available materials and how to obtain and use them.
41.       Implementation Schedule:  An implementation schedule for IEC activ-
ities supported through the project is given as Appendix 6.

THAILAND
EVALUATION AND RESEARCH (E&R) ACTIVITIES IN SUPPORT
OF FAMILY PLANNING AND PRIMARY HEALTH CARE
A. Present Situation
E&R Activities
1.        Foundations for formal academic research were laid in 1966 with
the creation of population research centers at two leading universities,
Chulalongkorn and Mahidol, the first concentrating on demography and the
second on public health. Out of the work of the Chulalongkorn Institute
of Population Studies came a series of national fertility and family plan-
ning studies that provide a most useful source of knowledge about trends in
vital rates, rising contraceptive usage, attitudes, and associated economic
variables. Chulalongkorn Institute's national longitudinal surveys, starting
in the late 1960s, showed trends in fertility and birth control that have been
the chief source of reassurance that fertility has indeed been falling. The
School of Public Health at Mahidol University and the Institute for Popula-
tion and Social Research, also at Mahidol, participate in a range of applied
studies, including the use of village volunteers, the placement of family
planning work within a rural development context, the diffusion of vasectomy
within the Thai culture, and more traditional topics such as use-effective-
ness determinations and service statistics improvement. Certain of these
projects, including an evaluation of field workers, have been conducted in
collaboration with the Ministry of Public Health (MOPH).  Important biomedi-
cal research has been done at the Chulalongkorn Medical School, as well as
at two medical schools connected with Mahidol University.
2.        Outside of Bangkok, the leading center is at Chiang Mai University.
Also in the Chiang Mai area is McCormack Hospital, the site of injectable con-
traceptive investigations. The Maternal and Child Health (MCH) Center at Khon
Kaen has been exceptionally active, and Songkhla University in the south has
increasingly taken part in social research projects in that region. The
National Statistical Office operates the census and vital statistics systems,
and conducts a continuous demographic survey covering the entire country. The
National Economic and Social Development Board focuses on the relation of pop-
ulation to socio-economic development.
3.        Although these institutions make important contributions, the core
of family planning evaluation and research in Thailand is the Evaluation and
Research (E&R) Section of the Family Health Division of the lIOPH. At pres-
ent, 30 staff within this Section process the national service statistics;
conduct the principal continuation surveys; and carry out directly or arrange
with outside agencies applied studies of contraceptive methods, special cate-
gories of personnel, and new delivery systems. This group also, more than
any other agency, coordinates the work of other agencies. The E&R Section



ANNEX 6
Page 2
is divided into three functional units (Appendix 1). The Research Unit de-
signs and executes studies. The Evaluation Unit handles the service statis-
tics system and interprets the results (paras. 42-49). The Reporting Unit
prepares and distributes documents. (The present program of research studies
by the E&R Unit is outlined in Appendix 2.) E&R activities are also carried
out by the Health Education Division and the Health Planning Division. (An
organizational chart for the latter appears as Appendix 3.)
4.        The E&R activities of the E&R Section began at the outset of the
National Family Planning Program (NFPP) in 1969, when an overriding question
was the extent to which paramedical personnel could safely distribute oral
contraceptives. The MOPH accordingly conducted a field experiment, utiliz-
ing experimental and control provinces, in which rural auxiliary midwives
screened candidate acceptors with a simple check list of contraindications,
and then resupplied the acceptors at their rural field stations. This
investigation found no increase in side effects or complications, and showed
a very large increase in acceptance rates and a continuation rate actually
higher when auxiliary midwives made the initial prescription than when
physicians did so.  This led to a MOPH ruling in 1970 that auxiliary mid-
wives, after a short training, could prescribe oral contraceptives. For
primary information on the functioning of the program, tIOPH evaluation
studies have been the key source. The MOPH conducted the client follow-up
surveys that established Thailand's exceptionally high continuation rates
for both oral contraceptives and the IUD, and it has run the national service
statistics system that showed the exceptionally favorable distribution of
acceptors as regards early ages and smaller family size. Thus operational
research has made significant contributions to the program from the earliest
beginnings, permitting NFPP administrators to test important program options
before implementing them.
6.        Nevertheless, results of the E&R Section have not been free from
shortcomings. Well trained professionals are in short supply and the most
able are severely overloaded. While individual staff members may work
chiefly in one part of the Section, he or she may participate in activities
in several of the units; clearly this has both advantages and disadvantages.
It gives the individual greater familiarity with the work of the entire sec-
tion, but it impairs the development of specialized skills, and it often
means that highly qualified staff perform relatively simple tasks.
7.        The creation of a field interviewing unit to which some staff
members would be permanently assigned has been proposed. This would have
the advantage of specialized training for interviewing and for interviewer
supervision.  However, at this stage such a step appears premature; such a
unit would probably have its own problems and might deteriorate in time.
8.        These staffing problems will be eased to some extent as young
graduates return to Thailand from foreign training. However, in the MOPH,
as well as in certain other ministries, career opportunities for researchers
are limited.



ANNEX 6
Page 3
9.        Notwithstanding the return of newly graduated Thai professionals
and the help of outside technical agencies, much remains to be done. The
program of assistance included in the project outlined below attempts to
support those studies that will give valuable new knowledge or will lead
to the improved application of present knowledge. A special effort has been
made to avoid undue increases in the numbers of projects carried by research
directors.  In most cases, research directors and the personnel under them
are spread thinly over many studies, and there exists a constant need to
balance demands for short-term productivity with the investment of time in
the in-service training of younger staff.
10.       Family Planning Evaluation and Research:  The National Family Plan-
ning Coordinating Committee has appointed a Family Planning Research and
Evaluation Sub-Committee, which stands as a useful resource for coordinating
family planning E&R. On a week-to-week basis, relationships with outside
agencies are handled by the Family Health Division, which contains the E&R
Section, and at a working level by the two or three most responsible staff
within the Section. Communication with most agencies outside the MOPH is
generally satisfactory, as is evident in the collaborative relations with
the National Economic and Social Development Board, National Statistical
Office, and groups in universities. There is room for closer collaboration
with other groups within the N4OPH; such collaboration will be encouraged by
the joining of health and family planning objectives in the project.
11.       Past Technical Assistance for Evaluation and Research:  The Rocke-
feller Foundation began an association with the Mahidol School of Public
Health in the mid 1960s which was later extended to population through the
participation of the Carolina Population Center. The Population Council has
supported with funds and resident personnel the work of the National Research
Council, the MOPH, Chulalongkorn Institute of Population Studies and the
National Economic and Social Development Board. Important advice on E&R has
also been given by the Ford Foundation regional office and the USAID popula-
tion section in Bangkok. Columbia University maintains a resident advisor
with the MOPH Research and Evaluation Unit. IHO resident staff concentrate
on the improvement of health statistics and management methods.
B. Strengthening Evaluation and Research Activities: National Level
12.       To assist in alleviating the dual constraints of lack of adequate
staff and lack of professional staff, personnel in two divisions will be
added under the project. Within the E&R Section, three policy and planning
analysts will be added, one each of the three years; two statisticians and
one administrative clerk and support staff will also be added. Within the
Health Planning Division, an operations researcher, a management special-
ist and a provinces coordinator will be added. These additional personnel
should enable the planned E&R activities to be carried out more effectively
and allow additional studies to be done which will further assist the na-
tional program effort.



ANNEX 6
Page 4
13.       The studies financed under the project at the national level sup-
port the ongoing E&R activities at the NFPP and the population research
activities at Chulalongkorn and Mahidol Universities.  A limited number of
important studies have been identified.  These studies are summarized below.
Appendix 4 provides a list of the studies, their estimated costs and the
names of the implementing agencies.
National Family Planning Progam
14.       Family Planning Client Follow-Up Survey:  In the three-year life of
the project it is realistic to think of at most three continuation surveys.
The MOPH plans to mix national surveys, done every two to three years, with
more frequent small scale regional surveys, which can concentrate on a par-
ticular contraceptive method of interest at the time. New methods can sud-
denly assume increased importance in the NFPP, e.g., female sterilization and
injectable contraceptives, which may soon be followed by vasectomy and even
by induced abortion..  Post-procedure interviews can be very important in es-
tablishing the quality of service received, and whether post-abortal contra-
ception was suggested, and taken up. The surveys will determine the average
length of use of a particular contraceptive method and the reasons for dis-
continuation. From this information more accurate estimates can be made of
the impact of the NFPP on continued protection from contraception and impact
on the birth rate.
15.       Injectable Contraceptive Program in Rural Areas:  If, as expected,
the MOPH decides to allow trained government midwives to provide contraceptive
injections, a considerable increase of supplies, not only of injections but
also needles, swabs and alcohol will be needed, with distribution complicated
by the fact that the midwives are stationed at some 3,500 rural sites. Early
and strict monitoring of this distribution process is particularly needed to
ensure continuity since high continuation rates among users of injectable
contraceptives is essential for program success. If continuation is high, the
accumulation of continuing users rises in a non-linear fashion. This budget
item allows for special field monitoring of the supply-flow system, stocking
capabilities of the service units, and central efficiency in meeting these
needs as well as early identification of any potential adverse effects of
Depo-Provera on the user population.
16,       Induced Abortion and Policv Implications:  Abortion is illegal
in Thailand except on grounds of rape or a serious threat to health. The
Buddhist injunction against the taking of life still carries weight in Thai
culture, but unsanctioned and unsafe procedures by unqualified practitioners
present a serious problem. Changes in the law are now under consideration
and form the subject of active debate. It is desirable to provide for semi-
nars/workshops to consider the medical and policy issues involved, as well
as to provide for service-related studies that would follow upon change in
the law. This would be required whether therapeutic abortion were incorpo-
rated in the official program or simply legitimized in the private sector.
Three studies are planned: (a) epidemiology of abortion; (b) attitudes



ANNEX 6
Page 5
towards abortion among the general population; and (c) dynamics of rural
abortion, post-liberalization.
17.       Evaluation Studies:  In addition to these three studies begun
in 1978, the NFPP will carry out some smaller studies in 1979 and 1980 to
improve the family planning services and program management. The terms of
reference for these applied studies will be developed in 1978, 1979 and
1980.
18.       Needed Advisory Assistance in Evaluation and Research:  A need
exists for short-term assistance for the NFPP client continuation surveys
(sampling process, design of questionnaires) to yield the most accurate
esti,ate possible of continuation measures.  Assuming three consultant
visits, each of one month's duration, the cost would be:
Travel:  $1,790 x 3                   $ 5,370
Per diem:  $50 x 30 x 3               $ 4,500
Fee:  $135 x 30 x 3        =          $12,150
Total                            $22,020
External Studies Carried Out by Mahidol and Chulalongkorn Universities
19.       Cost-Effectiveness of the National Family Planning Program:  It
is difficult to calculate the cost of the  WFPP in Thailand because it uses
the same facilities and personnel as the health infrastructure. Rough esti-
mates 1/ indicate that over US$1.5 million wqas spent on the NFPP during the
three-year period 1968-70, the annual amount rising from around $180,000 in
1968 to $860,000 in 1970; in addition the Thai Bureau provided $145,000 dur-
ing these three years as counterpart funds. W7hile these sums are substan-
tial, expenditures would have been greater had a separate family planning
organization been established. Finally, total foreign assistance from 1968-
71 approached US$3.5 million.
20.       In 1972 the Thai Government, for the first time, allocated budget
funds specifically for the NFPP, in the amount of $600,000; indirect costs
were estimated at about $1,000,000. Foreign assistance also increased, so
that in 1972 about $4,450,000 was spent from all sources.
21.       The total cost per acceptor in the Thai Program, estimated from
the total direct expenditures both Thai and foreign, together with the in-
direct budget costs, declined sharply from $17.91 in 1968 to $9.92 in 1972.
Cost per estimated woman-year of protection was $3.71 in 1968, $3.91 in
1/   The Population of Thailand:  A monograph prepared for the World Popu-
lation Year Series under the general direction of CICRED (Committee
for International Coordination of National Research in Demography),
Bangkok 1974, 66pp.



ANNEX 6
Page 6
1969 and $4.20 in 1970.  All these measures compare favorably with esti-
mates made with similar methodology for the Korea and Taiwan Programs in
their early years.
22.       The Institute of Population Studies at Chulalongkorn and the Insti-
tute for Population and Social Research at Mahidol University, have been work-
ing to refine the methodology and the results of calculations of this kind.
Because of their close relations with the MOPH and the National Economic and
Social Development Board, they are well placed to obtain the necessary data
and technical collaboration.
Impact Effectiveness and Efficiency of the NFPP Program on Health and Family
Planning Status
23.       The study will focus on the impact of the NFPP and will be organized
to compare the group of 20 AcceLerated Family Planning and Health (AFPH)
provinces with the remaining fifty-one. This requires a consistent set of
costing procedures across the two groups, and explicit attention to the
problem of joint costs. The output side is less problematic, inasmuch as the
regular service statistics system affords counts of acceptors by provinces,
facility type, method and personal characteristics of acceptors. This study
will be carried out by the Institute for Population and Social Research at
Mahidol University in cooperation with the MOPH.
24.       National Survey of Family Planning Practice, Fertility, and Mortality:
Thailand shares the unfortunate plight of many developing countries in that
reliable estimates of fertility and mortality cannot yet be derived from its
vital statistics. While the censuses provide an independent measure of over-
all growth, they come at infrequent intervals and are subject to important
sources of error. Undertakings such as the National Statistical Office's
continuous demographic survey also have not yet attained the desired quality
for demographic estimation.
25.       Thailand, however, has benefitted greatly from high quality sur-
veys based on household samples. This series of surveys, conducted by the
Institute of Population Studies, has continued for almost a decade. The
latest undertaking, the Thai participation in the World Fertility Survey,
has been the chief source of reliable demographic information.
26.       The next national survey falls within the project period.  The
Institute of Population Studies plans to conduct a national survey of fer-
tility, abortion, and family planning in March-June 1978, approximately a
ten-year interval from the dates of the urban and rural components of the
first study. The national sample can be segmented to provide separate esti-
mates for the aggregate of the 20 special provinces and the 51, with self-
weighting to permit merging of the two parts for national figures. The
presence of community variable information of some ten years ago permits
correlative retrospective work on the role of intrastructure development
in fertility change, etc., an exceptional opportunity of special interest.



AiNEX 6
Page 7
The survey will not attempt to cover health behavior and health facility
usage, as that would stretch the questionnaire excessively and impair the
quality of the fertility/family planning information. It will, however,
devote special attention to induced abortion. While abortion is known to
be practiced in Thailand, its incidence has never been accurately measured.
Results from the survey will provide a basis for national policy.
27.       In summary, this national survey offers a number of features that
are of advantage to the project and of special value both to Thailand and
internationally. These include: (a) the sample design comparing the 20
special provinces with the other 51; (b) the additional value gained by a
series of surveys, i.e., that this will provide not only its own estimates
of current fertility levels and contraceptive use figures but will extend
the time trends needed for the work of Government agencies particularly the
National Economic and Social Development Board; (c) it has community var-
iables; and (d) it will give special emphasis to abortion at a time when
liberalization of the law is under consideration.
28.       Field Trial of Ferility Control Incentives:  Both research results
and the actual response to the NFPP suggest that in Thailand the constraint
upon contraceptive usage has been supply rather than demand.  Nevertheless,
in looking to the future, leaders in the population field are exploring the
possibilities of further demand creation.
29.       A project is underway to design an action experiment with incen-
tives. The first stage was to collect background information on experiments
in incentives already conducted in other countries; in the second stage,
Thai family planning experts visited countries in the region to study the
use of incentives; and in the third stage the results of the first two stages
were reviewed in a formal seminar, which then formulated recommendations on
the kind of incentive that appears most useful in the Thai setting. Ford
Foundation funding is carrying activities through this seminar, which took
place in 1977.
30.       The seminar will be followed by a field trial.  It is proposed
that the project make a substantial contribution to the cost of the field
trial.  If necessary, this item could be made contingent upon a subsequent
detailed proposal, when the results of the national seminar are available.
The Institute of Population Studies at Chulalongkorn University will carry
out the study.
31.       Field Trial on Methods to Enlist Traditional Healers in Modern
Health Care: The MOPH and cooperating institutions are investigating the
potential usefulness of local "doctors" and traditional healers in the fam-
ily planning delivery system. The project would enable the School of Public
Health to conduct a small pilot project, first to determine the present func-
tions of the tambol doctor and traditional healer, and then to train them to
cooperate with the national health service in peripheral rural areas.



ANNEX 6
Page 8
32.       Village Experiment in Multi-Sectoral Population and Development
Activities: The School of Public Health at Mahidol University proposes
to conduct a small experiment in the establishment of village cooperatives
that would be based upon existing agricultural organizations, primary health
care and contraceptive services being provided through village health vol-
unteers or local Buddhist priests. If an experimental cooperative could
become self sufficient within a year, efforts to enlarge the experiment
would follow.
33.       Needed Advisory Assistance in Evaluation and Research:  A need
exists for short-term assistance to help design the studies and surveys to
be undertaken by the above-mentioned population institutes. Approximately
8 person-months of assistance are planned. The detailed phasing of the ad-
visory assistance inputs are shown in the implementation schedule (Appendix
5). The cost would be:
Travel:  $1,790 x 3                  $ 5,370
Per diem:  $55 x 30 x 12 =           $19,800
Fee:  $150 x 30 x 12      =          $54,000
Total                           $79,170
34.       Research Coordination:  The Evaluation and Research Sub-Committee
of the National Family Planning Coordinating Committee will oversee the im-
plementation of the studies conducted by the NFPP and by Chulalongkorn and
Mahidol Universities.
C. Strengthening Evaluation and Research Activities: Provincial Level
35.       To plan and evaluate the family planning and rural health activ-
ities in the 20 provinces, fully staffed planning and evaluation sections
will be established in the provincial health office. They will be staffed
by a Section Chief, an epidemiological worker and a statistician. To ensure
adequate supervision of the E&R studies in the field, to oversee the data
collection process and routine collection of service statistics, and to
facilitate provincial planning, each provincial health office will be pro-
vided with a two-wheel drive vehicle for use by the Section Chief and his
staff. In addition, typewriters, filing cabinets and calculators will be
provided to the provincial health office and district health office for
reporting and data compilation purposes. The E&R program for the 20 prov-
inces includes certain components already outlined: the service statistics
plan, the fertility/family planning survey, and the cost effectiveness proj-
ect.  In addition, there will be a series of surveys to detect trends in fer-
tility, health and in health facility usage, in both the private and public
sectors; a series of operationa:L investigations designed to strengthen proj-
ect implementation; an evaluation of the use and use-effectiveness of audio-
visual aids and equipment by health personnel in the 20 AFPH provinces; and
an external impact evaluation of the cost-effectiveness and efficiency of
the system being introduced through the project in the 20 AFPH provinces.



ANNEX 6
Page 9
36.       Surveys:  The four surveys to be conducted will cover variables
measuring fertility, health and health facility usage which, as mentioned
previously, cannot be incorporated into the Institute of Population Studies
national survey in 1978 without seriously compromising its primary goals.
The first of these surveys will establish a baseline against which overall
program impact, as well as that of selected components, can be measured.
This series will be repeated more often than the Institute's surveys, as
the intent is to measure progress from year to year instead of over a ten-
year period. The surveys will also provide information on utilization of
the facilities over time; improvements in the quality of medical and health
care with improved staffing and expanded in-service training, etc. The sur-
veys will be carried out by the Health Planning Division. Post graduate
students will conduct the surveys in 24 provinces, 20 of which are AFPH
provinces and the other four provinces are control areas, one in each region.
37.       Operational Studies:  Over the three years of the project, the
rapid expansion of the health system in the 20 AFPH provinces will call for
adaptability in administration. Difficulties may arise under such headings
as logistics, personnel, high cost/output ratios, and evaluation of health
staff performance. It is important that the problems that arise should be
promptly identified and dealt with, and the research program should make
provision for this. In the nature of the case their precise character can-
not be known before the fact, although the fact of their future appearance
is certain. This project will support operational studies over the three
years. One of these studies will focus on the implementation of the vil-
lage health volunteer/health communicator program in the first four of the
20 AFPH provinces in which this program is being implemented. This study
should examine, among others, the following elements of the program: train-
ing of provincial and district trainers, training of tambol-level staff,
preparation of the community for participation in the program, selection
and training of volunteers, performance and public acceptance of volunteers,
supervision by health staff, adequacy of logistical support, and, in con-
junction with the household surveys described above, the initial impact on
health and family planning knowledge and utilization of services. It is
suggested that this study be designed to take about four to six months and
that it should be carried out no later than about mid-1978. Technical as-
sistance is provided for in the project budget. This study must be closely
linked with the local evaluation of the program that each province is doing
in the initial trial tambols with the help of the Health Education Division.
The results should be provided to the Project Executive Committee through
the Project Director to ensure that necessary changes are made in implemen-
tation procedures as the program expands to other provinces.  Other possible
topics of operational research studies are: (a) the adequacy of four months
training for providing paramedicals with curative skills; (b) comparative
effectiveness of volunteers trained by the MOPH for primary health care and
distributors organized by the Community Based Family Planning Services; (c)
the adequacy of the referral system between rural health facilities, etc.; and
(d) the performance of: (a) the nurse practitioners in providing medical care;
(b) all categories of staff involved in family planning; (c) sanitarians in



ANNEX 6
Page 10
supervising primary health care and family planning activities while working
on environmental sanitation, etc. Emphasis will be placed on isolating the
effects of in-service training, staff supervision, availability of drugs
and medical supplies, and management of the quality of services provided.
These studies will largely be carried out by the Health Planning Division.
38.       Evaluation of the Use and Use-Effectiveness of Audiovisual Aids
and Equipment: To determine how frequently and effectively various cadres
of health workers have used the newly provided audiovisual equipment and
health education materials, about 2,100 interviews with health staff will
be conducted in five of the 20 AFPH provinces during 1978.  A follow-up
survey in 1980 will seek to determine whether the corrective measures imple-
mented on the basis of the results of the first survey were effective in
correcting the problems identified. This evaluation should assist the Royal
Thai Government, the Bank and other donors in subsequently supplying audio-
visual materials and equipment. These surveys will be carried out by the
Health Education Division with technical support from its E&R Section. The
interviewers will be regularly-salaried Division Staff.
39.       External Evaluation of the Impact of the AFPH Program:  This re-
search would be carried out by an external research group in Thailand. It
would provide program administrators with an independent external evaluation
of project impact or effectiveness on health status and the efficiency of the
AFPH program in reaching its objectives and targets. This research should
enable the MOPH to decide what elements of the project should be replicated
nationwide and in what fashion.
40.       Technical Assistance for E&R Activities in the 20 AFPH Provinces:
The project provides for one year of short-term advisory assistance in E&R.
Eight person-months of these would be used to provide a consultant in research
methodology to assist in the development of the design for the baseline survey
and for the further specification of the operational research studies. This
technical assistance would be required during the first eight months of the
project. Another two person-months would be required in 1979 to design and
help implement the comprehensive evaluation of the village health volunteer/
health communicator program that represents one of the key operational re-
search studies. This consultant would concentrate on the evaluation of the
training of provincial, district, and tambol-level staff, and administrative
and logistical support at these levels. The consultant would help set up
arrangements with the planning and evaluation sections of the Provincial
Fealth Offices in the four AFPH provinces that are initiating the village
volunteer program early to carry out a brief, but detailed assessment of the
implementation of the program at the village level. Subsequent follow-up
would be provided through two additional person-months of advisory assistance
in 1980 to follow-up on the studies underway and to assist in the design of
the impact studies, etc.



ANNEX 6
Page 11
D. Allocation for Innovative E&R Activities
41.       The experience of recent years suggests that, over the three-year
life of the project, important new delivery systems, birth control methods,
and avenues of demand creation are likely to emerge. There is no doubt that
the Thai NFPP will maintain its pioneering record. It is therefore probable
that studies will present demand on the contingency for innovative activities
during the project, especially the second and third years, since the most
pressing immediate E&R needs are being met by the E&R activities summarized
in this annex.
E. Family Planning Service Statistics System
42.       A card is completed on each new acceptor at local service outlets
showing method accepted and personal characteristics, and these cards flow
to Bangkok, where they are computerized. Second class hlealth centers and
midwifery centers prepare a daily report, including numbers of new and old
acceptors, number of home visits by type, and number of oral contraceptive
cycles given to new and return clients. These daily reports flow to the
district health office, which compiles them into a monthly report sent to
Bangkok. Acceptor characteristics and supplies distributed provide the
information by which performance by province and type of service outlet is
judged, and thus enable trends to be established reflecting the impact of
key policy decisions. All information is aggregated by geographic unit, so
that at the provincial level the performance of each of its amphoes can be
assessed, and each amphoe can know the record of its tambols. This system,
in broad outline, is similar to that of many Asian programs, and shares their
strengths and defects.
43.       The defects lie more in the utilization of the system than in its
character. Given the computerization of the information, considerably greater
exploitation of the data is possible. For example, little feedback is given
to subordinate units on their own performance, and none is given on compara-
tive performance elsewhere. Thus they lack a basis for judging their work
relative to that of others, or for knowing, for example, whether a bad month
was shared by others and was therefore likely due to general conditions out-
side their control. Moreover, the central processing of the data is somewhat
slower than it need be, so that the effects of such decisions as removing all
charges for oral contraceptives or the effect upon oral contraceptive perfor-
mance of introducing injectables are not monitored as closely as they should
be.
F. Strengthening the Family Planning Service Statistics System
44.       Needed Improvements:  The initial step needed to improve the ser-
vice statistics system is one which will reduce staff duties to routine so



ANINEX 6
Page 12
that informationL flows more quickly through the series of steps from receipt
and checking of records to keypunching, verification, and computer processing.
At present this flow is partially ad hoc, especially in its later stages,
where conflicting priorities sometimes cause interruptions.
45.       The second and more difficult step is to modify the computer pro-
gramas to produce desired new kinds of output. Certain output, in multip'le
copies, is needed for feedback to the provinces and possibly, amphoes. At
present, each province receives only its own results; a different output is
needed to show in a simple and effective manner performance in other prov-
inces, at least in the same region. Different output is needed to aggre-
gate results by type of facility within individual provinces. Yet a differ-
ent output is needed for convenient indices of performance at the national
level, and so on. The complexity of this work is compounded because appar-
ently small changes in coding and other processing routines carry implica-
tions when the volume is substantial as it is in the Thai program.
46.       A third step is to improve reporting from the field; about 70% of
the units report within 15 days of the end of the month, but the remaining
30% are often repeatedly delinquent and slow up processing. The delinquents
should receive admonitory letters, followed up if necessary by visits from
supervisors. A realistic goal is a two-thirds reduction of delinquent re-
porting units over two years.
47.       The long-term approach to expediting the monthly report is to make
each service unit a reporting unit which would send a copy of its monthly
service statistics report to the central level as well as to the district
health office. Besides expediting central feedback, this system would en-
courage program evaluation at the lowest service levels.
48.       To make the monthly report more useful for supervision and pro-
gram management, two changes are planned. The first is to add an acceptor
recruitment achievement rate based on an estimated eligible population; the
second is to translate the present caseload statistics into a true estimate
of the active number of cases per reporting area. This latter step is to
be accomplished by a semi-annual, active-caseload inventory of all service
units. Both these modifications will assist the comparative evaluation of
reporting unit performance. Unit and provincial rankings by achievement and
other factors will also help to guide the supervision of the program's service
units. Work along these lines will begin by the start of the project period.
49.       Project Inputs:  Project inputs in support of improvements in the
FP service statistical system con!sist of the following:
a.   Improvement of slow reporting units
(i) computer re-programming,
(ii) orientation of field staff,



ANNEX 6
Page 13
(iii) initial supervision and site visits to implement
system;
b.   Implementation of revised national system
(i) computer programming,
(ii) explanatory forms and instructions,
(iii)  mailing and processing of inventory,
(iv) site visits to facilitate implementation and
trouble shooting.
50.       An implementation schedule for the E&R project inputs is provided
in Appendix 5.

THAILAND
MANAGEMENT AND PLANNING IN SUPPORT OF FAMILY
PLANNING AND PRIMARY HEALTH CARE
A. National Level
National Family Planning Organization and Administration
1.        The ,National Family Planning Coordinating Committee oversees the
implementation of the National Family Planning Program (NFPP) in Thailand.
Chaired by the Minister of Public Health, it is primarily responsible for
coordinating the operational policies and programs of agencies providing
family planning services. The Committee includes representatives from other
ministries and agencies involved in family planning in Thailand (Appendix 1).
The Under-Secretary of State for Health, the chief civil servant in the Min-
istry of Public Health (MOPH), is the Director of the NFPP. The Director-
General of the Department of Health is the Deputy Program Director and the
head of the Family Health Division is the Program Officer (Appendix 2).
2.        The NFPP is administered at the headquarters level by the Family
Planning Unit in the Family Health Division. The Family Planning Unit is
in turn divided into five sections: Administrative, Evaluation and Research
(E&R), Public Relations and Information, Training and Supervision, and Spe-
cial Projects Sections. The Administrative Section is in charge of logis-
tic support, correspondence, storage and distribution of contraceptives,
and financial management. The E&R Section undertakes internal evaluation
and operational research to evaluate the impact and effectiveness of the
NFPP. The Public Information Section is responsible for the production and
dissemination of family planning informational and educational materials and
for the family planning motivational activities of the MOPH. The Training
and Supervision Section identifies training needs, coordinates training
arrangements and supervises the paramedical personnel in the field who are
responsible for the delivery of family planning services. Annexes 2, 4, 5
and 6 describe in greater detail the roles and functions of these sections.
Altogether there are some 122 employees of the NFPP; 29 are employed by the
MOPH in established posts and 93 in non-established posts. Around one-half
of the non-established (temporary) posts have been funded by subventions from
external donors (Appendix 3).
3.        To enable the NFPP to continue its present level of operations and
expand its activities, there is a need for the Royal Thai Government (RTG)
to employ many of the subventioned persons as Government civil servants and
to somewhat expand the nucleus. Altogether some 45 positions are required
to ensure that the Family Health Division has the operational and technical
support to implement the expanded family planning program (Annex 9). Salary
support for these posts are included in the project. Previously, as shown
in Appendix 2, family planning and maternal and child health activities were
discrete programs. However, the Family Health Division's recognition of the



ANNEX 7
Page 2
need to optimize scarce human and financial resources have served as an
impetus for adopting an integrated approach. The proposed organizational
structure (currently under consideration by the RTG) is shown in Appendix 4.
MOPH Organization and Administration
4.        The MOPH is responsible for the organization and administration of
public health services in Thailand. The Ministry is divided into five depart-
ments responsible for technical supervision of public health activities and
the Office of the Under-Secretary of State for Public Health responsible for
administration, management and planning and evaluation (Appendix 5). The
departments report to the Under-Secretary of State for Public Health and in-
clude: The Department of Medical Care which is in charge of medical care
in hospitals or institutions; the Department of Health which oversees eight
technical divisions: Family Health, Nutrition, Rural Waterwork, Environmental
Health, etc.; the Department of Communicable Disease Control which supervises
nine technical divisions dealing with malaria, leprosy, T.B., V.D., filariasis
control, etc.; the Department of Medical Sciences which is responsible for
diagnostic laboratories and the promotion of medical research; and the Office
of Food and Drug Committee which is responsible for the control and regulation
of drugs and drug abuse.
5.        The Office of the Under-Secretary of State for Public Health coor-
dinates the work of the various departments and the provincial health admin-
istration, and supervises 18 divisions in i.s office together with the 71
provincial chief medical officers. The Office of the Under-Secretary is
managed by three Deputy Under-Secretaries of State: Planning and Evalua-
tion; Administration; and Support Services (Superintendent). Six of the
eight divisions responsible for project implementation report to the Deputy
Under-Secretaries: Rural Health, Construction and Design, Nursing Colleges,
Health Education, Health Training and Health Planning. The Family Health
and Nutrition Divisions report to the Director of the Department of Health.
Project Organization and Administration
6.        The project will be administered and coordinated by the MIOPH
through the Office of the Under-Secretary of State for Public Health. The
Deputy Under-Secretary of State for Planning and Evaluation will be desig-
nated as Project Director, responsible for overall coordination and imple-
mentation. The Deputy Director-General of the Department of Health will
serve as the Deputy Project Director and the heads of the Family Health and
Rural Health Divisions will be designated Assistant Project Directors. The
Project Director's office will have attached to it a Project Administration
and Financing Unit, which will provide the Project Director with staff sup-
port in the following areas: reporting project progress, processing reim-
bursement applications, approving contract documents and technical specifi-
cations for procurement under the project, preparing and submitting to the
Association for its prior approval all designs for the physical facilities
to be constructed under the project, and monitoring of project planning and



ANNEX 7
Page 3
implementation activities. The Project Administration and Financing Unit
will include: one financial administrator, one coordinator of field activi-
ties, two accountants, two secretary/typists and one health planner/economist.
The health planner/economist will be a 36 person-month advisor who will
assist the Project Director and his staff in carrying out the project. Two
two-wheel drive vehicles, to be procured in the first year of the project,
will be provided to the Project Director and the staff of the Project Admin-
istration and Financing Unit to enable them to oversee project implementation.
7.        The technical aspects of project implementation will be handled by
the following Divisions of the MOPH: Family Health (responsible for family
planning), Rural Health, Construction and Design, Nursing Colleges, Health
Education, Health Planning, Health Training, and Nutrition. To ensure effec-
tive coordination and that appropriate project actions are taken, a Project
Coordinating Committee consisting of the heads of these eight Divisions and
chaired by the Project Director or his Deputy will be established (Appendix 6).
Civil Works Administration and Supervision
8.        Since the project provides for an expansion of MOPH activities,
particularly in the 20 accelerated family planning and health (AFPH) prov-
inces, incremental salary support is included for those divisions in the
MOPH which will take on special responsibilities. In addition to the person-
nel requirements of the Family Health Division, Rural Health Division, Health
Education Division and Health Planning Division, additional personnel will
also be required by the Design and Construction Division. Within that Divi-
sion, a special section will be established to implement the national-level
and provincial-level construction program. This unit will be staffed by a
section head, who is a senior architect and who will report to the Director of
the Design and Construction Division, and by four other engineers/architects
and by 20 technicians. Two two-wheel drive vehicles will be provided to the
section head and his staff to enable them to supervise site selections and the
construction work. To avoid overiextending the capacity of the special section,
the peak load of required architectural and engineering services will be
covered by technical staff recruited from outside the MOPH. Annex 9 provides
details on the additional posts resquired to implement the project.
9.        Designs for project facilities will be based on standard designs
that have been developed by the Design and Construction Division. The staff
of the special project section wiLl be responsible for the remaining design
work, for supervising site selection, and for supervising construction work
including preparation of bidding documents. Supervising the scattered and
small-scale construction work in the provinces will be particularly demanding
on technical staff input. It is particularly so for this type of work where
technical staff recruited from the outside will be required to supplement the
special section staff.



ANNEX 7
Page 4
B. Provincial Level (AFPH Provinces)
10.       At the provincial level, the provincial chief medical officer is
the senior health official responsible for coordinating and supervising cura-
tive and preventive health services, including family planning. At the dis-
trict level, the district health officer, under the authority of the district
officer, has similar district level responsibilities. Family planning ser-
vices are administered as an integral part of Thailand's rural health ser-
vices.  Annex 2 describes the existing and proposed MOPH strategy for deliv-
ering family planning and basic health services.
11.       Family planning and health services in the 20 AFPH provinces will
be administered as part of the Ministry's strengthened provincial health care
system. 1/ The project will utilize and strengthen the existing system in
accordance with MOPH plans to strengthen the provincial administration to
include planning, implementation, budget allocation, and personnel control.
This decentralization will enable provincial administrators to exercise
greater flexibility in solving local health problems. At the same time the
proposed project will fully utilize existing committees, including Provin-
cial Health Advisory Councils, District Health Advisory Committees, Tambol
Councils, and Muban Committees to coordinate health activities and to ensure
the involvement of community leaders in setting health policy. Appendix 7
provides an organizational chart of provincial health care administration.
The Provincial Health Advisory Committee is an advisory body headed by the
provincial governor and including senior representatives of the MOPH and
other concerned Government agencies. It promotes coordination with other
agencies concerned and advises on implementation of health projects. The
District Advisory Council is a body established at the district level with
similar functions. The Tambol Councils and Muban Committees also help coor-
dinate health activities and ensure the involvement of community leaders in
setting health policy. The main committee responsible for overseeing the
implementation of the project in the 20 AFPH provinces is the Health Plan-
ning and Evaluation Working Committee. Its main functions are provincial
health planning, monitoring and evaluation, coordinating with other health
units and other agencies concerned, and integrating health activities with
1/   The 1974 reorganization of the MOPH strengthened central coordination
and decentralized planning and management. According to the newly or-
ganized structure, all of the provincial chief medical officers were
given full control over the delivery of provincial health services and,
henceforth, were to report directly to the Office of the Under-Secretary
of State. The five Departments of the central health administration
provide technical support to the Office of the Provincial Medical Offi-
cer. The Health Planning Division assists the provincial chief medical
officer in developing provincial health plans.



ANNEX 7
Page 5
other relevant sectors. The provincial chief medical officer chairs the
committee whose membership includes all key administrators at the provin-
cial and district levels. The Chief of the Planning and Evaluation Section
in the provincial health office is secretary to the Committee. This section
provides staff support in planning, monitoring, and evaluation to the provin-
cial chief medical officer. The project includes salary support for this
unit.
12.       An implementation schedule for the management and planning project
inputs is provided as Appendix 8.



ANNEX 7
Appendix 1
THAILAND
COMPOSITION OF NATIONAL FAMILY PLANNING COORDINATING COMMITTEEI'
1.   Minister of the Ministry of Public Health (Chairman).
2.   Assistant Minister of the Ministry of Public Health (Deputy Chairman).
3.   Under-Secretary of the State of the Ministry of Public Health.
4.   Under-Secretary of State of the Ministry of Interior.
5.   Under-Secretary of the State of the Ministry of Agriculture and Cooperative.
6.   Secretary of the National Economic and Social Development Board.
7.   Deputy Under-Secretary of State of the Ministry of Public Health.
8.   Director of the Budget Bureau.
9.   Director of the National Family Planning Program.
10.   Director General of the Health Department.
11.   Director General of the Medical Services Department.
12.   Director General of the Department of Technical and Economic Cooperation.
13.   Director General of the Department of Public Relations.
14.   Director of the Population and Social Resarch Institution, Mahidol University.
15.   Director of the Institute for Population Studies, Chulalongkorn University.
16.   Director of Population Research Division, National Statistical Office.
17.   Head of Adult Education, Ministry of Education.
18.   President of the Planned Parenthood Association of Thailand.
19.   Director of the Family Health Division, Ministry of Public Health.
J/ The National Family Planning Committee was established in '974. It is
responsible for overseeing the work of the National Family P'anning Pro-
gram as planned and administered by the Ministry of Public Health. It
also helps coordinate the population activities of external donors.
Source: Family Health Division, Ministry of Public Health.
