DEMOGRAPHIC BACKGROUND AND PROJECTED IMPACT OF NFPP
A. The Current Situation
1.        The first population census of Thailand, in 1911, reported a total
population of about eight million. Nothing can be said with certainty about
the size of Thailand's population at earlier dates, but it seems clear that
by historical standards it had grown substantially during the preceding half-
century. It is reasonably estimated that the population in 1850 was between
five and six million. Of the increase of about 50% during the next 60-year
period (well under 1% a year), about two-fifths consisted of Chinese immigra-
tion, which continued at a high rate until the 1930s. At the time of the
1937 census, the population had grown to more than 14 million; the popula-
tion growth rate had evidently doubled (to 2.2%) compared with the 1850-1911
period.  During the Second World War, the growth rate decelerated, averag-
ing slightly less than 2% per year from 1937 to 1947. Thereafter, rapidly
falling death rates led to renewed acceleration of population growth, now
based almost exclusively on natural increase, that is, an excess of births
over deaths. Official census data are summarized in Table 1. Evaluation
studies of the 1970 census have led to an upward revision of the 1970 figure
to about 36 million. The revised annual growth rate for 1960 to 1970 is
about 3%. In 1977 Thailand's population was estimated at around 44 million
and the annual growth rate was estimated at about 2.5%.
Table 1:  CENSUS COUNTS OF POPULATION AND INTER-CENSAL INCREASES, 1911-1970
Census              Total            Inter-Censal       Annual Percentage
Year             Population           Increase              Increase
1911              8,266,408           -                     -
1919              9,207,355             940,447             1.4
1929             11,506,207           2,298,952             2.2
1937             14,464,105           2,957,898             3.0
1947             17,442,689           2,978,584             1.9
1960             26,257,916          11,467,727             3.2
1970             34,397,374          11,860,542             2.7
Fertility
2.        With the exception of the war years of the 1940s, fertility has
remained at traditionally high levels during most of the twentieth century.
In view of the high level of mortality, this was in large measure a condi-
tion of survival. Up to the early years of the century, it is clear that
fertility must have been only slightly higher than mortality. But mortality
appears to have started downwards about the time of World War I, leading to



ANNEX 12
Page 2
the sharp increase in the rate of natural increase recorded for the 1920s
and 1930s, which was resumed after the interruption of the Second World
War. Fertility remained high (by international standards) as is indicated
by a gross reproduction rate of 3.2 estimated for 1950-55 and of 3.1 for
1964-65.
3.        Other evidence of recent high fertility can be found both in the
1960 estimate that the average number of children ever-born to ever-married
women past the childbearing age was about 6.5 and to the fact that some esti-
mates for 1970 put the crude birth rate at between 42 and 45 per thousand
population.
4.        However, in recent years there is growing, if still somewhat impre-
cise, evidence to the effect that a significant overall fertility decline
has commenced. This conclusion has been reached in part from the results of
the 1975 World Fertility Survey which showed a downward trend in the total
fertility rate from 6.6 in 1960-64 to 4.8 in 1970-74, representing a decline
of 27% (Appendix 1). Some unpublished work of the National Economic and
Social Development Board on the basis of a comparative analysis of data from
registration statistics, censuses, sample surveys and other studies also sug-
gest that a fertility decline has been under way for some 10-15 years and has
possibly been accelerating during the last five years. This conclusion can
draw support from the fact that urbanization is proceeding rapidly and that
marked urban-rural differentials exist. The fertility of the metropolitan
area of Bangkok-Thonburi is on the average 30% below the national average,
while that of provincial towns seems to be about 20% below the level of the
country as a whole. It also appears that regional differentials in fertility
are widening. Fertility in the Central Region, including Bangkok, and in the
North appears to be falling significantly.
5.        Thailand in both rural and urban sectors is notable for a relatively
high age at marriage. The census shows that between 1947 and 1960 the propor-
tion of women married by ages 20-24 declined by almost one third. In 1970
half of all females were married by about age 21, males by age 24. lWhile
late marriages tend to depress overall fertility, the traditional Thai pattern
of continuing child bearing into the late 40s has compensated to maintain high
crude birth rates.
6.        The results of the mosit recent demographic work of the National
Economic and Social Development ]3oard and the National Statistical Office
indicate that the crude birth ralte has fallen to around 34.5 per thousand
by 1977 and that the death rate has fallen to around nine per thousand. It
is therefore estimated that the declared population growth rate target of
2.5% in 1977 has been achieved. These declines parallel evidence of sharply
rising levels of contraceptive usage, including sterilization, as seen in
survey measurements of proportions using acceptance figures for the National
Family Planning Program as estimates of private sector distribution.



ANNEX 12
Page 3
Mortality
7.        Until recently mortality in Thailand was high.  An estimate by
Bourgeois-Pichat (1960) presents a crude death rate of 50 per thousand for
the early part of the twentieth century. This would suggest that the expec-
tation of life at birth was only a little over 20 years. Life expectancy
in 1937 has been estimated at 35 years, implying a death rate of about 25
per thousand. A more rapid decline in mortality began after the Second World
War, leading to an increase in life expectancy to 50 years in 1947 and to 56
years for males and 64 years for females in 1964-65. The crude death rate
had by the mid-1950s fallen to below 20 per thousand and by the mid-1960s to
about 11 per thousand. The crude death rate can still fall modestly below
its present level of nine per thousand, but for future growth the dynamic
factor will be fertility.
8.        From various sources, infant and maternal mortality rates are esti-
mated to have decreased by two-thirds over the three decades between 1940
and 1970, but they remain at serious levels, with infant mortality probably
at about 70 per thousand live births and maternal mortality about four per
thousand live births as of the early 1970s.
Population Distribution and Internal Migration
9.        By Asian standards, Thailand is still a relatively sparsely popu-
lated country, although it will not long remain so if present growth rates
continue. In 1970 average density was about 70 persons per square kilometer.
Because of the high rate of population growth, this figure may now
be expected to have risen to approximately 85 per square kilometer. Density
per unit of cultivable land is of course much higher and varies significantly
from region to region. Average density is highest in the Central Region,
which includes the most fertile agricultural land in the country, as well as
the Bangkok metropolitan area. The population of the Central Region, com-
prises about 30% of the total population, one-third of which is accounted for
by the Bangkok metropolitan area alone. Next in terms of density comes the
North East Region, which has the poorest soil. Nevertheless, the North East
remains predominantly agricultural and supports about 35% of the total popula-
tion. The North and the South have more favorable agricultural conditions,
but the North is also developing considerable pressure on cultivable land.
These two regions hold about 22 and 13% respectively, of the total population.
10.       Urban growth is proceeding at a high rate.  It has been estimated
at 6.5% per year, or more than twice the rate of natural increase during the
1960s. The proportion of population residing in urban areas has increased
from 10% in 1947 to nearly 17% in 1976. Since the designation of an area
as urban is in part a matter of administrative considerations (it alters
the services that must be provided, for example), these figures most likely
understate the degree of urbanization that has actually taken place. The
extent of underenumeration is not known.



ANNEX 12
Page 4
11.       The outstanding feature of the pattern of urbanization in Thailand
is the dominance of the Bangkok metropolitan area. Thailand's capital has
approximately four million people, about one-tenth of the country's population,
and constitutes the most extreme example of a "primate city" anywhere in the
world, exceeding the size of the second largest city, Chiang Mai in the North,
by a multiple of more than 40. This figure is, however, likely to be some-
what exaggerated by the just-mentioned problem of definition of urban areas.
The degree of dominance of Bangkok, in terms of relative numbers, has in-
creased significantly in recent decades.
12.       As ,elsewhere, it appears that the main motivation for migration
is economic, as urban areas offer the prospect of better incomes and better
social services. Additionally, migration occurs in connection with marriage
and other family-related changes. Apart from rural-urban migration, movements
from less to more promising rural areas are relatively common, in particular
when settlement schemes or other new or improved facilities offer good pros-
pects for cash income.
Population Structure and Composition
13.       As might be inferred from its high level of fertility, the rela-
tively low and declining level of mortality, and insignificant external migra-
tion, Thailand has a young population. In 1970 slightly more than 45% of the
population was below 15 years of age and only about 3% was 65 years of age or
older (Appendix 2). If the working-age population is defined as those between
ages 15 and 64, it follows that the dependency burden was 93 dependents per
100 persons of working age. This represents an increase from 85 per 100 in
1960, when the population below ][5 years of age was somewhat lower. Due to
the concentration of rural-urban migration in the working ages, the dependency
burden is significantly lower in urban than in rural areas, about 72 versus 93
per hundred for the country as a whole.
B.  Demographic Impact of the National Family Planning Program
14.       Overpopulation means essentially too much pressure on a country's
available resources to permit generating those improvements in social and
economic standards which are called "development." The forms which demo-
graphic pressure takes are many--e.g., the demand for more school places,
increasing competition for the available employment opportunities, downward
pressure on wage levels, less and less arable land area per farm family and
per consumer, more pressure on available food supplies, on energy resources,
on forestry, on dumping-spaces for human and industrial wastes, etc. While
population size is only one source of this growing pressure on resources (the
other major source being rising living standards themselves), it is the base
to which all other aspects of the problem must ultimately be referred. Thus
population size, and its rate of growth, are basic determinants of human
welfare.



ANNEX 12
Page 5
15.       The table on p. 3 of this report's main text shows the estimated
size of Thailand's population over a 30-year period, 1970-2000, under three
different assumptions concerning fertility trends. Two main conclusions are
evident:  even if Thailand should succeed in achieving a net reproduction rate
of 1.0 by 1990 the built-in momentum of the country's young age-structure would
cause population to continue increasing to around 66 million in the year 2000
and through the middle of the next century, when it would be over 90 million.
The second conclusion, obvious but dramatic, is that the ultimate total popu-
lation would be much larger than 90 million people if a net reproduction rate
of 1.0 is reached only some years after 1990. Thus these overall numbers
strongly support the Government's attempt to slow population growth. This
conclusion gains in force when one examines some aspects of the demographic
structure under the three different growth-rates that underlie the projections.
The following provide estimated differences in the dependency burden, in the
size of the school-age population, and the size of the working age population
under the three assumptions used about future fertility trends.
Table 2: AGE DISTRIBUTION, 1970 AND 2000, UNDER VARYING FERTILITY ASSUMPTIONIS

16.       Table 2 shows the effects of varying fertility assumptions on the
age distribution of the population in the year 2000.  It can be seen that
the proportion of the population not of working age declines sharply with
the level of fertility, resulting in dependency ratios which vary between
1.03 and .47. The absolute number of persons under the age of 15 shows an
even more striking disparity. If the target fall in fertility is achieved,
this group will be little more than one-third the number occurring with
constant fertility or one half the figure for the medium projection. The
potential for resource savings in education and other social sectors oriented
toward the young is easily apparent. With the target fall in fertility, the
population under 15 increases to about 20 million in 1980 but then falls
slowly again before becoming almost stationary at approximately its current
level.

17.       Table 3 gives figures for two school-age groups for the constant
variant, medium variant, and the Thai target projections.  The table clearly
illustrates the effects of lowering fertility on these two age groups.  With
the higher fertility, the school-age population will increase rapidly over
the next 30 years, doubling even under the conditions of the medium variant
projection. If the target projection goals are achieved, however, future
increases in school-age population will be significantly more modest and
some decline might actually take place after the mid-1980s for the 6-12
age group and in the older age group around the mid-1990s. Under the more
favorable conditions of the Thai target projection, it should be possible
to devote an increasing proportion of educational investment to expanding
enrollment ratios and improving educational quality. The wide variations
between the target projection and the other projections point to the diffi-
culties which will rise if fertility is not greatly reduced.
18.       Forecasts of the labor force are especially difficult to estimate
given the number of volatile economic and social factors involved. Using
the assumption that recent (1970) labor-force participation rates of 52% for
males and 46% for females remain unchanged, the projections indicate a nearly
three-fold increase in the labor force by the year 2000 (Table 4), an increase
which will undoubtedly be difficult to absorb into the overall economic situa-
tion.
Table 4: PROJECTIONS OF THE LABOR FORCE, 1970-2000
(OOOs)

ANNEX 12
Page 7
Table 4 also indicates that the level of decline in fertility, as indicated
by the different projections, will have only a slight effect on the size of
the labor force up to 1990. This is because the working-age population from
which the labor-force is drawn (those 15 to 64 years of age) will not be
affected for the next 15 years regardless of the change in fertility, and
then only gradually will these changes have an effect. By the year 2000,
the Thai target projection results in a labor force approximately 16% below
the constant fertility projection and 14% below the medium variant projection.
